THE RED PAGE
NEWLY PUBLISHED
A. W. a Beckett, author of The (i Becketts of
Punch (Lond., Constable ; 12s. 6tf. net), descends from a
talented family well-known in the last generation. His
father Gilbert was a London police magistrate—and
comic author. His uncle William was first Chief Justice
of Victoria—and wrote poetry. His uncle Thomas was
a Victorian Legislative Councillor—and lectured on
“Painting and Painters.” One of his cousins “was
said to be the best amateur herald at the antipodes.”
Another cousin is the Victorian Judge who was shocked
by “the extent to which women dare swear falsely
against positive evidence,” and declared that “ they are
utterly destitute of shame, remorse, or any feeling of
regret for wrong they have done.” Thus the whole
family has shown peculiar interest in the fine arts.
Author A. W. supplies an effective footnote to “ Angus
McNeill’s ” jibe that “ the English are a nation of em-
ployed persons. .. You will meet hoary and bald-headed
Englishmen who will tell you with a quaver that they have
been in the employment of one and the same house, man
and boy, for over half-a-century, sir ! Somehow the
Englishman tells you this with a look of pride, and rather
expects you to regard him as a sort of marvel.” And
“my father” (says A.W.) “was on Punch for fifteen
years, I for eight-and-twenty, so between us we were on
the staff for forty-three years. That may go some
way to accounting for Punch.
A.W.’s retirement from the staff was apparently
occasioned by increasing age (undenirble) and infirmity
(denied). He has founded since and conducts now John
Bull, a somewhat tepid example of “ wholesome English
fun.” His volume of reminiscences contains 310 pages
with a remarkable index, and was “ knocked off” (p. 275)
“within the space of little more than a fortnight.” It
looks like it. One wonders how ii Beckett, having seen
so many interesting people, could write so little of inte-
rest or importance. His family chronicle is all the world
for him ; but for us, as he has set it down, it is a tiresome
tale of trivialities, and out grows as weary of Punch with
devotion as “ Mark Twain ” grew of “ Punch with care.”
The case is pathetic indeed. It is the case of an amiable
honourable young elderly gentleman. His heart’s desire
is to occupy an inherited arm-chair till he dies (in time
for publication) with a mild joke on his loyal lips. And
his chair has disappeared suddenly.
By the light of The d Becketts of “ Punch ” the case is
tragic indeed. Id is the case of Father William,
jt
You remember +Gdm, our national prophet?
—magnetic, perfumed + Gn.vi ? He recalls himself to
memory in The Origin and Nature of the Universe (Ade-
laide ; Co-operative Printing Co.)—fifteen pages, with a
lovely and soothing portrait of the author. And the origin
and nature of the universe cease to be obscure, j Gum
is no mystic madman with a swag of sinister symbols.
He is a prophet up to the date of State schools and past
the data of Lord Kelvin. His gospel is as plain as pro-
found, as lucid as logical. One is sure that when he per-
mits himself a false concord it are merely a concession to
the natural weakness of the churches.
Leave thou thy sister when she prays
Her early grammar, her pious bulls... , •
The quotation reminds us that t Gum writes much better
than G.-G. Tennyson his speeches. And Pope Pius him-
self breathes no finer air of infallibility. It is useless for
the origin and nature of the universe’ to dodge. Vainly
they hide behind a word or a world, j Gum unrolls them
like a panorama, and shows you the pitiful forked radish
of all the religions shivering in the cold blast of hi#
assurance.
How, then, about Design? Has the universe a
Designer? Yes; the universe which we see has a
Designer, but not the whole of creation. For ths
Designer Himself is part of creation ; He Himself is the
product of Evolution.
The Evolution of the created universe may be
divided, into two periods: that which preceded the
existence of God, and that which has been contem-
porary with His existence . . .
For all practical purposes God is Omnipotent.
Omniscient, and Omnipresent..

Two Awheel, by Arthur Jose (Lond,, Dent) is a
singularly barren book. In a series of articles, partly
Reprinted from The S.M. Herald, Jose recites cycling
experiences on N.S.W. roads. His impressions are
crisply Written, but show hardly a trace of feeling or
imagination. The trifles that interest him glitter like
quartz pebbles, and are as worthless. Only once or
twice, as in this passage about Australia, i 3 the book in
the least stimulating—
Back there! Ah, dear country of ours, where else
could we wish to be? Is it bricked-up, hedged-in
England that should tempt us away, or the damp
Cornish moors, or the neat formal mechanisms of
France? Where else, so long as your streams run
brown among their boulders and your tracks wind along
the valley-sides through clean bush scents and clear
bushland air ? Let us go back while we may, while your
live alert youthfulness calls to usstillyoung; while there
is heart in us to be inspired, to answer all your chal-
lenges, to take joy in all your moods. Here we grow
dull with the clouds and the crowding townspeople,
we grow soft with the lush green fields and the rain ;
rarely- high on the brow of a Surrey moorland, or
watching the far-off Thame from some hanging wood on
Chiltern edge—we can recall for a moment those air-
spaces lit with splendid sun whereof you were so prodigal.
Will you take us back, dear land, land of our dreams
and our memories—and of our hopes?
The illustrations by “G. W. Lambert, of N.5.W.,” are
much more interesting than the book. Lambert has
failed to send to N.S.W. Gallery yearly pictures, under
the terms of his travelling scholarship : he has appar-
ently settled in London. His work with pen and brush
for Two Awheel is fine—charming pictorially, and for the
most part admirable in fidelity to Australia. His painting
has helped him to gain delicacy and colour with the pen,
and his drawing has lost stiffness. Perhaps a hint of
value will be preserved in the reproduced example.
Jt
In The Five Nations the Kipling geyser falls to
an unremarkable warm spring. The volume includes fifty-
four pieces of verse composed in the last half-dozen years
or so; and not more than three or four of them are
Y?. man who wrote Barrack-Room Ballads.
All the life has gone out of Kipling as artist, and there
remains only the preacher who fits laborious morals to
aull tunes. For the greater part, that is : for the lesser
there are imitations of Swinburne and Tennyson, toler-
able chap-book verse, tolerable journal-verse, and oeca-
Sionally a line or stanza that rings and surges. But little
ot it counts ; none of it matters ; and even the injudicious
ldolators must see that the god has become a compara-
tively stale and weary mechanic.
Look at the “ Dedication ” in the forefront— what harsh
mouthings ! what uncouth posturings 1
Except Distress, which, so will prophets cry,
O ercatne them, houseless, from the unhinting sky ...
And in the imperial task (as worthy) lay
Up our lives’ all to piece one giant day.
There is a meaning ; but look at the crude form I And
unless he make his form valid, Kipling had better preach
in prose.
, an d the Hills ” is Swinburne, carefully
phrased but dissonant, and lifeless, lacking the poetic
impulse, the poetic pang, the breath that makes verse
memorable. More worthy is “The Destroyers," whioh
at least gives a vigorous picture rhythmically. In verse
lute The Second Voyage ” the craftsman that Kipling
tries to be is sufficiently justified.
The sea has shorn our galleries away,
The salt has soiled our gilding past remede :
Our paint is flaked and blistered by the spray.
Our sides are half a fathom furred in weed.
. ,(Foul weather !)
Ana the doves of Venus fled and the petrels came instead,
But Love he was our master at our need /
It is mere phrasing ; but the phrases are neatly put
together. One can compare a piece like “ The Explorer ”
with local rhymes like Ogilvie’s “ The Men Who Blazed
the Track.” Kipling is the cleaner workman, bub he
leaves you cold. Ogilvie writes as if he felt his subject,
and he transfers his feeling to you. Kipling shows
feeling, too, in “Sussex,” but “Sussex” is one of the
scarce items in the fifty-four. “ The Settler ”is another
—with its effect spoilt in this book by printing in
eight-line instead of four-line stanzas.
Always Kipling’s trouble has been that he is nine-tenths
artificer and one-tenth artist. In The Five Nations he is
artificer through and through. The section of “Service
Songs ” holds some swift, bright impressions ; but never
for a whole flawless piece does Kipling bring convic-
tion that he is the soldier’s true mouthpiece. One may
be quoted for the Australian reference.
LICHTENBERG.
N.S.W. CONTINGENT.
Smells are surer than sounds or sights
To make your heart-strings crack —
They start those awful voices o’ nights
That whisper, “ Old man, come back."
That must be why the big things past
And, the little things remain,
Like the smell of the wattle by Lichtenberg,
Riding in, in the rain.
There was some silly fire on the flank
And the small wet drizzling down —
There were the sold-out shops and the bank
And the wet, wide-open town ;
And we were doing escort-duty
To somebody’s baggage-train,
And l smelt wattle by Lichtenberg—
Riding in, in the rain.
Jt was all Australia to mo—
All 1 had found or missed:
Every face I was crazy to see.
And every woman 1 'd kissed :
All that I should n’t ha’ done, God knows !
(A* He knows 1 ’ll do it again).
That smell of the wattle round Lichtenberg,
Riding in, in the rain !
And I saw Sydney the same at ever.
The picnics and brass-bands;
And the little homestead on Hunter River
And my new vines joining hands.
It all came over me in one act
Quick as d shot through the brain —
With the smell of the wattle round Lichtenberg,
Riding in, in the rain.
J have forgotten a hundred fights,
But one I shall not forget—
With the raindrops bunging up my sights
And my eyes bunged up with wet;
And through the crack and stink of the cordite
(Ah Christ ! My country again !)
The smell of the wattle by Lichtenberg,
Riding in, in the rain !
It would be natural if Kipling, for whom circumstance
and himself have built a reputation far above his literary
worth, were by the disillusionment of successive books to
shrink to his real size and due esteem. But the process
may be left more agreeably to clear-sighted Time. One
hopes that The Five Nations will not have a still more
degenerate following.
others received
God and the Agnostics, by “An Englishman”
(Lond., Swan Sonnenschein). A survey of philosophy,
religion, science and politics by an author with Australian
affinities who has read widely, thought shrewdly though
not clearly, and written so clumsily that his book is a
heavy, undigested mass of quotations and opinions, prac-
tically useless.
Essays in Buff (Lond., Walter Scott ; 2s. 6d.)
represent a modest reformer’s plea for the right use and
artistic'adaptation of . the human body. The writer
preaches the propriety and virtue of nudity ; counsels
the marriage of art and athletics ; and declares that “ the
artist is better employed in rendering the naked youth in
all the beauty and vigour of early manhood, than in
depicting the full-blown bourgeois in all the attributes of
vulgar magnificence." When he leaves art and life for
idealistic morality he is less convincing.
The Anglo-Saxon Century, by John R. Dos
Passos (Lond., Putnam; 95.), is an argument for “the
unification of the English-speaking people ” by “ a mem-
ber of the New York bar.” A smooth, clever, and com-
prehensive plea, sweetened to the average intelligence.
The subject has few practical facets at present, and one
watches with only languid interest Dos Passos
polishing. Suffice to say D.P. considers that, precedent
to a useful alliance between Britain and the U.S.A., the
latter must be bribed by the annexation of Canada. That
might be to Canada’s advantage, but discussion is useless
while nine-tenths of Canadians are of a different opinion.
The Exaggerators, by Alan Field. (Lond.,
Richards; 2s. 6d.) [Series of tales with usually an
Indian military background. The motives are cleverly
concocted ; the handling is often spirited. Some of the
author’s ideas are so good that one wishes for a master’s
touch : Field is limited and artificial.)
The Child’s History of Australia, by A.1.M. ;
first part. (Syd., W. Brooks ; 6 d.) [The scheme ig good ;
the details are not well managed. The author does nob
show good judgment in the choice of matter — e.g., Capt.
Cook and skin diseases; defines badly — e.g., transit of
Venus; writes slovenly — e.g., “One reason that the
Dutch did not care to people this country was that they
judged it by the coasts they saw ” ; blunders verbally—
e.g., “ A Dutch vessel called the “ Leewin,” which means
Lioness, sailed from Cape Leewin...” The little book is
badly printed on glossy paper, and the cover curls in
handling.)
A.G.S.
“The Man Who Does” discusses Trousers in
T.P.’s Weekly :—“ Never wear the same pair two days
together. This means that every suit must have an extra
pair, and the practice really is economical. Never lose
sight of the original creases made by the tailor’s iron ;
treasure these creases, and renew them faithfully after
every wearing. I have heard of ideal trouser-presses ; I
am prepared to admit they may exist ; but- I have never
seen them. A truly excellent method of pressing
trousers is to lay them carefully between bed and
mattress and then sleep on them for seven hours.
But the best trouser-press that I have ever had
is a folio edition of Plato in three volumes (printed
by Henry Stephen in Paris in 15— something), I place
the lower part of the fortunate trousers (as far as the
knees) on a chest of drawers, letting the upper part hang
down. I then stretch them out in a faultless shape and
drop Plato on the top of them. The result is worthy of
Plato’s world-wide reputation (when I want to read Plato
I use the two half-crown blue volumes in the Golden
Treasury series).”
The British censor of plays refuses to allow
Duse to perform d’Annunzio’s The Dead City on a London
stage. Europe and the United States have raised no
objection, but British morality is a sterner thing. Wit-
ness Piccadilly at midnight.

Up To-Morrow , by W. C. Platts (Lond., Long ;
2s. 6d.). [A book of “humourous” sketches, somewhat
like Adeler’s Elbow-Room, but less good. Still, for
English humour, the book is not so gloomy. The
" seventy illustrations ” fail to amuse.)
CONTRIBUTORS
Medicus : Was at the last Crossley evening in
Adelaide : had to sit on the steps of the starboard organ
gallery, a few feet from the songstress, with a good view
of her port quarter. From this position I could estimate
fairly her expansion of chest during forced inspiration.
Wonderful! Over the horizon of a well-modelled shoulder
one saw a curve heave slowly into view commencing at
the collarbone and ending about the fourth rib. At the
end of a long expiration this entirely disappeared, leaving
the space within these limits almost hollow. And yet
there was no gasp at the recovery. The action was as
easy as that of a blacksmith’s bellows and almost as
majestic. Excepting of this and her statuesque pose and
her absorbing voice, I have no recollection of her : her
singing was so utterly satisfactory that when she turned
and distributed some bows and smiles our way I was
thinking of something else. What a magnificent muscu-
lature she has! Certain dimples are the hall-mark of
beauty of muscle without undue fat. One, which C. lias,
is about the centre of the scapula where the trapezius,
latissimus dorsi and infraspinatus meet. One could see
this go and come with her perfect respiration.
C.G-.W.: Kendall on Browning (17/9/3) is
responsible for the following reflections on qualities in
Australian and other genius.
Some facts have to be pre-reeognised as an introduction
to the “ tortuous ” genius. What he wants to convey is
not a meaning of fact, definitely presentable in so many
words, but a mood-picture he has caught of that fact in
his psychic camera. The true poet at his height is
always a mystic, recognising some batfling quality or
expression in the sphinx-like face of things, which cannot
be written in the line : his words are just a doorway
into an inner world he sees. You must walk in, and let
the atmosphere reveal what it will to your native sense.
Poetry—Symbolism—are midsummer madness to folk
who can’t do this. In an artistic masterpiece, crammed
with meaning, the impatient type of critic who wants all
his art in sharp Rembrandt lights may see little more
than a four by three canvas. Another nify find his
universe mirrored there.
' Size, force, and presentation are three dominant
features in the demand of our Australian spirit, whether
the subject be men, manners or emotions. Fortunately
we temper assertiveness with the somewhat poignant
melancholy that usually broods over a shadowed under-
consciousness. We live in a merciless daylight out of
doors, but in a curtain-drawn luxury of gloom in our
retreat. In the clash of outer and inner forces we have
struck the personal note with some of the intensest heart-
beats that have been spoken into modern poetry.
This cross-stress keeps us to an incessant reaction of
consciousness. We cannot forget ourselves and plunge
away into an inward vision that is a pure reflex of some
living objective, whatever its mood-colouring. Too self-
centred for such abandon, we are at the same time
realists too sharp-sighted to give ourselves away cheaply.
Speak, by Heaven ! we must, but we won’t make fools of
ourselves. So, when we do let our souls go—-in paper or
atmosphere—we are short-cut and elliptical. Then we
get away as quickly and as far from the disburdened
sentiment as if it had turned corpse or Lot’s wife.
Our poetry is still in the constructive stage. We have
not yet drunk deeply enough of its life to have reached
the involuntary, which touches the highest in elemental
matter. (Take Matthew Arnold’s “The Buried Life"
and Wordsworth’s “ Ode to Immortality” as types of the
involuntary quality : here and there in our own Gordon
you find it—giving, more than the calibre of his poetry,
its charm.) In our new lands, with their mental atmo-
sphere still thick with adjustments and unsettled bear-
ings, we have not quite learned to let the spirit go—
beyond easy reach. We always have it (so to speak) by
the sleeve or collar—like an unbroken youngster who
might trip over the bric-a-brac, or the conventions, if we
did n’t watch him.
But we are true impressionists. We must have eternal
freshness and fact: we dare n’t linger on the notes—not
even to save the context. We have yet to learn the art
of completeness, which lies by way of aspect. Our native
genius has still to mature itself. When it does it will be
terse and vital, never out of hand-reach of the communi-
cable, but still with a passionate sense of the Want that
is more t han it can satisfy, the huge that is more than it
oan speak.
“Old Penjostler ” : As to Marcus Clarke’s fads,
Hs was once au apostle of the sun-bath as a cure for all
nervous diseases. One day he called on a friend at Carl-
ton who was suffering from sciatica, and as there was no
one else at home at the time, Marcus easily persuaded the
sufferer to go into the orchard at the back, peel off every-
thinc, and ramble round a la Adam before the Fall by
way of giving the sun a chance to soak in. The friend
looked so happy that Marcus decided to have a solar wash
also,• and the pair were soon strolling round eagerly
discussing one of those psychological subjects upon
which Marcus could talk so.learnedl.v and So seductively.
No notice of time was taken, until suddenly a peal of
woman’s laughter was heard quite close, and a female
voice called out—“ Come this way, Nellie ; Charlie’s in,
the summer-houseiiaving a smoke, I expect. Nice fellow
he is to leave in charge of a house ! ” That closed
the psychological discussion with a sudden snap, and
the naked Marcus darted behind the.gqually naked
Charlie, and holding him as a shield, excitedly whispered,
“ Good heavens ! What shall we do ? Those women are
between us and our clothes.” “Oh, it’s all right,” said
his friend; “It’s only my wife and her sister.” “Oh,
y-y-yes,” stammered poor Marcus, it may be all right for
you—vou know the ladies, and besides you’re a hardened
married man, but I’m an innocent bachelor, and I ’ll drop
dead if they see me in this shameful state. Good Lord,
here they come 1 Hide me ! Sit on me ! Shout to them to
go back. Don’t stand there like a dumb ass ; do some-
thing! No more sun-baths for me!” The “hardened
husband ” gave the warning, and Marcus soon got
into his clothes and over the back fence; but he never
smiled again—until he found himself in his favourite
Melbourne pub. trying to make the billowy-hosomed
barmaid believe she had been Cleopatra during a former
incarnation. (Marcus told this to,Garnet Walch, who told
me.)

[FOR THE BULLETI. ]
A Graveyard Chanty.
There’s a coffin being lowered
On the white ropes sliding easy ;
In a minute they’ll be heaving down
The gravel, clay, and stones;
They’ll stack white flowers above him
That will wither ere the morning;
The priest will ask a blessing,
And the worms can have the bones.
Ho ! he won’t be back to-morrow ;
He’s hid away for ever,
Staring wildly in the darkness
For the Reason of It All;
So braid your tresses, girlie,
Dry your eyes and stay your yearnings—
He wouldn’t heed your kisses
And he cannot hear your call.
Someone else will walk beside you
In the starlight of the future ;
Someone else will softly tell you
All the tilings he used to tell;
Someone else will pick his work up
Where he left it all unfinished—
The system never falters —
Someone else will do as well.
’Bye, good-bye, the ropes are running;
He’s hid away for ever ;
The rusty shovels grate among
The clogging clay and stones.
Leave the drooping flowers to wither ;
The grass will grow to-morrow ;
Someone else will do his duties,
And the worms can have the bones.
M. L.
J. BARR.
In the Shadow of the Hillside.
In the shadow of the hillside all the scrubland is
a-flower ;
All the magic and the beauty and the fragrance of
the Spring
Is astir in every gully, is awake in every bower,
Where the red-gum blossoms scatter and the
wild birds sing.
In the shadow of the hillside golden sunlight
streams and lingers
Through the fretted leaves a-murmur, on the
tangled fern and moss,
All the winds are softly playing, as with magic
fairy fingers,
Music of the merry woodlands, of the flowers
and the floss.
In the shadow of the hillside we have wandered
in life’s Maytime,
In the hours of youth and gladness, in the days
of long agone,
When the dewy morn was fragrant with the
scented winds of daytime,
While the birds and wild bees tarried and the
sunbeams shone.
From the shadow of the hillside all the glamor is
departing,
We have lost the keys of fairyland— the shining
silver keys—
And the woods have lost their magic, and we go
no more sweethearting
In the shadow of the hillside, ’neath the old gum-
trees.
VIO.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
The Retreat.
Against my lonely latter years
I ’ll build a faery home for me—
Proof against sorrow with its fears,
And age with its adversity.
Within a region bosomed high
Above the ways of worldly men,
In a demesne where by-and-by
I oft shall come and go again ;
Ah ! there my home in a green nook
Shall sweetly stand the siege of time,
Where Thought may read his riddle book
As to the murmur of old rhyme.
And faery footings still shall lead
My feet among mesmeric ways —
Where life is like a dream indeed,
And all the days are summer days.
But sylphs and fays and simple things
Shall murmur in my pensive ear,
Until the change shall come that brings
Me and my world to ruin here !
N.s.w.
R. CRAWFORD.
Noontide.
Wander free over hills and passes,
Rest ’mid the ferns in the hollows blue,
Lying at ease on the scented grasses,
Fresh and cool from the morning’s dew.
High overhead flames the sun in splendor,
Pungent the scent of the gum-trees tall;
Shimmer of heat on the foliage tender,
Noonday silence is over all.
Over all lies the hush of sleeping ;
A small spring sings me a drowsy song
In a silver thread from the hilltop creeping,
Kissing the pebbles it glides along.
The sun glows fiercely in molten splendor,
The wind wakes not and the birds are still 5
Only the sky, throbbing, blue and tender,
And the lullaby soft of the gliding rill.
’Tis the lotus-calm of the noontide hour.
A bee o’ercorae by the drowsy smell
Sweetly sleeps on the glowing flower
Where his weary velvet body fell ;
Rests till the breath of the night wind tender
Blows from the gates of the south ajar,
When the sun sinks red and the pale moon slender
Shall rise o’er the crest of the hills afar.
VIC,
K.A.H.

A Torturing Liver.
“HN EXPERIMENT."
“The Best Bargain I Ever Made.”
The Case of IV|rs. B. LftWUfJ.
(by a reporter).
The inventions of paper and the press
have put an end to all the restraints with
which our forefathers were fettered, and
they have not only made everyone a writer,
but have also enabled every mind to pour
itself into print and diffuse itself over the
whole intellectual world. The consequences
are inestimable, as, if the slow and laborious
operation of writing on parchment was still
in existence it would be difficult to acquaint
all our readers with such important matters
as are spoken of by Mrs. Bridget Lawler,
of Nortliwood-street, Camperdown.
‘ l l was once stricken with a terrible
malady,” began that lady. “It was an
attack of rheumatism which started in my
feet and worked its way into my hands, the
pain being so great that the joints felt as if
they were being screwed round in a vice. I
was very stout when I was first taken ill,
but four years’ intense suffering took all the
flesh off my body, besides making me very
weak and irritable. Some days the pains
were not so bad, while at other times they
were so severe that I was a regular cripple
through them, and could not get out of bed.
At the best my limbs were almost useless,
for I had to go from one room to another
on my hands and knees, so that will tell
you how sorely pressed I was.”
“ bid any complications arise?” queried
the newspaper man
“ They did indeed—any amount of them,
for my system seemed to be upset right
throughout. One of the chief causesofmy
weakness was through my food coming back
almost as soon as it was taken, and I hardly
got five minutes’ respite from letching both
day and night. No wonder, then, that my
eyes felt swollen and that my sight was bad,
and that 1 used to lie in a state of exhaustion
for hours, with my head pierced by sharp
pains as if rheumatism was there also. Oh !
it was a wretched time for me, and one
w hich I would not go through again for
thousands of pounds. I thought thevomit-
lng was caused through food being taken
against my inclination, but I w-as bound to
ea a, li tic sometimes, even though my
appetite w as gone, or I would have collapsed
altogether A heavy kind of pain in the
cnest was very troub esome, too, as it
seemed to weigh me down to such an extent
that I could not get my breath properly,
and thoughts of smothering often entered
my mind, although I was not afraid of
doing so in my sleep, because I very rarely
got any. It was quite an extraordinary
event for me to sleep three hours at a time,
ami only happened when I was so exhausted
that even my tortures could nob keep me
awake any longer ; but for all that I did
not feel rested afterwards, as the nasty
dreams I had seemed to take away all the
refreshing influences of sleep. When my
eves opened again I still felt languid and
heavy and so low spirited and desolate that
all brightness s< eised to have vanished from
my life. After taking any necessary
nourishment my stomach filled out with
wind, thus bringing into force another dis-
comfort, which frequently developed into
pains like the cramps, and lasting till every-
thing, as usual, was vomited. About the
slioiuders, too, I had reason to complain
owing to irritating pains getting a hold
tliei e, whde nothing could be more un-
welcome than the sickly taste I had in my
mouth every morning, besides having a
■whitish coating on my tongue. These
symptoms made me understand very clearly
that my liver was deranged to a serious
degree, and I suppose my doctors knew that
as well, but still I did not benefit myself iu
any way by remaining under their treat-
ment.”
“ How many doctors did you have?”
“ It would be very bard for me to say
exactly. Indeed, I had so many that I
cannot remember them all, and I have no
reason to keep them in mind, because not
one of them did me any good. The more I
went to the worse I got, and then I started
taking advertised pills and medicines, one
particular kind of which having such an
injurious effect upon me that after taking a
certain quantity I was almost paralysed on
one side of my body. There was very little
medicine I had not taken more or less of,
but I was now afraid to resort to any of
them again. I went on in misery for a
while without anything, but, being so help-
less that I could not even handle a cup, I
thought I would never get better that way,
and as I had tried everything but Clements
Tonic it would not do much harm to see
what that medicine was like.”
“ Was your experiment attended with any
better results ? ”
“It was the best bargain I ever made in
my life when I bought some Clements Tonic,
for there was no uncertainty or indecision
about its action or my internal organs,
which felt its curative propensities much
more readily than I had deemed possible.
For a long time past my nerves had been
very weak, and I was upset and tremulous
at all sorts of trifling incidents, but that
was one of my troubles which Clements
Tonic remedied straight away. My nervous
system was greatly invigorated after taking
three or four bottles, and then I ceased
meeting difficulties half-way, for I was so
much brighter in spirits and more hopeful
through receiving such benefit from
Clements Tonic that I thought I might yet
leave my sufferings behind if I gave that
medicine a fair trial. And thus when each
bottle was finished I got another, the
returns for my outlay being a good healthy
appetite and a steady falling off in my
pains. My headaches did not last long
against Clements Tonic, and all the time
my liver symptoms were losing their in-
tensity. 1 slept well at nights, because my
rheumatic pains were less violent, whilst
those about my shoulders and chest had
left altogether, leaving me to breathe with-
out the least distress. I got up with my
mouth free from its objectionable taste, and
after each meal of the day there was an
absence of flatulence and consequent pains ;
but to describe each healthful blessing
which came from every bottle of Clements
Tonic would, perhaps, take up too much of
your time. I must content myself by say-
ing that in a few weeks my internal com-
plaints were quite obliterated, and I was
able to get about my work with a cheerful
heart and a contented mind. The
strengthening and disease-removing powers
of Clements Tonic simply astounded me
after the many experiences I had with other
medicines, and I would be very ungrateful
indeed if I kept this knowledge to myself.”
“ Did your various pains leave as quickly
as the rest ? ”
“ No ; but although it took some time to
get me well and strong again in every
respect there is none the less credit due to
Cli ments Tonic, as it was the only medicine
which was able to bring me back to health,
and I am willing to let you publish my story
in any way you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.

Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC Tie in.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free He It, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen—ln answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month .t You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts..
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been. wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in yorir
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very .
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient, '
W. FOLLEY
GENUINE BELTS give GENUINE RELIEF.
Send to THE DR. McLAUGHLIN CO.,
100 W. Market-st., Sydney ; 167 J Swanston-st., Melbourne
64 Willis-st., Wellington, N.Z.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 19, 1903.
Wanted (again) A Freetrader who
Doesn’t Find his Creed too Foul to he
put in Practice.
The Commonwealth political campaign has com-
menced in earnest, and already almost everything
has happened except the Freetrade candidate who
promises to vote for Freetrade if he is returned.'
Also, anything that hasn’t happened yet may
probably be relied upon to happen before the
campaign is over—except the Freetrade candidate
who is going to vote for Freetrade. He never
happens. The fair green surface of Australia is
already black with the crawling of candidates who
declare that Protection is accursed, that whoever
advocates it is a fool or a knave, that whatever
country adopts it is on the high road to ruin ;
that Freetrade made England the greatest, richest
and most durable country on earth, and that
it is the only way to prosperity and the only thing
which makes one jolly and fat—and who also
promise solemnly that, if the people will only put
them in, they will not go in for Freetrade. These
amazing people offei', as the price of votes, to
practise a reasonable amount of the accursed
thing that leads to ruin ; and undertake, by way
of allaying popular alarm, not to heap all the joy
and blessings and wealth of Freetrade upon the
people on any account whatever. And their press
organs follow the same line, so that the campaign
is decorated with such things as these :
But Mr. Reid, as a matter of fact, does not propose any
radical alteration of the tariff. He stands for a revenue
tariff, and rightly so; but he recognises that, after the
turmoil and disturbance of the last two years, it is
essential to make haste slowly. Speaking a week or so
ago at. the Protestant Hall, he declared in effect that he
would be no party to another upheaval.—S.M. Herald
leader (13/11/’O3).
The tariff is still a thing of shreds and patches, with its
80 per cent, duties on boots and shoes, etc,, but with a
little judicious pruning it may be made a fair and reason-
able instrument for raising revenue. In his speech at
Sydney the other day Mr. Reid outlined the policy of the
Opposition. He does not propose—in the event of the
Ministry being defeated—to do more than apply the
pruning knife to excessive items. It is not intended to
throw the tariff again into the melting pot.—Launceston
(Tas,) Examiner,
Freedom of commerce should be the watchword of the
Opposition, even as is freedom of trade. The latter we
cannot enjoy to its full extent, because our financial
requirements (which may not be met by direct taxation)
demand a heavier tariff than Freeiraders like. But free-
dom of commerce, freedom of class dictation, we may
fight for, even as our forefathers have fought, and suc-
cessfully fought, for religious and political liberty.—
Edward Rraddon’s electoral address.
He, for his part, intended to go in for a reduction of
the existing tariff only.—Bruce Smith.
And so on ad nauseam.
* * . * *
The Australian Freetrade candidates thus divide
themselves into two classes. There is the class
which professes that it is going to be a traitor to
its principles under compulsion because it must
raise revenue (which statement is wholly false,
for there is no revenue difficulty in the way), and
the larger class which doesn’t allege any special
necessity, and which promises to betray its Free-
trade principles, and to help to injure its country by
practising a certain amount of Protection, out of
sheer villainy. As regards the former class—the
Braddon class—it is a very abject thing. If
Australia can’t follow Freetrade because it needs
revenue, then no country can follow Freetrade,
for every country needs revenue, and if no
country can follow Freetrade then why babble
about the foolish old impossibility ? But this
crowd of sickly apologists can’t help knowing that
its alleged revenue difficulty is pure invention
and a mere cowardly dodging of the responsi-
bility. The situation may be summarised thus :
The amount raised by all forms of taxation (Federal,
State and local) in the Commonwealth represents at pre-
sent £3 16s. 6d. per inhabitant. It is the necessity for
raising this sum which, it is claimed by Freetraders,
makes Freetrade impossible.
The amount raised by all forms of taxation (national
and local) in Great Britain is equal to £4 7s. 6d. per in-
habitant, and this necessity doesn’t make Freetrade im-
possible.
The essence of Freetrade, as practised in Britain (the
country which the Reid and Braddon party always points
to as the example to be followed) is to give no advantage
whatever to local over foreign industry. It is only neces-
sary to counterbalance all import duties which are held
to be necessary, with exactly equal excise duties, so that
the local and foreign product shall be taxed equally ; to
raise the rest of the revenue by direct taxation, as in
England where direct taxation supplies the great bulk of
the revenue ; to abolish all preferential railway rates in
favor of local produce ; to give no special postal advan-
tages to local newspapers, and to give all Government
contracts to the lowest tenderer —and we will have Free-
trade in all its essentials as Britain understands it.
* * * »
Of course, if Braddon and Reid and Bruce
Smith have degenerated into a state of cowardice,
which makes them unworthy of the name of
Englishmen, and unfit to do what Englishmen do,
then they may have some excuse—though even
then it is difficult to see where the excuse ccimes
in. By their own showing, no great courage is
required to practise Freetrade. The revenue the
country requires can be raised by Freetrade
methods—the example of Britain proves that; yet
the Braddon and Reid crowd won’t follow its
own constantly quoted example. Braddon and
Reid and Bruce Smith, and the rest, allege that,
when money is raised by Freetrade methods, it all
goes into the Treasury, whereas, if it is raised by
Protectionist methods, much of it is intercepted
by cormorant manufacturers, and they swear that
£1 levied from the people by Freetrade methods
brings as much coin to the Treasury as 255.,
or even more, raised by Protectionist devices ; yet
they refuse to adopt the cheap method. They
say, also, that under Freetrade the people are
much richer and can better afford to pay taxes,
yet, with no excuse save their own treason and
wickedness, they refuse to even attempt to adopt
the methods which will make the people so much
richer. Every politician and every paper of the
Freetrade faith says that Freetrade will make the
people wealthier, and at the same time diminish
the amount they have to pay out of their increased
wealth to support the State. And every man of
them promises that, if returned to power, he won’t
shake the country up by recklessly supplying all
these blessings. He calls his blessings an “up-
heaval,” and the saving of the country a
“melting pot.” And those of the party "who
explain this weird apostacy say they are driven
by the necessity of finding revenue to wilfully
adopt methods which will tax the people
more heavily, and at the same time make
them less able to pay the taxes ; that in order to
raise money they must adopt means that will
make the people have less money, and which, at
the same time, will make them pay more money.
The Australian Freetraders constitute the only
party which has ever appealed for support on the
ground that it was a liar and a traitor to its prin-
ciples, and that it was going to deliberately injure
the country if entrusted with office. Its election
cry may be condensed into a sentence : “Please
note what a scoundrel I am, and vote for me in
consequence!”
* * * *
The Freetrade party, wherever it lias been in
office in Australia—whether in N.S.W. or else-
where—has practised Protection more or less,
generally more. It has been a liar, and denied
the fact, in the face of its own tariff. It has been
another liar, and declared that any prosperity that
ensued was the result of Freetrade, though it knew
that Freetrade didn’t exist. Now it promises to
. be a liar again because it asks to be put into office
as a Freetrade party, and promises to give a fair
amount of Protection if it gets into office. Also,
some of it is an extra liar by adding that it is
driven to this course of action by revenue necessi-
ties, when the example of Britain proves that it
is not driven by anything or anybody save its
own evil conscience. And it has been all its life
a bumptious and scoffing combination, which pro-
fessed to look down with tremendous scorn on the
party which preached Protection, and wasn’t
ashamed to practise what it preached. It has
been very haughty on its pinnacle of cheap politi-
cal infamy, and neither it nor its pinnacle im-
proves on further acquaintance.
Why Britain Wearies of Freetrade.
When a general election is approaching in Aus-
tralia, and the unsinkable old tariff spectre floats
a little higher than usual on top of the sea of con-
troversy, a vast amount of crude bunkum re “ the
example of Britain ” is invariably poured out by
the Foreign-trade party, and eagerly swallowed
by a certain class. The general public, which
can’t accommodate many ideas in its head at
once, takes the “example of Britain ” very much
as it takes the hero and the blood-curdling villain
in the screamodrama. One of them is wholly
good and the other wholly bad, and that saves ail
trouble, and makes it unnecessary to analyse the
finer shades of character. The great mass of the
public dodges the wearisome details of history
in the same way. Its theory is th’at Britain
one day took to Freetrade all at once, and
prospered ever afterwards, while Protectionist
countries didn’t prosper. It is a simple theory and
saves much trouble; but, unfortunately, history
isn’t hacked off in square and easily understand-
able blocks in that fashion. It has a habit of
tapering off and doing things by degrees.
• • • •
About 1840, Britain was the most Protectionist
country in the world, and it was also by far the
richest. To be the richest country then, of course,
wasn’t anything like so good a thing as to be the
third or fourth richest now. There wasn’t much
gold in the world then, for the deposits of Cali-
fornia, Colorado, Klondike, Australia, Maoriland
and South Africa had not been discovered. The
world was very short of gold at that period, but
Britain had a lien on most of what there was. All
the manufactures produced in a year by the whole
world then were less than those produced in a
year by the United States now, for machinery
was still primitive, but the little British isles did
30 per cent, of what manufacturing there was to
do, and all the rest of the immense globe did the
other 70 per cent. There wasn’t very much com-
merce to do then, for steamers were few, sailing
vessels slow, and railways little more than com-
menced, but of what commerce there was to do
Britain did nearly one-fourth. Wages were low
everywhere, for with the primitive machinery of
those days a man couldn’t produce much value in
goods, but wages were higher in Britain than any-
where else. There was very little money in
savings banks, for there wasn’t much money any -
where, but of what money there was in the world s
savings banks Britain owned nearly two-thirds.
It wasn’t so very much of a world then but as
far as concerned banking, commerce, manufac-
tures, and general wealth Britain was supreme.
It was the most Protectionist country in the
world, and it pretty well owned what world there
was.
* » * *
Great Britain took to Freetrade by a slow
process of instalments extending from about 184 G
to about 1869. During that period a number of
things happened which made a rush of prosperity
all the world qver, and lent a curious glamor to
the beginnings of partial Freetrade. The greatest
gold discoveries in the world’s history were made
in California, Australia, and Maoriland, and they
poured in on Kurope such wealth as it had never
heard of before. During that same period the
railways of the world grew from 4500 miles to
125 000 miles. The steamships of the world
multiplied tenfold —in fact, it might almost
be said that the steamship began during this
period. Machinery was improved so vastly,
that a man could turn out twice or three times
as much goods in 1869 as in 1840, and with less
labor. Therefore hours were shortened, wages
were increased, and still the profits of manufac-
turers grew. And still Britain remained supreme,
though it was hardly so far ahead as in 1840.
Freetraders are accustomed to point to this
period of great awakening as an evidence of the
blessings of Freetrade, but it was a world-wide
awakening, and the Freetrade country hardly
secured its fair share of what was going. But
the contest between Freetrade and Protection
had not yet really commenced. During the period
between 1846 and 1869, or thereabouts, Great
Britain was a partially Freetrade country com-
peting with rivals mostly like itself. France
fooled with Freetrade doctrines for 18 years, till
1870, and Britain hold its own against Freetrade
France. Germany had a mere revenue tariff
till 1878, and Britain held its own against
Germany; in fact, Germany was absolutely
nothing in its Freetrade days—commercially, in-
dustrially and otherwise. The United States made
no serious atttempt at Protection until about 1865,
and the idea of the Freetrade or low-tariff United
States ever being Britain’s rival was one of the
world's jokes. Canada and Argentina were
countries with small revenue tariffs, and mere
names on the map. Italy was a revenue tariff
country, and a mere geographical expression—a
land of old ruins and organ-grinders. In 1840
Protectionist Britain, in an industrial sense,
absolutely bossed a world that was, in the main,
less Protectionist than itself. In 1869 Freetrade
Britain still bossed a world that was partly
Freetrade and partly of the humble revenue-
tariff description.
* * * *
A few years later the contest between Freetrade
and Protection really commenced, with astonish-
ing results to the Freetrade country. France
adopted Protection after 1871. Germany took to
Protection in 1878. The United States adopted
the Protective policy partially in 1865, definitely
about 1875, arid in the late 90’s went in for
the most vigorous Protection the world had ever
seen. Canada adopted Protection in 1878;
Argentina in the same year. It is only since these
events that Britain began to lose its grand old
self-confidence, and to feel the stress of com-
petition. The “made'in Germany ” bogey never
troubled it till Germany took to Protection.
United States competition was a jest and a thing
too absurd to be dreamed of till the United
States became Protectionist. What the world
knows for certain on the subject, now that the
fight between Freetrade and Protection has been
going on in real earnest for 15 years, is :
That every new State which has risen to sudden
industrial eminence (the United States, Germany,
Canada and Argentina are the principal examples)
is a high Protection country', and only' appeared on
the industrial horizon after it adopted high Protection.
That every country which is attracting a rush of immi-
grants (the United States, Canada, Argentina and Maori-
land are the most remarkable examples) is a high Protec-
tion country.
That no new Freetrade country has come to the front
as those Protectionist countries have done. Mexico never
attracted immigrants like Argentina ; nor Cape Colony
like Maoriland ; and Freetrade Scandinavia mouldered
while Protectionist Germany, with no more advantages,
rose, almost alongside it, like a new giant.
• * • •
Great Britain field its own better than the
other Freetrade States, but even it held its own
very badly. During the last half-century it has
built up the most sudden mushroom empire that
the world ever saw, barring, perhaps, that of
Alexander of Macedon. All through Victoria’s
reign it was annexing country at the rate of a
square mile every eight minutes, and it extracted
immense wealth from these conquered countries. It
annexed in South Africa the richest goldfield, and
the richest diamond field in the world. It got the
goldfields of British Guiana, and India, and the
ruby mines of Burmah. It forced most of its new
territories to keep their ports open to its trade. It
made thousands of miles of railways in its new
territories, and taxed the people heavily to subsi-
dise these railways, and the dividends thus raised
went to Britain. It annexed the banking busi-
ness of its new possessions and made great
profit thereby. It opened factories in its new
territories and worked them by local cheap labor.
A huge proportion of the gold of Australia,
Maoriland and Klondike went to Britain, and it
has just annexed another promising gold country
in West Africa. Britain started its Freetrade
experiment as the richest country in the world.
It bolstered up the experiment with the biggest
annexation boom the world ever saw. And with
it all the results are so bad that an alarmed
country thinks seriously ot taking to Protection
again.
* * * *
Mulhall’s great “Dictionary of Statistics”
shows how the Freetrade experiment progressed
in many important directions up till about 1898.
It has shown even worse results since then—so
much worse that it is only since then that the
bottom has practically fallen out of the Freetrade
superstition in Britain—but in order that the
public may conveniently verify the figures here
quoted, and test their reliability, and see that
they are in no way like the statistics of George
Reid and Pumpkin Smith and Max Hirsoh, only
those matters covered by Mulhall’s volume are
dealt with :
In 1840 Protectionist Britain din nearly 30 per cent, of
the world’s manufactures (£387,000,000" worth out -of a
world’s total of £1,314,000,000). In 1896 Freetrade
Btitain did about 16 per cent, of the world’s manufac-
tures (£876,000,000 out of a total of £5,710,000,000). It
had dropped to second place, with two other competitors
so close on its heels that it bade fair soon to assume
fourth place. (It has since got to third place, and the
whole British Empire now manufactures less than the
United States.)
In 1840 Protectionist Britain owned 60 per cent, of the
savings bank deposits of the world—the deposits in the
banksof the people(£23,4oo,oooout of £38,700,000). In 1890
it had per cent. (£154,800,000 out of a world’s toti lof
£1,458,000,000). Its people had less savings bank deposits
than those of the United States, Germany, France or
Austria. The savings bank deposits of the whole British
Empire in 1896 were only half those of the United States,
and rather less than those of Germanv (British Empire*
about £204,000,000 ; Germanv, £212,000,000; United
States, £446,000,000).
In 1840 Protectionist Britain was the richest country in
the world—so far the richest that competition was
nowhere. By 1896 it had fallen into second place, its
aggregate wealth being about £4,500,000,000 less than
that of the United States; and France and Germany
had both so nearly caught it up that it may soon be third
or fourth.
These are only a few points in the case, but they
are the most important points. The moral of
them is that Great Britain, which started Ps
Freetrade career as the richest country in the
world —not only the richest, but so far the richest
that it was worth two of its leading competitors
put together—and which has lent a fictitious
glamor to its Freeti’ade career by almost the most
stupendous land-stealing boom the world ever
saw, began to be left as soon as other countries
abandoned the Freetrade idea. For the last 15
years (since the beginning of Protectionism in
Germany), and especially for the last 13 years,
since the M‘Kinley tariff first rose on the Yankee
horizon, the world has seen a square fight between
genuine Freetrade—not the N. S.W. article—and
Protection. And already the people and , papers
of Great Britain are more than half inclined to
decide that Freetrade is a losing game.
* * * *
Of course, even if the Freetrade dogma still
held up its dishevelled head in Britain with the
old vigor, that would prove nothing in the case of
Australia. If Australia adopted Freetrade to-day
it wouldn’t do so under the favorable British con-
ditions, and it couldn’t possibly expect anything
like such good results as even Britain has shown,
bad as those results are. Australia doesn’t boss
the world in manufactures, orshipping, or wealth.
It hasn’t loaned money to all the earth, and it
hasn’t a lien on the globe that is accumulating a,t
compound interest. It can’t hope to make an arti-
ficial boom to disguise the disasters of its fiscal
policy by going out and annexing half-a dozen
countries, and appropriating the biggest gold and
diamond mines on earth. If Australia adopts
Freetrade—the real article, not the N.S.W. kind
—it has to start on the same basis as Holland,
Norway and Sweden, Turkey and Mexico, with a
fair and reasonable prospect of reaching the same
goal. They represent the results of Freetrade
upon an indebted country which doesn’t make an
artificial boom by almost the most frantic career
of conquest and annexation in history. Scandi-
navia and Mexico are respectable, plodding,
reasonably well-governed countries, but there is
certainly no great culmination or moral pinnacle
or shriek of glory about them to make Australia
desire to go out instantaneously and do likewise.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The ’Derry Party.
That very inefficient politician, Wilks of
N.S.W., enjoys the disgrace of being the first
Federal candidate to come out definitely in the
present electoral campaign on the dirty sectarian
issue. At a recent public howl in Balmain
Wilks is reported to have announced himself
as “a bigot and a firebrand, ” and declared that
he was proud of the titles. Also he claimed
political support on the ground that he was a
Protestant, and urged the people to return none
but Protestant candidates at the forthcoming
election. Yet with all his frenzied cries about
the walls of ’Derry and other dead and gone
rocks, he quite omitted to explain himself on any
important point, or to come down from the
clouds and get to particulars. Wherefore the
following questions are asked, and if Wilks will
supply definite answers to them a great many
things will be cleared up :
How does Wilks reckon that his demand that all
Catholics should be shut out of the new Australian
Parliament accords with his blather about civil and
religious liberty ?
What, exactly, does Wilks reckon will happen if some
Catholics get into the Australian Parliament? So far
there is only talk about the R.O. desire to do something
to the Education Act, but as the Australian Parliament
has nothing to do with the Education Act that excuse
doesn’t hold water.
What, exactly, does Wilks reckon will happen if none
save Protestants get into the Australian Parliament?
(This question to be answered without any loose irrele-
vances about the walls of ’Derry or Bagdad or Limerick,
or any other disconnected boulders.)
What are Wilks’ views about the nefarious partiall.v-
Freetrade policy under which Australia buys about
£3,000,000 worth annually from purely Roman Catholic
countries, instead of having the goods made here by Pro-
testant Australian workmen ?
Will Wilks vote for a specially heavy tariff on goods
made in Roman Catholic countries ?
Is Wilks prepared to vote that Roman Catholics should
be deprived of the franchise and shut out from Parlia-
ment?
Is Wilks prepared to exclude Roman Catholics from
the Civil Service ?
Is Wilks prepared to do anything that will make the
slightest difference—in other words, what legislation will
the people get if they elect Bill Wilks that they wouldn’t
get if they elected Patrick Hoolan ? (In answering this
question please leave out the walls of ’Derry and come
down to particulars.)
In other words, what is the exact nature of the lift that
Protestantism will get if Wilks and his sectarian friends
are elected ? Will they spend the public money to endow
Protestant churches and schools, or give Archbishop
Smith charge of education matters, or do anything of a
definite character, or will they merely talk about the
walls of ’Derry?
What is the exact nature of the present alleged Roman
Catholic conspiracy' ? Do the R.C.’s. propose to disfran-
chise Protestants, or shut them out of Parliament, or out
of the public service, or to make Cardinal Moran head of
the tramways, or to do anything tangible? The walls of
’Derry will please be kept out of the answer if possible.
Are Wilks and his friends aware that 'Derry isn’t in
Is the fact that he is a Protestant the only qualification
Wilks has as a politician that he puts it so conspicuously
forward? If so, what good will his Protestantism do to
the community, and how far will it help to supply work
and victuals ? The people can’t eat the walls of ’Derry.
If Wilks gets in on the walk of ’Derry ticket, what
is he going to do about finance, tariffs, old age pensions.
Federal land, income and absentee taxes, or Federalisa-
tion of the debts and railways ? How far will the walls of
’Derry help to clear up these matters?
When was the last Protestant burned in Sydney, and
did he crackle much?
These questions, though addressed specifically to
Bill Wilks, are also addressed to every other
politician who runs on the sectarian ticket. A
definite answer will be gladly accepted from any
one of the crowd—and published. Australia
wants to know the exact nature of the R.O.
danger, the exact nature of the steps which the
Great Deliverer, Bill Wilks, and his friends are
going to take to avert the danger, the exact
amount of extra Protestantism it will get if it
elects Wilks, and the exact amount of extra
Roman Catholicism it will get if it doesn’t elect
Wilks. It wants to know something tangible.
I twants decimals —not ’Derry.


About Some Labor Party Sweaters and
Anti-Unionists.
Now that another Australian election is close at
hand it is a suitable time for those Labor bodies
which have hitherto “sunk the fiscal issue” to
consider whether the hollow pretence of sinking a
thing that always rises to the surface is worth
maintaining any longer In other words, it is
time to consider whether there is any room for
Freetraders in an Australian Labor party, and
whether a Freetrader can be a sufficiently sincere
Laborist to be worth calling such. A Freetrade
Labor politician is an anomaly that requires a
great deal of explaining away He is an advocate
of trades-unionism and union rates of wages ; that
is to say, he demands that the Australian work-
man shall not work for less than, say, Is. an
hour, and shall not be undersold by other
Australians working for less. Yet he is
quite willing that he shall be undersold by
any foreigner who takes no notice of Austra-
lian union rates, and works for 6d. or 4d. an
hour. He would gladly prevent the use, sale,
or circulation of goods made by the Australian
who blacklegs hy working at, say, 9d. an hour,
and who thereby deprives the shilling-an-hour
Australian unionist of a job. Yet at the same
time he is willing to allow the free competition of
any foreigner who works at 4d. an hour, even if
that person deprives the shilling-an-hour
Australian unionist of his job. If the
shilling-an-hour Australian unionist, under
pressure of lack of employment caused
by the competition of imported goods, blacklegs
at a ninepenny wage, he is called a “scab ” and a
traitor to the cause, but there is no reprobation
for the fonrpenee-an-hour outsider who drove him
to it. The production and use of goods made
here by an Australian who works at 2d. an hour
less than the local union rate is accursed ; the use
here of goods made by anybody else who works
for (id. an hour less than the Australian union
rate is actually glorified as a blessed example of
free and enlightened policy. Sweating in Aus-
tralia is denounced by the Freetrade Laborist as
utterly disgraceful, but he is perfectly willing
to encourage the foreign sweater by admitting
as much of his goods as he can send here. He is
prepared to boycott Australian goods made by men
who work more than Australian union hours—but
not foreign goods. Cheap goods, made cheap by
sweating, are anathema if they are made and sold
in Australia, but not if they are made anywhere
else and sold in Australia. His theory is that the
Australian workman should demand good wages,
and yet that he should be able to produce goods
so cheaply that they can compete with the goods
made by equally capable foreign workers at half
or one-third the wages. He holds that the supply
of goods made by the Australian who cuts under
our union rate is disastrous, while the supply of
goods made by the foreigner who cuts much fur-
C r below our union rate is beneficial. That
is what the Freetrade Labor member demands
if he lives up to his creed ; the only
thing that prevents the iniquity of the posi-
tion becoming fully apparent is the fact that
there has never been Freetrade in any part of
Australia. The Freetrade Laborist is the advo-
cate of the foreign sweater and the foreign black-
leg. It is no use demanding that the Australian,
in any specified trade, shall not work for less
than, say, Is. an hour, when he must do it, or else
lose his billet, because the goods of the foreigner
at 6d. an hour will otherwise take the place of
those he produces. Sinking the fiscal issue is
impossible in Labor politics. The trades union
wage is the fiscal issue. There is nothing more
distinctly fiscal in all the Commonwealth.
N.S.W.’s Sore-Headed Minority.
Thebe has been much weeping, ever since the
Commonwealth Parliament first met, about how
the Australian Government is run by “Victorian
influences.” Certainly N.S.W. has grabbed a
huge and wholly extravagant allowance of the
honors that were going. It supplied the Premier
and two other Cabinet Ministers, making an alto-
gether disproportionate share of the Cabinet; the
leader and deputy leader of the Opposition ; the
Chairman of Committees, the leader of the Labor
party; the Government whip, and the Opposition
ditto; it annexed two out of the three Federal
judgeships, and most of the good civil service
billets. Queensland got the third Federal judge-
ship, and the Speakership of one House and
the Presidency of the other went to South Aus-
tralians. Victoria took hardly anything, yet the
Victorian influence was always there, and the
Victorian vote kept the Government in office.
Therefore the Sore-Headed Minority in N.S. W.
complains bitterly of some intangible wrong that
has been done to its State. The wrong, if it could
be called one, was unavoidable by reason of the
attitude of the greater part of N.S.W. and the
bulk of the N.S.W. members, so the past may
rest; the question now to be considered is whether
N.S.W. is going to deliberately continue its unfor-
tunate attitude. Australia, in the main, is Pro-
tectionist, and is likely to grow more so instead of
less. There is a Protectionist Government, a Pro-
tectionist majority, and an angry, yapping,
unpatriotic Freetrade minority consisting of men
like Ketd, Bruce Smith, Pumpkin Smith, .Toe
Cook and M'Millan who never had the
courage of their convictions while in office.
The State which supplies the largest and
most solid phalanx to the winning side (which
State is Victoria) has the largest say and the
biggest influence in shaping the country’s policy.
The State which supplies the largest contingent
to the Sore-Headed Minority (which State is
N.S.W.) has the sorest head, the loudest yap, and
the largest sense of injury. It is bound to be so
unless the fixed laws of arithmetic can be altered,
and if N.S.W. ch: oses to continue voting the
exploded Freetrade ticket (which is no use what-
ever to it, for it never attempts to establish Free-
trade even when it gets a Freetrade Government
in office) things must remain in their present con-
dition. The Protectionist majority will run the
Government. The Victorian section of that
majority, being the largest section, will mainly
decide what the Government's policy is to be.
And though N.S.W. may continue to be liberally,
even lavishly treated, as in the past, it will be
so by the grace and tolerance of Victoria. It
has -deliberately chosen that position; if it
again returns an alleged Freetrade majority it will
deliberately choose that position again. If
N.S.W. likes to return a solid Protectionist
phalanx, then it, and not Victoria, will be the
largest element in the Government following;
and it, not Victoria, will have the principal voice
in deciding the Government policy. N.S.W.
started the latest Federal movement, and did so
with a full knowledge of the political views of the
people it was asking to join it. Its own principal
clown and contortionist called them “ the five
drunkards ” to emphasise the fact that they were
not of N.S.W.’s unfortunate way of thinking, it
being a peculiarity of the drunk to imagine that
other people are intoxicated. It knew perfectly
well that an allegedly Freetrade State, which had
never dared to practise its own alleged creed, had
very little show of inducing other States to accept
that creed. So it deliberately applied for, and
naturally got, the billet of Sore-Headed Minority
to the Australian Parliament —that is almost the
only billet which is always to be had for the
asking. The question to be settled now is whether
it is deliberately going to apply for the same
billet again.

The N.S.W. Labor Party’s Attitude
The Wokker’s latest explanation of the action of
N.S.W. Labor party in supporting the Owe’See
Government’s loan madness reads as follows:—
We had argued that the N.S.W. Labor party was par
tially justified in condoning the See Government’s extra
vagance because it had, at all events, filled the mouths of
the otherwise workless with earned bread. The Bul-
letin’s reply is that we are afflicted with a strange mental
disease in condoning the doing of this by means of bor
rowed money when £3,000,000 of additional revenue
might have been raised by an increase of land-tax on
large estates and the income-tax on large incomes, by
the levying of an absentee-tax, and by making local
government bodies increase their rates and carry a larger
share of responsibilities. The Worker most assuredly
agrees with the raising of additional revenue by some of
the named methods, but at the same time would suggest
to The Bulletin that if the See Government had re-
frained from giving employment until its coffers had been
filled by these means that starvation, destitution, and crime
would long ago have claimed their own, and that our gaols,
asylums, and graveyards would ere now have been filled
to bursting point. For we have an Upper House, and the
bowels of its compassion are bound around with staves of
iron, and its soul is deposited in a steel vault along with
its mortgages and title deeds. We venture to say that if
See had delayed to provide employment until he had
passed a progressive land-tax, The Bulletin would have
damned him long ere this for a cold-blooded monster.
This is even a less convincing defence than the
previous one. If the N.S.W. Upper House
refused to pass Bills for increased income-tax,
graduated land-tax, absentee-tax, and the other
measures necessary to raise money for public
works, it could be swamped by new ap-
pointments. But the Bills were never intro-
duced, and the Upper House didn’t reject
them, and it wasn’t swamped. The Upper House
wasn’t responsible ; it never had a chance to be.
The See Government and the Labor party made
no attempt to do anything in the matter save bor-
row. Even assuming that the increased taxes
required couldn’t be imposed and collected fast
enough to meet the emergency, which isn’t certain
as no one tried to collect them, the needful money
for relief works could have been borrowed for a
year, or two years, and repaid out of the new
taxes as they came in ; but the Labor party made
no attempt to drive the sluggish See to impose
any new taxes, or to repay anything. There is
plenty of time now to impose these new taxes, and
set aside the proceeds for the repayment of theloans
—mostly short dated loans which will have to be
repaid soon—which, it is claimed, were necessi-
tated by the recent very bad drought experience ;
but the Labor party is doing just as little now to
stir the sluggish See into honesty as it has done all
along—that is to say, it is doing nothing at all.
The N.S.W. Labor party has settled down to be,
by its own choice, a party of huge borrowings, of
deficits, and of let-it-slide, and its chief mission is
now, apparently, to whitewash that rich man.
See. In practice, if not in theory, it has dropped
graduated land-tax, absentee-tax, honest finance,
and pretty nearly everything that distinguishes it
from any other party. Unless it can resurrect its
good old self it is a lost party, and might as well
be a dead party.
A Tragedy in Finance.
After all, S. Australia (according to its Audit
Commissioner) did not have a surplus. For a few
months before the end of the financial year it
looked as though it were going to have a deficit;
but then a rich ironmonger died, and, by waiting
impatiently on his executor’s doorstep on the last
day of the year, the Treasurer managed to collect
enough probate duty on account to bring out the
State’s balance about £.3000 to the good. It
was such a very little surplus; yet S. Aus-
tralia took so much trouble to get it,
and none of the other Eastern States could
get one of any size at all, that Adelaide
felt proud, and proceeded to reduce its
land and income taxes. And now the Audit
Commissioner says it was all a mistake, and that,
as the Commonwealth Government credited to
»S. Australia £4OOO which ought to have been
credited to the N. Territory, the surplus of £3OOO
was really a deficit of £lOOO ; and all the weary
waiting on the dead man’s door-step was of no
avail, and the pride was built upon a foundation
of sand, and the reduction of the taxes was
achieved by false pretences. Surely there was
never anything more like a tragedy in Australian
finance—this pitiful little struggle to show a
surplus, the proud expansion of the chest when
the surplus was shown, and then the horrid
discovery that it was all a dream—all except the
reduction of the landlord’s tax, and that is solid
reality. Of course, as a matter of hard truth,
S. Australia didn’t have a surplus in the
first place; it simply showed one by
ignoring the Northern Territory deficit of over
<£loo,ooo in spite of the fact, which it doesn’t
deny, that it is just as much responsible for the
Northern Territory deficit as for one of its own.
But perhaps it is even harder, after you have
gone to all the trouble of faking a surplus, to find
some unimaginative wretch of an Auditor-General
denying you even the satisfaction of owning the
fake.

“S.M. Herald” Denounces the Empire
Builders.
S.M.H., on Nov. 9, fired by loyalty or something,
went a little too far in its antagonism to White
Australia:
The only justification for the holding of Australia by
the handful of people who occupy it is to develop its
virgin resources FOR THE TEEMING POPULATION
OF OTHER LANDS. Europe has need of its raw pro-
ducts, and there is absolutely no excuse for their being
withheld by any narrow Federal policy, which would
raise a wall round the continent and confine its benefits
to 4,000,000 of people, or to any number who, as the re-
sult of it, are likely to come here within the next cen-
tury. Any claim of so few people to pursue the Deakin
policy is nothing less than an outrage upon the rights of
humanity; a selfish prevention of the use of territory
which we have not created ; but which ive have been
simply put in possession of by the enterprise and expendi-
ture of the yreat nation from which xve have sprung.
Such a policy is but a little removed from that by which
Mr. Kruger sought to shut the world out of its rights in
South Africa.
The miserably unpatriotic Herald has now got
to the point of asserting that the Australian has
no rights on Australia ; he only holds the country
on sufferance for the benefit of the “teeming”
nigger. Yet when an individual Australian land-
lord holds 100,000 acres of good land, and keeps
it as a sheep-walk, and shuts out the “ teeming”
human race generally, and blocks settlement,
and puts up notices that trespassers will be prose-
cuted, the S.M. Herald calls any attempt, by
graduated land-tax or otherwise, to make him
admit the teeming population “a dastardly
socialistic outrage on the rights of property.”
For 4,000,000 Australians to hold for their own
use 3,000,000 square miles (say, 480 acres each)
and warn off the nigger, is a crime; but for one
Australian to hold 300,000 acres and warn off every*
body is a beautiful instances of the “rightsof pro-
perty.” And the Herald’s toothless jaws are full
of lamentation that we have no right here because
we were put in possession “by the. enterprise
of the great nation from which we are sprung.”
Aren’t we that nation ? It was the adventurous
English pioneer who came here and became an
Australian who made Australia—not the dull,
plodding English cheesemonger who stayed
behind. The Herald, like most of the organs of
silly “loyalism,” thinks that the English pioneer
who came here should lose all his claim to con-
sideration ; that, having enlarged the empire by
adding to it a new continent, he should spend the
rest of his days grovelling to the British grocer who
stayed behind, on the ground that the grocer did
the enlarging of the empire; and that the Eng-
lishmen who blazed the track —the men who
founded Australia, and who became Australians—
should believe that they owe the new country
wholly to the “enterprise” of the Englishmen
who stayed behind and did nothing but sell calico.
The Herald’s view is that when once an English-
man goes out to spread the bounds of the empire,
and build new cities for it, and explore new lands
for it, he becomes accursed ; he loses all claim to
consideration ; and he is only fit to be lectured on
his base ingratitude to the Englishman who
never moved out of Cockneydom. The Empire-
builder is the only man, in the Herald’s idea,
who should have no voice in the destinies of the
empire. No paper has more grossly and coarsely
insulted the Englishmen who made the empire—
the man who carried its blessed Flag all over this
continent—than the S.M. Herald, and the
curious thing is that the gummy old organ hasn’t
the least idea of what it is doing.

The Fall of Cold Tea and Co.
Premier Skddon’s Licensing Bill has been thrown
out by Maoriland House of Reps.—which event
is one of Skddon’s biggest triumphs up to date.
He has been ostensibly defeated, but he has
rubbed the ear of Cold Tea in the gutter, and
filled its eye with mud, and dabbed its head into
the ash-barrel of humiliation. Hitherto Prohi-
bition has been something of a farce in Maoriland.
When it was voted in any district the hotels in
that district were closed, but any man who could
afford to buy in quantities could get his liquor
sent to him from the nearest wine-merchant, and
keep it in his own house, and drink himself blind
as often as he wanted to. The Prohibition vote,
in fact, was made up of three classes of voters :
(1) Those who didn’t drink.
(2) Those who voted for Prohibition because it was the
correct form of snuffle and a pood advertisement, but
who laid in their own whisky and drank as usual.
(3) Those who voted for Prohibition for snufflesome or
other reasons, and laid in their cwn whisky, and drank a
great deal more, privately, in the house, than they had
dor.o when they were in the habit of drinking publicly in
hotels.
The Prohibition party for years stormed Seddon
with demands for more drastic legislation, and
when Seddon had counted up the forces in the
House he found that he must do something.
Then he did a fiendish act. He introduced a Bill
providing that there should be real Prohibition in
Prohibition districts, that no liquor should enter
those districts, and that any person having liquor
in his house should be heavily punished. And
the Cold Tea party had to vote against it because
it would have alienated the Drinking Prohibi-
tionist, and the party couldn’t afford to lose the
Drinking Prohibitionist’s support. Now Seddon
laughs wildly in the background, and says:
“Here is my Bill providing iov real Prohibition
thrown out by the Prohibition vote in the
House! ” It is a triumph, but it may cost
Seddon dearly. The Cold Tea party will never
forgive him for putting it so artistically in the
hole.
