Wanted—Better Paid Australian
Politicians.
There is a lamentable shortage of candidates in
many places for the coming Australian election.
Every party organisation is tearing its hair in the
struggle to find men to put up who seem at all
decently qualified to uphold the party’s views.
And in many places, because the men that the party
wants won’t come forward, it is being forced to
put up men whom it doesn’t want—tenth-rate
men who won’t supply any brains to the cause,
and whose only qualification is that they agree
to trust to the party leaders for the
brains, and to vote as the party leaders
bid them. This is only the second elec-
tion under the Australian constitution, and
already the miserable inadequacy of the
wage paid to members begins to be manifest.
Even after one Parliament the big men are
dropping out, on the ground that they can’t afford
to remain in. Australia is a very commodious-
place, and the majority of members have to go a
long way to the scene of action, and when a mem-
ber pays his extra living expenses there, the
amount available out of his salary of £4OO a year
amount available to maintain the home he
left behind him —is seldom much over £3 a week.
Therefore Australia is coming down to the posi-
tion that it must choose its candidates almost
exclusively among these classes :
Young men who were born rich —a small and very un-
desirable class, with, as a rule, very little knowledge of
politics and a comfortable belief that, because it is well
off, the world is a very good world and doesn’t require
improvement.
Ancient tired-out wrecks who have made their fortunes,,
and are willing to give the fag-end of their lives to politics.
These, as a rule, know very little of politics, and are too-
old to learn. Also they feel it isn’t worth while repairing
a world which they are to leave so shortly.
Men who are busy making their fortunes, but who are
willing to drop in for a minute or two now and then, and
vote for or against something, without having time to-
inquire what it is, and who will then rush back to their
bales and their ledgers.
Incapables who are glad of a job which srives them evem
£3 a week over and above working expenses, because
they can’t earn so much as that at any other occupation.
Four classes, and it is difficult to say whiqlv-is the
most undesirable. Australia wants good profes-
sional politicians ; like every other country it
must choose between the professional politician
and .the amateur politician, and the' latter
is just as complete a failure as the amateur
carpenter, or the amateur Judge, or the ama-
teur watchmaker. Unless it pays for good-
professional politicians it will get bad profes-
sional politicians. And just because Australian
politics involve longer travelling distances,
greater expense, more complete neglect (by
reason of the distances) of any business that is
left behind, the Australian Parliament may get
even worse professional politicians in the end
than the State Legislatures—unless it pays for
better ones. The United States, which is
situated very much as Australia is, recognises
this, and pays its Federal legislators £lOOO a
year, also, travelling allowances and allowance for
secretary and stationery. Australia will yet have
to do the same, and it can’t make the cha’ ge too
soon. Anyone who compares the calibre of the men.
who are dropping out of Australian politics
because they can’t afford to remain in, or who
threaten to drop out for the same reason, with
the calibre of the new men who are coming to tho
front, may see the beginning of a tendency
towards a cheap Australian Parliament—cheap
and nasty.

The excitable and ill-tempered Judge Docker
let himself loose again at Orange (N.S.W.) re-
cently, and thus addressed an acquitted prisoner :
You are discharged. The jury say you are not guilty,
but how they arrived at that decision I cannoc under-
stand. I don’t know how the jurymen would like to go
into the witness box and then be told that their evidence
was not believed.
The Judge thus, by implication, laid down these
three propositions :—:
(1) That it is a gross insult if the jury doesn't believe
the witnesses against the prisoner, no matter how weak
they may be.
(2) That it doesn’t matter at all if the jury insults the
witnesses for the defence by not believing them.
(3) That the jury should convict every prisoner lest the
witnesses who testified against him feel insulted.
(4) That a Judge may use insulting language to a free
citizen if he feels that way
Isn’t N.S.W. getting just a little tired of its
Docker ? It certainly ought to be.
A letter from “J.J.”:—
lie your leader on the N.S.W. Labor party. I have
been a battler for the party since ’9l, and I am going-to
stick to it on the ground that it is, still streets ahead of
either the See or the Carrutiiers crowd. Still, I am com-
pelled to agree with all you say about the party. I know
almost every one of the 25 members personally, and I can
only find three or four real men amongst them ; but even
that is a better average than either of the other parties
can show. When I say men, my standard is high. I
mean men at once brainy, courageous and honest. I
place them in this order- Holman, Griffith, Dacey, afld
Donald Macdonell. None of these four is popular with
the ruck of the party. They are aggressive, and the
cringers call them ambitious ; they dare to talk si might,
and they call them reckless ; they won’t smo. dge or
cringe, so the wasters call them tactless. But these are
the men that we, outside, appreciate, and Griffith will
get a vote for the Senate that will astonish a lot of
people. M’Gowan is a straight, good man, too, but
slow-witted and cautious almost to cowardice. So
much does he dread a change that he nearly
wrecked the Party by the tenacity with which he
stuck to George Reid. Edden has a bluff, effective stylo
of speaking, but he has never read a book in his life, and
thinks coal-mining the only thing worth legislal ing about.
For all his bounce he lacks real courage, and will never
take the unpopular side. Cann, as an officer of the
House, is, of course, not in the fighting line. Neilsen,
Hollis, Hugh Macdonald and Scobie are good second-
grade men, straight but narrow. As for the rest—well,
they are like the ruck of all political parties—decenr,
well-meaning fellows, to whom a culvert is more than a
Constitution, and who can see nothing else in public life
as important as Bill’s dirt-money. I admit the party is
at a low intellectual ebb just now, but there is lots of
good material outside, and I am not going to give up
hope yet a bit, and I hope The Bulletin won’t either.

The Queensland local-governing person, who has.
for years past regarded the State as an institution
divinely appointed to spare him from taxing
himself, is beginning to think unkindly of
Kidston, the new Treasurer. “ We’re going
to try to be honest, and so must you be,”
Kidston says in effect; “so pay up.” The
amount of overdue interest and redemption is
about £190,000; and the Treasury is calling for
it in a tone that the local tax-dodger
doesn’t like. To get round Kidston some
of the local bodies are asking now
for permission to issue debentures on
their own account. Queensland has hitherto
followed the practice of allowing municipal bodies
to borrow only from the State; and, with an
honest Treasurer owning a backbone, this is
an excellent idea, because the Treasurer can
then insist upon the loan being paid in half-yeai ly
instalments, instead of allowing the local property
owner to pass it on to another generation, to he
paid when the thing that was bought with the
money has been worn-out. Fortunately, Kidston
sees through this dodge, and seems disposed to
give it no encouragement; and that is the riiht
attitude, for it would be idle to slam the
door against wild State borrowing and open
it to wild local borrowing. Whether, however,
it ought not to be made some sort of an
offence for a local-governing body, once it has
borrowed from the State, to ask for suspension of
interest and sinking fund payments, is another
matter. Even the man who, like Kidston, is
game to say “No” (he said “No” witli great
emphasis to a Southport deputation a few days
ago), may suffer for it; and that shouldn’t be—it
is the people who try to induce the Minister to
break a contract and thus rob the State, who
ought to have something heavy fall upon them.

Apropos A.M.P. general manager Teece’s remark
that, though the State pub. at Gwalia (W. A.) was
a success, still
If the Government took control of the liquor traffic in
a city like Perth, or in big places like Sydney or Mel-
bourne, political influence would immediately be exer-
cised, and Government supporters would, perhaps, make it
a condition of their support that the Government should
purchase liquor from some of their clients —
why is it always assumed that the politician who
is at the head of a Government department must
be a rogue ? The A.M.P. has 33 directors in Aus-
tralasia, and more than half of them (17, to be
exact) are politicians. Several of them have been
Premiers, still more of them have been Cabinet
Ministers. How is it that they don’t ruin the
A.M.P. ? The Bank of New South Wales has
six directors, and four of them are politicians ;
the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney has five
directors, and four of them are in Parlia-
ment ; the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. has
seven directors, four of them in politics; and so
on through the long list of successful Australian
concerns. Nobody suggests that these private
enterprises must fail because the politicians
who run them are not honest enough to run
straight; but as soon as it is suggested that
the same politicians should manage the same and
other things as public enterprises, the discovery
is made that they are a gang of scoundrels who
will ruin the business.
♦
Governor-General Tennyson, who leaves Aus-
tralia soon, makes the sad mistake of mixing up
with his valedictory speeches a good deal of
political advice. One daily paper, the other day,-
seriously discussed Tennyson’s “platform,” as
though he were Prime Minister or leader of the
■Opposition, instead of the rubber-stamp hired to
sign things when told to, and to look pleasant in
public as often as possible. The “platform” is
in some respects good, in others, otherwise—
economy, consolidation of debts, water conserva-
tion, closer agricultural settlement, industrial
arbitration, etc. But the point is not whether
Tennyson is a good or a bad politician, but that
he has no right to be a politician at all. His
position as Governor forbids him getting on the
political stump. If he will resign his governor-
ship, get an elector’s right and come out for the
Legislature, The Bulletin will be prepared to
■consider Tennyson’s candidature on its merits
and sink any natural prejudice against lords.
But whilst he is Governor, Tennyson’s one duty
in regard to politics is to keep silent.

By blurting out the fact of his approaching
resignation a couple of months before it was
timed to come off, Yic. Premier Irvine cruelly
upset the calculations of his colleagues, Shiels and

The Comte de Rossi, who died last week
in Sydney, at the age of 81, was an erratic French
nobleman with a quaint capacity for self-adver-
tfsement and a constant desire to do battle with
somebody or anybody over a piece of fluff or some
other trifle. He became a public character a
number of years ago, when he and the
Bishop of Goulburn skirmished for years
in the law courts over some matter con-
nected with a tablet in the Cathedral. The
late Count stood a siege in the holy edifice for
some days, and when the holy edifice was opened
with an axe or some such carnal weapon instead
of with prayer, and the Count was carried out and
laid on the pavement, the legend goes that he
refused to rise till the photographer had come and
taken his portrait as he lay encumbering the
highway. Then he went to Sydney to interview
the papers, and explain how profoundly calm he
was while defending the cathedral, and how there
were no unmanly fears under his waistcoat while
the bishop’s minions raged outside.
From Sydney Telegraph, Nov. 13 :
Now that the Wentworth selection has been made,
interest in Sydney will be centred upon Lang. The posi-
tion there has undergone some changes during the past
two years. In accordance with a resolution reported
some days ago, a meeting was held at Newtown on
Wednesday night to determine which of the Freetraders
should contest the seat. This meeting, which was held
in private, was attended, it is understood, by delegates
from the various electorate branches of the Australian
Protestant Defence League. Five names were submitted
—Messrs. Johnson, Trevarthen, Cullam, Salomon, and
Martin. Ballots were held until the number was reduced
to two, Messrs. Johnson and Cullam receiving an equal
number of votes. The meeting being unable to arrive at
a final selection, it is stated that the matter was referred
to the council of the Protestant Defence Association.
There doesn’t seem to be much real difference,
according to this, between the George Reid party
and the Dill Macky party at the elections, since
“The Protestant Defence Association.” that
noisome gang of sectarian stinkpotters, has the
final say as to the choice of the Freetrade candi-
date. It is, after all, appropriate that the party
of foreign trade and foreign borrowing should also
favor bringing foreign religious rows into Aus-
tralian politics.

Nellie Stewart had a cordial reception at
the revival of “ Sweet Nell,” on Saturday last, at
Sydney Royal—this despite the fact that the piece
has been very hard-worked during the last year
or so. It was intended to stage “Peg Woffing-
ton,” but time did not permit, as the co. breaks
up with the close of this week. “Sweet Nell”
has, however, just about this one last run left in it,
and so far it has played to good houses. It is prac-
tically identical, as regards cast, with the first pro-
duction in Sydney, theonly important change being
that Harry Hill plays Chief Justice Jeffries —in a
somewhat quieter key than Albert Gran favored.
Miss Edith Stewart, as Lady Olivia Vernon,
is also a new and pleasing minor feature. For
the rest it is the same thoroughly artistic Har-
court Beatty as King Charles, and the same
brilliant-in-patches Nellie Stewart as “Sweet
Nell.” Miss Stewart gives a popular, if not alto-
gether consistent reading to her character. Her
Nell is sometimes orange girl and sometimes
grand Duchess, and doesn’t make any effort to
grow from one to another ; but she is always an
attractive figure, and is an even more wonderfully-
dressed figure than in previous productions.
Friday will be the last night of “Sweet Nell.”
* * * *
What will happen then at the Sydney
Royal was unknown when The Bulletin went to
press. Mr. Musgrove’s comic opera co. will have
reached the Commonwealth before Saturday, and
with it a new opera, “The Serenade.” But a
lawsuit—Slapoffski v. Musgrove—will have also
readied a hearing, and several members of the co.
will probably be in Melbourne giving evidence on
Friday. If so, the law will have to hustle con-
siderably out of its usual stride to allow of these
people singing in Sydney on Saturday ; the case
will have to go through very rapidly, and the
mummers will have to put up a record sprint to
Spencer-street station afterwards. But possibly,
when the parties to the law-suit gather together,
“Slap.” will mount a chair, disarrange his hair,
and waving a walking-stick, recall old pleasing
memories, and everybody will dissolve into tears
and music, and there will be no more thought of
damages.
** * *
The domain of comic opera is a pleasant
land where everything is in a delightful state of
topsy-turveydom—where the moon is always new
or full; where the crops are green and growing, or
golden and ready to be reaped ; and where every-
one can sing or dance ; and everyone is young.
When age is introduced it is there to be laughed at,
good-naturedly, as a has-been that cannot teach the
prima donna or the leading lover, singing through
the oardboard streets at noonday, anything worth
learning. A domain where none asks the raison
d'etre of anything, for there isn’t any, and where
few of the incidents dovetail, and where people
and things that have nothing to do with the case
drift in and out, and no cold-nosed critic rises to
ask plaintively, “ What about the classic unities?”
Tliese aberrations passed over writer’s mind
qn a second look at the “ Country Girl ”
(Her Majesty’s, Sydney). The Rajah of Bhong
interlude lias all the materials for a tragedy, but
the consequences don’t tradge in the least—they
only end in laughter. Claude Bantook, as the
Rajah, (n a white European suit and a turban,
with an emerald big as a turkey egg blazing in
front, sings his comic songs in a way for
which we are grateful—every word is heard,
Miss Ghiloni, as his Indian betrothed wife,
ha,s never h,ad a better show than as the Princess
Mehelena,h. She glows in an orange robe,
swathed, not fitted , round her voluptuous form,
ajidj with her ropes of turquoises and her forehead
gem, she. looks the. Jewel of Asia from the ground
up, and her singing of “Under the Deodar,”
makes us long to sell our gum-tree at a great re-
duction and buy a deodar with the proceeds.
Also, Miss Rose Musgroye’s “ Chook-Chook ”
duet vyith Lauri almost drives writer to go

Very soon after these few rerparks reach
the. eyes of an expectant public, Miss Fitzmaurice
Gill, of Sydney Palace, will cease to be an Ameri-
can widow, and the Bank of England will have to
get out of its troubles as best it can without her.
It isn’t much of a piece that she has been playing,
ye.t the widow and the Sherlock Holmes, parts
gave Miss Gill and Charles Blake considerable
opportunities, and throughout their audiences have
appreciated their painstaking work. “ The Pro,-
digal Parsop,” alleged to be something not hitherto
uncorked in Australia, will be served up on
Saturday of this week.
** * *
Charles Holloway’s revival of “Harbor
Lights,” at Sydney Criterion, compares favorably
with the best of the Holloway season. It is
mounted elaborately ; part of the scenery was first
used by J. C. Williamson when he produced the
piece five years ago, and Ricketts has added to it
inhis best style. Aboard the Britannic, in the
third act, there is sword drill; this is the most
striking tableau of all, though the concluding
lighthouse scene is unusually effective and well-
managed. Beatrice Holloway is the girl whose
eyes are the harbor lights. She still talks of
“Meeses Smeeth’s beaby,” with whom she has
left the tobacco, but otherwise hers is more
than a creditable performance. Though for
most of the time it is love-making, there
are dramatic moments—when she finds that
she has been entrapped and compromised by the
weak squire, for instance; and on these occasions
the girlish actress shows more power than she
has done in anything hitherto. Vivian Edwards
opens the lieutenant-lover’s lines in a thunderous
voice ; but the gale moderates presently, and the
strain is eased. Charles Holloway makes the com-
paratively small part of the distracted Mark
Helstone conspicuous ; it is a fine little character
study. J. P. O’Neill rather overdoes Tom Dos-
siter, as usual. Miss Hellmrieh, excellent in
the subdued scenes, fails only as the cast-off Lena
Nelson during the dramatic encounter with Mor-
land. Frank Hawthorne makes a first appearance
with the co. in the last-named part, and helps
Godfrey Cass in the villainy; and Hesford,
Coughlan, King, and the others of the stock co.,
do work of good average quality. “A Bad
Character ” follows on Saturday.
*# * *
The end of the Sydney triumphs of Mar-
zella and her cockatoos, doves, ravens and eagles
is being hinted at, and if Harry Rickards weren’t
so modest a manager he would by now be advising
people to hurry up and not miss one of the
prettiest and most unique shows he has ever
introduced. The next attraction, to be added to
all the existing ones on Saturday, will be Miss
Jennie Grey, whose work at the London halls
struck manager Rickards, the first week after he
landed in London, as one of the things he and the
Tivoli wanted.
** * #
“The Favorite” was revived at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday with much elaborate
scenery, great crowds of supers., mobs of horses,
various weird “mechanical effects,” and all the
other features of Lyceum melodrama. As a play,
“ The Favorite ” has a good deal more coherency
and probability than some of the modern yellow
dramas ; it is not too obviously written around a
horserace or a railway accident, or a great fire
scene. Also it is thorough—in the melodramatio
sense. The characters are sawn off square from
the great aggregate mass of black villainy
or pure virtue. There are no lights and
shades, no between colors. The Yes-No attitude
on any point of ethics is barred. No personage
is allowed to trim ; he or she must start bad and
remain bad (if not become worse); or begin in the
paths of virtue and follow them steadily until the
sprouting angelic wings make knobs on the shoul-
ders. “The Favorite” has for its complete
villain Dr. York, whose crimes range from wallop-
ing the virtuous kid of the piece and attempting
to drown the minor heroine in a drainage cistern,
up to attempting to stiffen a horse. Its com-
pletely virtuous man is even more pronounced in
goodness than the wicked man in crime, for
he is a horse-trainer and yet honest! A
company, capable as to its leaders and numerous
as to its “supers,” presents “ The Favorite,” and
does it all the justice it deserves. Miss Eugenie
Duggan, favored this time with a virtuous part,
manages to divest herself of all the traces of past
stage-wickedness except the fine clothes. There
is, somehow, a suggestion of something wrong in
a virtuous lady in melodrama wearing gorgeous
raiment. Willard presents the villain very power-
fully, and Robert Inman is an honest horse-trainer
of great merit. Miss Gorrick and Mr. Dunbar
are others of a big caste that show out a little
from the ruck in the run home. “The Favorite”
will be continued next week.
“The Eternal City,” at Her Majesty’s
(Melb,), appeals to the artless disciples of Hall
Caine and Marie Corelli. It doesn’t threaten to
be as awfully successful as “Sign of the Cross,” but
ft might have rivalled that earlier Roman drama
in public estimation if Wilson Barrett had been
asked to dramatise Caine’s novel. Barrett could
have made a better job of it in all respects, and
to say this is to tell the whole truth about the
literary demerits of “The Eternal City.” The
author goes floundering out of his depth whilst
persisting in a most solemn pretence of
being a champion swimmer. His puppets
have a Family Herald unnaturalness, and
JOHN BEDE D ALLEY— the son of a great
Australian —now standing as the National
candidate for Wentworth (N.S.W.).
Photo, by The Talma Studios, Sydney.
they talk the baldest journalese at moments
which are meant to be thrilling. The interview
between the Pope and his long-lost offspring is
surpassingly twaddlesome, yet not weaker than
the lines in which his Holiness had previously dis-
closed his matrimonial secret to a dreary friar in
the Vatican garden. One notices many points of
absurdity throughout the drama. These papal
points are especially obvious because the action is
not rapid what time the holy father presides over
the scene. In a general way this is at least an
eventful, noisy drama, and the fact that an
invisible populace overthrows the local Govern-
ment should encourage the Melbourne proletariat
to go and do likewise.
* * * *
Nothing remarkable has happened to
“ The Great Millionaire,” at Melbourne Royal,
except the substitution of a tall brown-bearded
King Edward for the fragile little monarch who
wore the tin crown of Great Britain for the first
week or two. The new Edward is about eight
inches too tall and 20 years too young for the charac-
ter, but his gentlemanly Australian appearance,
combined with the inspiring strains of the National
Anthem, make a deep impression on all who can ap-
preciate a joke. The previous King, although too
funny for words, was a treasonable caricature of the
original E.R., whereas the present one provokes
hearty shrieks of laughter. Queen Alexandra
remains the same cheerful young person as before,
rather wanting in regal dignity, but eminently
calculated to make a large hole in the
Guildhall banquet—after the curtain falls.
Unfortunately the audience misses the pleasure
of seeing their majesties feed. There are,
however, two bona-fide refreshment scenes—firstly
when the stone-broke lordling sends out into the
street for a dozen guests, and bids them “scoff”
the remnants of his fortune, and, secondly, when
Walter Baker gives a supper to celebrate his
capture of the wheat market. Baker’s arrogant
heartlessness at this time of public distress is
getting him into bad odor with his old admirers.
They had not thought him a Baker who would
wilfully raise the price of the quartern loaf to five
shillings.
* #
One Cirnoc, a vigorous young fellow,
describing himself as Master of Locks and
King of Handcuffs, is the latest importation
at Rickards’ Opera House. Handcuffs, leg-
irons and other instruments of the law are
applied to Cirnoc, and Cirnoc demonstrates the
foolishness of such restrictive measures as regards
a man who can go behind a curtain and free
himself from his shackles in a mysterious way.
The trick is surprising in its results, but the
periods of Cirnoc’s seclusions are frequent and
dull. Also the number of criminals interested in
handcuffs and leg-irons is comparatively small,
and none of them can reasonably expect the
police to give them the opportunities that Cirnoc
enjoys.
* sf? * *
Melbourne’s appetite for circus has lost
its edge since Cup week, so the Fitzgerald Bros,
talk of taking the full strength of their co.,
including the elephant, away on another long tour.
Saturday is the last night of the slack-wire lady
who sheds her outer garments for the gratification
of the bald-headed men in the reserved seats.
Also the fluent citizen with the brawny arms will
cease from lifting the local carthorse on the same
date. The smellful leopard is about to change its
spot, and the place where the lion roars will be
wrapped in silence by Monday next. The Prin-
cess’s and the Bijou are dead to the world just at
present. The Circus is cutting its season short.
Melbourne must be suffering a relapse into inipe-
cuniosity.
* * * *
Melbourne Exhibition trustees last week
imported another big crocodile from Queensland.
Up to now the climate at the Exhibition has
always proved fatal to monster croc’s. ; they have
died of asthma or cold on the liver, and their
decease has been tedious and rather malodorous.
A crocodile in a strange land dies by inches, and
each inch smells somewhat.
# * * *
Adelaide Tivoli has had a week of a weird
extravaganza, “Ha ! Ha! ! Ha !!!” by Pat Finn-
Local amateurs worked hard at it, but puns in
every line rather weary the modern taste, and it is
a bad sign when the audience is smaller at the end
of the evening than at the beginning. Still, it ran
its week. A short season of Rickards is immi-
nent, with Marzella and her birds as the star
attraction. At the Royal a mesmerist opened
for the holiday-evening only, and, putting various
youths into an apparently genuine slumber, made
them bleed and otherwise suffer in a manner
which was accepted as highly humorous. At
present an American bioscope holds the stage, and
flickers surprisiugly little. Also, the fiery, un-
tamed dramatic amateur has emerged as is his
custom at slack seasons —and at various suburban
halls has attacked various plays, from “Merchant
of Venice” downwards.
vjj
Dan Barry has had a whole fortnight of
Hobart Royal at show time, just by a fluke.
Months ago, when small-pox was ravaging every-
thing in Flyspeck, Geacn told Dan that he had
engaged the house on the Derwent banks for a
couple of weeks, but couldn’t go there because of
the difficulty of getting away again. Barry said
he’d take the risk of small-pox. Time went on,
and Dan danced when he heard that Flyspeck
was wiped clean, and he’d have the Hobart Royal
all to himself then. Half Tasmania was in
Hobart for the show. Barry opened with “A
Gilded Sin ” and closed with “The Kelly Gang,”
and the middle pieces moved Wapping by their
very titles. Show night, manager Grant declared
he had spurned 400 people who asked standing-
room, and a lot who offered double price to be
allowed to hang on to a rafter. But Daniel’s
judgment at the end was against Hobart, with
costs, which weren’t paid.

“E.”: “Why this complaint about the
lack of Australian dramatists ? I know of at least
one Melbourne dramatist whose works are
published by himself and sold at about 9d. a
dozen to dealers—a price that brings them within
the reach of all. The author’s pen-name is
“Fergenham.” Bought one of his dramas for a
penny and found it most interesting. Title 4 4 The
Hermit’s Daughter,” a play in 14 scenes, the
characters ranging from “Kipple, a sheep station
owner ” (with an overseer, two boundary riders,
and other Australian supporters) down to the
more mystic character of “The Prince of Poverty.”
The drama starts in the sitting-room of a rich
citizen’s home, where some young ladies and a
young man, Deatrus, converse together :
Eda : Do you know, Deatrus, that we have only 20
minutes to meet that train ?
Deatrus : In 20 minutes all the world can change—
Rose : Their boots.
Deatrus : No ; not their boots, for some haven’t a
second pair to put on.
Rose : But even they can change their boots. Put
the right foot boot on the left foot, and the boot that’s
left on the right.
Deatrus : But what of them that have no boots at all ?
Then the Prince of Poverty invades the rich man’s
domicile—“dressed in golden and silver attire
and bearing in one hand two gleaming swords.”
No Australian dramatists !
THE ETERNAL CITY.
Ere Pius the Twelfth was enrolled as a priest
He suffered from weaknesses human ;
He married a wife, and the race was increas’t
By the aid of that willing young woman.
But they parted meanwhile, and to holier vows
He became a devoted subscriber
When he heard that the corpse of his sorrowful spouse
Had been brought to the morgue from the Tiber.
The years rolled along, and Pope Pius the Twelfth,
Looking thin on his Vatican tucker,
B,eheld a young man who had sought him, by stealth,
With a plea for protection and succour.
The stranger cried “ Father, I’m Julius Knight 1 ”
Then the Pope, much astonished to hear it,
Said—“ Come to my arms—let me cuddle you tight—
I’m your Father in flesh as in spirit.”
Thus two single fathers are merged into one,
But Julius, though he’s a bold ’un,
Could hardly presume, as a dutiful son,
To “ Dad ” the pontifical old ’un.
o,n Saint-days his parent he meekly address’t
As “ Pope,” which was fitting and proper,
And sometimes his filial love was express’t,
By the quaint exclamation of “ Popper.”

The Oathoart Proprietary G.M. Co. (Victoria)
has lately placed an order with the Austral Otis Company
of South Melbourne, for a complete electric lighting and
power plant, including also boilers, feed pump, surface
condenser and air pump. The new plant will furnish
sufficient electricity for motors to drive puddlers, the
winding engine and a three throw pump. The latter
being capable of lifting 26,000 gallons of water per hour
against a total head of 216 feet. The generating set will
consist of a vertical enclosed compound engine of 160
8.H.P., coupled direct to a Ganz three phase generator of
110 killowatts output. The electrical portion of the plant
will be carried out in the latest three phase system of
Ganz and Co., with 600 volts working pressure and a
frequency of 60 periods. We believe this is the first
instance in Victoria of an individual mine adopting
electricity as a motive power for pumping, hoisting and
puddling machinery, and no doubt when the plant is in
operation, which it will be in about six months’ time, a
number of other companies will follow the example of
the Oathcart Company.

* * * *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

Alleged present amount of Assurance Fund .. £15,764
No other existing Australian life assurance concern shows
such a remarkable record as this. The co., having paid
itself regular 10 per cent, dividends for some years, had
then an actuarial valuation made of its liabilities. Taking
in the Brisbane freehold at the figure at which it had so
far stood in the balance-sheet, a deficit of £3869 would
have been disclosed; but by writing up the freehold
£10,095, and by putting it in the balance sheet as an asset
for £24,000 instead of for £13,905, as previously, a paper
surplus of 16226 was created. A year or two later, by the
same charmingly simple process, £5500 more was added to
the company’s wealth. In the following year, however,
£5500 was written off; in other words, the property which
one year increased in value by about 20 per cent., in the
next year fell in value by exactly as much. But the
original £IO,OOO or so with which the Brisbane bidding
was papered still sticks —the freehold, included ori, inally
for £13,905, is still taken in for £24,500. (The odd
£SOO is apparently value legitimately’ added by improve-
ments made by the co.) This writing-up of assets one
year and writing them down the next; this conversion of
deficits into surpluses, of small surpluses into big ones—
all this may not be juggling, but it bears a strange re-
semblance to it.
No dividend is payable by the Australian Met-
ropolitan this year, or at least the directors recommend
that none shall be paid ; and that is the only cheerful
feature in the whole dreary landscape. The expense rate
is still preposterously high. Notwithstanding that no
dividend is allowed for this year, the outgoings, apart
from claims, amount to £BO 10s, 6d. per cent, of the total
income. This compares with past records in the fol-
lowing way’:—
Expenses and Dividends Compared with Total Income.
1897 .. £6O 13 2 per cent. 1901 .. £59 8 7 per cent.
1898 .. 69 3 9 „ „ 1902 .. 107 14 3 „ „
1899 .. 70 7 0 „ „ 1903 .. 80 10 6 „ ..
1900 .. 63 4 5 „ „
There has, it is true, been an increase in income of
£4578, which is presumably what the co. has to show
for its extraordinary expenditure in the previous year,
when £4751 had to be taken from the funds to meet
expenses and claims. Yet, in spite of that extraordinary
expenditure, which ought to have been followed by a
rush of business provided the co. had anything decent to
offer, it only added £976 to the funds this year. At that
rate it will take five years to return to the till the amount
which the co. had to take out last year in order to make
its splash. That is also assuming that the claims won’t
increase meantime. Here, anyway, is the position for
the last two years : Of the total income £9O 4s. Id. per
cent, has gone to pay canvassers, clerks, superintendents,
shareholders, and directors; and only £9 15s. lid. per
cent, has been left to cover the ever-increasing liabilities,
for every new premium means anew liability undertaken,
and every renewal premium represents an actuarial
increase in the amount of an existing liability.
The amount of insurance on the Metropolitan’s
books is not disclosed. It is stated, however, that the
premium income on policies in force on August 31st
was £19,682, and this seems to suggest, on a rough
estimate, that the total amount of the policies for which
the company is liable, is something approaching £400,000.
And apart from the £10,095 paper which it has plastered
on the Brisbane freehold, it has accumulated £5669
towards meeting the liability. On this basis, if all its
policy-holders dropped dead to-morrow it could pay
(exclusive of the paper beforementioned) very nearly 3Jd.
in the £. Even reckoning the paper as worth 20s. in the
£, it cbuld only pay 9d. in the £.
Nevertheless, the directors announced (Nov. 5,
1903)that “during the past year an actuarial investiga-
tion of the position of the co. as at 31st August, 1902, has
been made, with the result that the liability under every
policy in force at that date is provided for.” This may be
read to mean that, on Nov. 5 of this year, the liabilities as
at Aug. 31 of last year were “provided for”—in other
words, that this year’s premiums have had to go towards
providing for liabilities incurred prior to August 30 of last
year. The last previous actuarial investigation was made
as at August 31, 1900, when a surplus of £6227 was dis-
closed. Even if the assets (including the paper on the
Brisbane building) on August 31, 1902, were equal to the
liabilities at the same date, it looks as though the
alleged surplus had disappeared in the meantime. Any-
way, the statement is utterly unsatisfactory ; it doesn’t
disclose who made investigation, or what was the precise
result of it; and the annual report of a co. which is
touting for the savings of the people should be definite.
And this society “ has been placed upon the list
of offices with which employees may’ assure their lives, in
compliance with the Public Service regulations of the
Commonwealth of Australia ” ! Do the Commonwealth
officials exercise any discrimination at all, or do they
simply approve of every concern that applies, allowing
the concern to make what use it can of the imprimatur?

* * * *
It has been stated in N.S.W. Assembly that
the claim of Howard Smith and Cos. for £250,000, on
account of two wharves resumed by the State, was
settled, when (he case had made a little progress, by the
firm accepting £35,250. Naturally the public feels
interested. The co.’s last balance-sheet declared (and
the document was sworn to) that the value of all its
freehold and leasehold property in all parts of Australia
was £43,901. It has got £35,250 for two wharves in one
of the many ports where it trades. It asked £250,000 for
them—apparently, unless there is some explanation that
doesn’t yet appear, nearly six times the alleged value of
its whole freehold and leasehold properties. Did anybody
swear to that £250,000 valuation? Parliament should
insist on an explanation. The idea that discrepancies of
that size can go unexplained won’t help the State to get
fair terms in future resumption cases.

There are 80,000 cumulative preference shares, 20,000
non-cumulative (or deferred) preference shares, and
70,000 ordinaries, all £1 paid ; and. the strain of paying
heavy dividends on the preference shares, especially as
most of the capital is represented by goodwill and similar
items, has been too great to allow the ordinaries to draw
anything now that the bicycling boom isn’t what it was.
Still, though the bicycling boom isn’t what it was, the
Dunlop Co. has added new industries to the old one (it
has started the manufacture of solid rubber goods, for
instance), and so the turnover is greater now than ever.
The gross revenue has been as follows :
1900.. ..£41,286 I 1902 .. ..£37,883
1901 .. .. 36,413 I 1903 .. .. 47,681
Prospects, therefore, are very considerably improved,
though the future will depend very largely npon what
happens during the Commonwealth elections. In 1900-1
the co. spent £21,830 in wages, salaries, etc. ; in 1902-3
£30,377 ; and if Australia wants to lose that £30,377 as
well as all chances of an extension of the business, it
has only to vote for the party which prefers that
rubber and other goods shall be imported, and the wages,
salaries, &c., paid and spent in foreign countries. In the
Dunlop Co.’s figures at the head of this notice the
reserves are described as nominal. To get at the real
reserves it is necessary to deduct the merely paper assets,
but this can’t be done, owing to the fact that goodwill,
trademarks, and similar intangible items are bunched
with other property. Originally the composite item
represented £130,716, but this has now been written down
to £121,500; how much of it is represented by goodwill it
is impossible to tell. The co. is now working on a pretty
heavy overdraft (£24,556), whioh isn’t too healthy.
Apparently it needs more capital, yet unfortunately it
has to carry too much capital, represented by goodwill,
&c., already. To level things up the preference share-
holders might supply some ordinary capital.

And yet the Cape Colony working expenses per cent, of
earnings are higher than they are in either of the eastern
States—proof, to some extent anyway, that high railway
profits and low wages don’t necessarily run together. On
the other hand, low freights and excessive borrowing to
build “ developmental” lines are pretty certain to mean
big railway deficits and in the end low wages. The Cape
has borrowed about £23,200,000 in connection with its
railways; N.S.W.’s railway debt is approximately
£41,000,000. In other words, N.S.W. has to earn
£630,000 more than Cape Colony in order to pay interest
alone.

Westralian railway system is jerry-built, the depreciation
is particularly heavy.
* * * *
The interest on the accumulated deficits of the
Northern Territory (they amount to about £1,190,000)
now mop up all but some £7000 of the gross receipts.
One year’s expenditure (including interest) is equal to
three years’ revenue, and the annual deficit (about
£120,000) is equal to 2J years’ revenue.

The N.S.W. Miners’ Accident Relief Fond,
which began operations in 1901:—
Carried to Re-
Gross Claims. Expenses, serves for Un- Total
Income. expired Risks. Reserves.
1901.. £31,906 £6,821 £101 £24,984 £24,984
1902.. 39,036 11,096 1,546 26,394 51,378
The fund is provided, half by the workmen (every mine
employee is a contributor, the employer being required
to deduct 9d. per fortnight from his wages), and the
other half by the employers and the State, in equal por-
tions. Interest is also becoming an item of some im-
portance, amounting for last year to £1479. The large
increase in the expense rate is due to an amendment
of the law providing for the payment of the local
committees which, under a central board, administer the
fund. Many miners, seeing the rapid increase of
reserves, have urged that either contributions should
be reduced or benefits should be increased ; but, in view
of the fact that the fund provides for the payment of life
pensions in certain cases, it is much too early yet to say
that the contributions are too heavy. The following
figures, which show the amounts per cent, of the
employees’ contributions which have been required to
meet claims for each quarter since the fund began, go
still further to prove that no change can safely be based
on experience so far : —
March, 1901 .. 19’61 p.c. March, 1902 .. 39'09 p.c.
June, 1901 .. 27’08 ,, June, 1902 .. 43‘23 „
Sept., 1901 .. 36-5 „ Sept., 1902 .. 51'61 „
Dec., 1901 .. 37-29 „ Dec., 1902 .. 5771 .,
The funds are for the most part invested in N.S.W. stock.
The Public Actuary of S. Australia makes the
usual discovery with regard to the friendly societies of
that State : most of them are insolvent. Of them all
there is only one the present value of whose assets is
equal to the present value of its liabilities. That is to
say, there is only one which, in the ordinary course of
events, according to the actuary’s calculations, will be
able to pay 203. in the £. The trouble, of course, is that
the societies were founded on unsound principles, yet,
because claims were paid all right while the great
majority of members were young and vigorous, the con-
tributions were held to be sufficient; they had no thought
for the time when the young members would become
old, and sick, and death claims would pour in; and
some of them are not doing much to repair
the damage even now. The oldest of the orders
are, no doubt, meeting with the greatest.difficulty simply
because a greater proportion of their members are now
old, and incapable of paying higher rates, while the drain
in sick and funeral expenses is also naturally higher.
Among the larger societies the Grand and Manchester
sections of Oddfellows, the Foresters, the Druids, and the
Hibernians stand lowest on the list. All were pro-
jected with the best of intentions. They taught men to
deny themselves to-day for the sake of to-morrow. But
they had no eyes for the day after to-morrow, and the
weary day that is at the back of that.
• * * *
An alleged American society, according to an
S.M. Herald paragraph (which is proof, anyway, that
the thing isn’t a joke), gets over the difficulty which these
South Australian societies are bumping against. “ When
the number to be relieved is more than the funds can
provide for, then the dependents are thinned out by
suicide, and the one or more to commit suicide are
selected by ballot.” Probably, however, there would
be scruples against introducing this system in these parts ;
and certainly the alternative plan of making the contri-
butions a bit heavier at the outset, so as to obviate the
necessity of subsequent suicides, does seem to have its
advantages. * * *

* * * *
A most useful property is that English capitalist
of immense importance that nobody seems to have heard
of before, who always turns up at the right moment to
corroborate the morning papers, and tells them that,
unless the thing they object to is abolished, England will
lend no more money. This is the S.M. Herald’s latest:
“ Mr. J. Bevan Braithwaite, an English financier of
note, who arrived in Sydney on Saturday, spoke in strong
terms to a representative of the Herald of the condition
of our credit in England. He stated that the credit of
New South Wales was perfect so long as it was a Free-
trade State, but with Federation and the tariff, financiers
became shy. At the present time, he would not dream
of underwriting any loan from New South Wales or any
of the other States.”
Considering that N.S.W. never was a Freetrade State,
that when it called itself a Freetrade State it paid as
much as 5 per cent, interest, that heavily protected
Canada gets loans more cheaply than N.S.W. ever did,
and that Mr. Bevan Braithwaite doesn’t seem to be so
well known here as he might be, it is a fair thing to ask
for further information.
* * * *
After all the Tory shriek of joy that “ the suc-
cess of the Victorian £5,000,000 conversion loan ’’ was
solely owing to the resolute way the Irvine Government
trampled on “socialism,” it is interesting to hear that
Irvine, though he offered most lavish terms, only per-
suaded the old holders to renew £3,400,000. The public
took £500,000 at an exorbitant rate of interest, and at
time of writing Irvine was looking for £1,100,000 with the
eye of one who looks for ice in Sheol.
* * * *
A Sydney firm of auctioneers is offering for sale
a bit of old Sydney which is connected with many old-
time merchants, a four-storeyed store in De Mestre-place,
which is situated on the block between Hunter-street
and Martin-place. In the year 1830 there were but three
owners on the frontage of George-street between the
G.P.O. and Hunter-street—Riley on the corner of the
latter street, Prosper De Mestre in the centre, and
MacDonald on the southern end.

The co.’s balance-sheet gives very little information. The
principal asset is freehold property ; the original cost was
£179,513; from this £32,513 has now been written off,
leaving it at £147,000, and it carries a mortgage of
£72,000. In 1893 the mortgage stood at £IIO,OOO, so
a substantial reduction of £38,000 has been made in 10
years. The only other asset mentioned is “ amounts due
to the co., £95,534" —presumably advances. The only
test which can be applied to these is the amount of
the profits ; and these, for the past year, are equal to a
solid £7 13s. on shareholders’ funds as at the commence-
ment of the year, reckoning the reserves, of course, as
above. Probably, therefore, the advances are satisfac-
tory.
* * * *
Another fight in the coal trade is showing up in
the distance. Hitherto, the inter-State business has been
in the hands of the shipping and carrying co’s. —the
Adelaide, Howard Smith, Huddart Parker, and others.
Lately, however, J. and A. Brown, who own the Pelaw-
Main and other collieries, took steps to setup a coal-yard
of their own in Melbourne ; and other owners are follow-
ing that lead. Hitting back, cue of the carrying co.’s has
taken up coal-bearing land at South Maitland, and others
will probably follow. The coal-user will smile for a while,
but in the end he will probably have to hump the extra
weight of the capital put into the new and unnecessary
mines, wharves, ships, yards, &c. And the miner will
suffer, too, unless he has more luck than usual—though,
if owners deliberately set out to cut one another’s throats,
it isn’t certain how far the Arbitration Court will say
that the miners ought to bleed for it.
* * * *
South Australia finds out over again the
difference between a big crop at a small price and a
small crop at a big price. Last year it was selling its hay
up to £3 a ton ; this year, with no prospect of an export
market for chaff, it is selling good stuff for as little as 15s.

The innocent, but well-meaning, Melbourne
dailies cordially approved of the severity of a
Port Melbourne magistrate who lately inflicted
fines totalling £64 125., in default 37 months’ hard
labor, on four ruffians who had brutally assaulted
a publican, his wife, and a constable—the last was
THE GLAD COOK.
“ A Victorian poblican, prosecuted the other da y for having bad liquor on his premises, pleaded that it was
THE COOK:"'Sorl ri', bosh; 'sonly th' cookin' whiskee!"
...not much short of being kicked to death. The
maximum penalty was inflicted in each case, but
as-far as the ruffians' themselves were concerned,
they might almost as well have been acquitted.
Within four days friends had subscribed the full
amount and paid it in to the clerk of the court.
“Friends” are always available in such cases.
Some unfortunate female in the background has
to find the money for her “bloke” under pain of
being thrashed something extra when the party
concerned comes out ; also under pain of being
thrashed by proxy while he is in. To all intents
and purposes she might as well be fined in the
first place.
Some of the results of big landlordism in
Victoria. Take a 30-miles radius round Melbourne,
excluding the city itself (every place within seven
miles of the G.P.O. is here reckoned the city).
Gn the west is rich land, and big landlords who
grabbed it and shut off settlement; in that half
the population averages not quite 12J to the square
mile, and there are 19 State schools and post
offices. On the east there is poor land ; the big
landlords didn’t think it worth grabbing, so it fell
into the hands of small holders. The population
is 56j to the square mile, and there are 86 State
schools and post offices. It is only necessary to
break up those big holdings, and give the west of
Melbourne the same chance to accumulate popu-
lation as the east, and a good many thousands of
Victoria’s emigrating citizens would be accom-
modated.
• • * •
“ M.” : Much is being printed about the
sweating of parsons and curates. How about the
sweating of congregations by the parsons ? There s
an Anglican clergyman in a N.S.W. town I know
who probably hasn’t had a meal in his own house
since his arrival. He and his wife invite them-
selves out to breakfast, dinner, tea and supper.
And now and again they billet themselves on
members of the flock for a week or a fortnight at
a time. This condition of affairs has been going
on for months —but a revolution is fermenting.
* • * *
There are not Hebrews enough in Hobart
now to pay a Rabbi. There is a synagogue,
though, a relic of the days when Hobart had a
large Jewish population, and opposite the syna-
gogue is a pawnshop. There are only two pawn-
shops in Hobart.

“J.A.C.”: Be “ War-E.’s ” remarks on
the tobacco cadger (B. 29/10/’O3). I was seated
one day, in Hyde Bark, when I was joined by
one of the Honorable Guild. He was very
affable, and entered into conversation
regarding the weather and other absorb-
ing topics. Shortly, he wanted to
know if I had a pipeful of tobacco.
I had, and he got it. He then
told me that he had asked me
because he “ didn’t like asking
a stranger ! ” Evidently the two
or three remarks about the weather
had raised me to the status ofa friend.
insufferably tiresome “ temporarily
insane” verdict was lately replaced by “suicide
while suffering from mental depression,” at Wel-
lington (M.L.). There had been quite an epidemic
of self-made defuncts—nine in three months—and
the coroner said it was wholly improbable that
they were all mad. The man under review (who
had shaved too deeply with a razor) was, in fact, a
mo3t cheerful person, as evidenced by his last
letters. So the jury varied the old formula, though
it is by no means certain that it improved things
much. Going strictly by the evidence, the verdict
should have been “Suicide while apparently feel-
ing more glad than usual.”
The ridiculous precedence question, which
leads to two fat ecclesiastics being stuck publicly
in a narrow doorway, was virulent half-a-century
ago, when N.S.W. was a very young self-govern-
ing State. Then the Duke of Newcastle, the Joe
Chamberlain of the day, sent out a despatch
ordering that, on all public occasions, the clergy
of the Church of England should have precedence
of all other clergymen. Robertson kicked against
the dictation, and the subsequent row in Parlia-
ment ended in matters remaining as they were.
Many questions have been settled since then, but
the question as to which humble shepherd shall
go first has defied all settlement,

A religious paper says this :
Why are churches, as a rule—both Protestant and
Papal—so opposed to all proposals of sanitary reform and
evince such preference for unclean practices ? Take two
matters only. The strongest opposition sanitary reformers
encounter against the introduction of cremation, in lieu
of the unhealthy practice of placing dead bodies in and
around all our towns and cities, comes from the churches ;
and the strongest antagonism to the introduction of the
separate communion cup as against the use of the single
goblet comes from the same quarter. All institutions
seem to grow with the times except the churches.
Well, the churches started with the theory that
everything that it was necessary for a man
to know was written and finished some 1900
years ago, and every new discovery has been
an outrage on this theory. As for the
dirtiness of the churches, the churches live
to some extent on preventing or curing
disease by prayer, and the party which cures
or prevents disease by sanitary science is a
rival show. The Protestant churches have
not been so bad in this way, for they have
been washed to some extent by the civil law,
but the Papacy lost its temporal power
because there was no civil law to wash it, and
it wouldn’t wash itself. The Papacy was
certainly no worse than the average of its day
as a civil ruler—except that it was dirtier and
less scientific. If a first-class American en-
gineer had been elected Pope in 1846, and had
made the Papacy synonymous with a rebuilt
Rome, with good drains, good water-supply,
good roads, with railways, tramways, public
baths, cleanliness, and a general rush to get
manufactures started and big pork-canneries
put up, the Pope might to-day rule Italy
from the Alps to Sicily. There was a time
when a new drain and a soap factory might
have turned the scale, but the offal lay in
the streets till Victor Emanuel’s troops came
and cleaned up. No one can say now which
dead cat left in the gutters of old Rome was
the last straw, but there must have been some
such cat. Protestantism has been more for-
tunate. It never had a centre where it wielded
the temporal power, and where it was ob-
viously responsible for the dirt, and was
judged by the defunct cat in the gutter.
The girl who wrote this—a scholar in a
high-class N.S.W. school—will have a vote in a
year or two :
In England there was an important event going on
which is known as Free Trade. The people wanted this
Act to be passed so they would not have to pay too much
for goods to be brought from England to other countries
by ships so they thought it would be better to have a
Bridge or Canal put across the river and then they would,
be able to go and fetch the goods themselves and do away
with ships and so this was carried out and is known as
Free Trade.
“C.P.” : One generally expects discord
from the average amateur choir, but when two of
them get together, as happened in Bendigo (Vic.)
recently, things are likely to end in chaos. A
concert was organised to give the flagging
soldiers’ memorial fund a lift, and both choirs
were to take part. No. 1 got through its first
performance with great 'success, and retired to
criticise its enemies. But there was a conspiracy
in No. 2 organisation, and, after finishing its
number, it began to “Gor Save” with great
vigor, and the audience thought it was all over,
and, grabbing its hat, started for home instead
of waiting to hear No. 1 choir sing again. Then
the two conductors came forward and performed
a brilliant duet with a crescendo movement, in-
terrupted by the mayor fortissimo, and the town
clerk, and the meat inspector. Arguments
andante, allegretto, diminuendo, pianissimo, and
cussissimo, in 2-4, 6-8 and even time, were going
on all sides, and the police were just about to have
the Riot Act read when the lights went out and
saved the situation. Now the opinion of No. 1
choir about the place No. 2 choir will go to when
it dies consists mostly of brimstone and pitchfork.
• • • •
N.S.W. Government analyst Hamlet’s
evidence before the Parliamentary Select Com-
mittee on Preservatives in Food, the other oay ;
should make cheerful reading to those persons
who confine themselves to non-intoxicating liquors
for their health’s sake. He said that he had
known as much as 60 grains of salicylic acid in one
pint of colonial wine, while 23 grains were fre-
quent. The highest percentage of acid—as much
as 80 grains—was found in non-intoxicating wine,
labelled “Pure Grape Juice.” This wine was
used in churches for sacramental purposes. Also,
many temperance drinks contain d not much
short of 80 grains of salicylic acid per pint! No
wonder some teetotallers look sour.

to me that she deserves praise rather than any-

scape. If we can’t have a world full of real
dimpled Junoes and Psyches, let us endeavor to
cover up the fact. It is the most objectionable
feature of our sister of the W.C.T.U. that, as a
rule, she refuses to avail herself of aids to beauty
but prefers to walk abroad in her unredeemed
scrawniness. When Virtue starts to wear high-
heeled shoes, silk stockings, curly hair, etc., bin
will be knocked flat. Also it will stay flat.

MISJUDGED.
[FRO THE BULLETIN.]
via
Passed me in the crowd,
Dressed in something grey.
Thought me rather proud,
Would not look your way ?
Wore a stately dame
On my arm—what then ?
You would know her name ;
Cannot trust us men.
Oh, you make me laugh—
Why destroy your glove ?
Do not want the half
Of my fickle love ?
Yet that fair “ divine,” May
(Curb your passion hot),
Lady-love of mine, May,
Certainly is not.
Let me hold you so, pet,
Since I must explain ;
Kiss me soft and slow, pet,
Twice and once again.
So that stately fair, dear,
Shall not cloud your life—-
By my faith, I swear, dear,
She was but my wife.

The Bulletin
Unvarying

General Hutton still affects to misunder-
stand the attitude of the Australian Parliament
towards his department. (Perhaps he really does
misunderstand ; recent disclosures show the
average British officer capable of almost any
power of misunderstanding.) Speaking the other
night, General Hutton said :
Our unpreparedness for war had been well-known to
soldiers for many years. But the soldier had been set
aside. The civilian had triumphed, and the need i of
defence had been subordinated to political considerations.
That is very far from being the real situation.
General Hutton asked the Australian Parliament
for a huge sum of money for the fuss and feathers
of fashionable staff officers. Parliament, at the
instance of the Labor party, said “No,” and
insisted on shearing a lot of the boa, and tiie
guipure, and the insertion, and the silk-lining off
the military system. But the Labor party, all the
while, urged an increased expenditure on rifles.
The position really is that Gen. Hutton asked for
feathers and was offered guns. He says nothing
in reply about the guns but wails loudly over the
feathers. He has the housemaid’s idea —that there
is nothing like a rooster’s tail for sweeping an
enemy away.
♦ * * »
There was a lot of flummery about the
reception of the Governor-General and State
Governor at the review of the Victorian military
forces on King’s Birthday. It seems that at former
functions of the sort the State Governor had been
ignored. Little Clarke objected to this, and
thrust his chin higher in the air than usual. It
was arranged, therefore, that on the present
occasion His Ex. was to arrive by pure accident,
three minutes ahead of the Governor-General,
and be received with a salute. Then he had to go
and conceal himself behind a blade of grass while
the Governor-General arrived, and kept up the
pretence that no one else had been there before
him. He, too, received a salute. Then little
Clarke had to step out to the G.G.’s carriage as if
he had just dropped accidentally from heaven,
and again there were salutes for “both of them
together sitting side by side.” A great deal of
international complication would be saved by
having no State Governor.

“ S.” : The pervading desire to get some-
thing for nothing usually leads to getting Nothing
for a great deal. When a certain
stout gentleman, whose voice was
like the foghorn of a P. and O.
mail boat—Bent was his name,
if I remember rightly was
making a general mess of Vic-
toria’s great deficit department,
one of his economies, calcu-
lated to restore the country
to a sound financial position,
was to grant free rail tickets,
ranging from 12 months to
nine years, to anyone build-
ing a house in his own
electorate, or any one of
various other places.
Various houses were
slung together in a
hurry and got the con-
cession. Now every
‘‘To Let” in the
vicinity is advertised
as with “free rail-
way ticket.” The
something- for - noth-
ing man comes along,
takes a lease and
moves in, and in many
cases finds his free rail-
way ticket is for a
month only. It is an
ordinary season ticket,
paid for by the landlord.
The great free pass, good
for nine years or so, has, of course, been issued
to the landlord, and he isn’t going to part with
it. In some cases it is worth more than the
house.
* ♦ * *
Some of the Sydney suburbs experienced
an unusual sort of burglar, recently. What most
distinguished him from others of the Bill Sikes
fraternity was that lie would only touch hard
cash. His method was to enter the house per the
verandah post, go thoroughly through the pockets
and purses of the sleeping inmates, and then
depart, leaving untouched all jewellery or identi-
liable valuables. He seems to have had a charmed
life, and his assurance passed all understanding.
One householder was awakened by Sikes getting
through his window and straightway fired a shot
at him, whereupon he vanished. Ten minutes
later the disturbed shooter heard firing from
another house near by, and on cantering over to
give assistance, found that the same gentleman
had just deft hurriedly in advance of a couple of
bullets. The flurried citizen returned home, but
hadn’t been in bed ten minutes when he was
startled to discover the late intruder coining
through his window again. Though he made
bullets fly very suddenly, the thief got away as
safely as before. That neighborhood spent the
rest of the night on its respective front verandah
with a gun, and even then the amazing burglar
is said to have tried sundry back doors.

Malcolm M‘Eacharn’s amiable spouse made
a little speech to members of the Sex at the Pres-
byterian Schoolroom, North Melbourne, t’other
day. Mac. having introduced her to the company,
she meekly asserted the need for all women to
diligently and intelligently barrack for the “ best
men.” Mac., of course, was specially instanced
as the best man for North Melbourne, and all the
right-thinking ladies present were enrolled as a
committee. At the forthcoming election every
candidate on the wrong side of 40 will want a wife
in the field. If he can’t produce his own wife he
must borrow somebody else’s.

The death of an old woman in Melbourne
last week seems to have been helped on by
shocking indifference on the part of the hospital
authorities. The woman was 80 years of age and
had met with a burning accident. This is the
story of the constable who took her to the Mel-
bourne Hospital:
After waiting in the casualty-room for a few minutes he
asked if the doctor was coming, and the nurse said that
the doctors were at dinner. When the doctor came wit-
ness went outside, where deceased’s son told him that the
doctor could not admit deceased, as there was no bed.
Witness at first refused to take her away, and, having
discharged the cab, had to go and get another. When
they put deceased into the cab she was in great agony.
She was taken to the Homoeopathic Hospital, where she
was refused for want of room, and eventually she was
admitted to the Alfred Hospital.
The worst feature of the case is that Melbourne—
its sensibilities dulled by a long course of Irvine
administration—takes practically no notice of these
frequently-recurring hospital horrors.
Once more the greatest leniency has been
shown by a Victorian J udge to a person found
guilty of barbarous cruelty to a little child. A
woman, named Lilian Nancy Murray, of Drouin
South, was, on Sept. 23, convicted, before a police
magistrate, of wilfully ill-treating a girl under 14
years. This girl was her niece, and the woman
had two children of her own. The evidence
showed that she had beaten the girl with a stock-
whip, struck her on the head with a poker and a
piece of wood, and had cut her forehead open with
a teapot. The medical testimony was that the
child’s body was covered with bruises, her nose
had been beaten in, and there was a great scar on
her forehead, and she had a broken bone in the
wrist. The magistrate sentenced the woman to
three months’ imprisonment without the option.
There was an appeal to Judge Chomley, who
reduced the sentence to a fine of £5, because if she
were imprisoned her two children would be
deprived of her cai-e. Had this woman stolen a
bunch of Drouin South carrots from her neighbor
she would probably have got 18 months and a
severe lecture on the sacred rights of property
from worthy Judge Chomley, even if she had
22 children.
A recent Melbourne petitioner in divorce
put up quite a record as a long-suffering husband,
slow to anger and of gradual habit. He had been
married 14 years when, returning home one even-
ing, he found a certain Mr. Mann in the house.
There was “a bit of a scramble, and he walked
Mann out,” his wife following an hour or two
later. A week later he met the lady and Mann
taking a box to some lodgings. He took the box,
expecting the lady to follow, but miscalculated
somewhat.- A month later he visited her in ser-
vice, and once more desired her to return. She
refused. _ Then he took her to see her children,
and put it to her that if he was willing to forgive
she might surely accept his indulgence, but she
wasn’t taking any pardon business. A month or
two later he once more happened across her, with
Mann, and again urged her to cotne home and be
virtuous some more. He even, when she declined,
tried to put her in a cab and rush her away to
respectability, but a passing constable assured
him that the law wouldn’t allow any lady to be
violently rescued from wrong-doing in a. cab.
Then he gave the job best—and got his decree.

Judging by the columns of report of the
speeches at Melb. Mayor’s inaugural dinner, all
appropriately interlarded with “ laughter,” “ hear,
hears, ’ “cheers,” etc., one might almost imagine
that the real leaders of Australian public life and
thought were speaking; whereas, what were they ?
Two imported gubernatorial figure-heads, the
imported Admiral of a foreign fleet, a Japanese
Consul, an imported Major-General, and an
obscure State Premier Only plain George Turner
stood for Australia, and he stuck strictly to his
own business—finance—and enthused not at all
about the “ unity of the Empire.”

John Forrest brightened the platform of
Adelaide Town Hall on the occasion of his
speech there by inducing his wife and several
brightly dressed society ladies to occupy seats on
either side of the Mayor. They beamed down on
the audience in a moat patronising fashion and
courageously displayed their shapely ankles as
they reclined in their chairs during the two hours
oration. Well-turned ankles are a strong in-
fluence in politics.

A truly devilish blunder by the comp.
An American savant has been investigating
the effects of the eating of vegetables on the
human temperament. It appears that carrots
induce mildness of disposition, while the beet
creates and fosters jealousy. Consumers of this
tuber always imagine that the other party cannot
beet root to them.

Melbourne University and Museum will
both have an unexpected windfall through the
death of George M'Arthur, of Maldon (Vic.), who
suicided lately. M‘Arthur owned what is proba-
bly the best Australian collection of Burns’
editions, and these, it is said, go to the University
library, along with a number of old Bibles, illumi-
nated missals, &e. The Museum’s share will be
a collection of coins and swords.
CONTENT.
[FRO THE BULLETIN.]
Time has laid his sceptre by just for one sweet afternoon,
Glamorous gold the sleepy sunlight beams,
Willows bending touch the river, with a tremulous kiss
and quiver
Breathing softly through its sylvan dreams.
Rippling o’er the feathery grasses come the clover-
scented breezes,
Resting on my lips caressingly,
And a boundless, deep content, by the breath of heaven
lent,
Thrills slumbering heart and brain in harmony.
Love was false and I, despairing, dreamt not I would
cease from caring,
But the sun will be warm-hearted till the last,
And though all men were untrue, while the skies above
are blue ;
Why trouble with the Future or the Past ?
Where the graceful black swans glide on the river’s lazy
tide,
Where the willows with their weight of green are bent,
When my heart wakes up again to the outside wmid of
pain
To the grassy bank I’ll come and be content.
HELEN DOUGLAS.
LIFE.
[FRO THE BULLETIN.]
M.L.
I set my ships a-sailing
Upon the deep blue sea—
With tattered sail and broken mast,
Lo ! they returned to me.
I sent my heart a-fighting
The battle of the free —
Bub wounded with a mortal wound
Came my heart back to me.
I sent a prayer to Heaven
(If such a place there be).
Like ships and heart, will my last prayer
Return, some day, to me ?
W. FRANCIS CHAMBERS.
LIFE AND SONG.
[FRO THE BULLETIN.]
If Life and Song were one—if noble deed
Could ring as sweet as some dear charmed reed,
If Life around were all a blossomed dell
Where Song went pealing like a silver bell,
What tides of Joy unseen might surge and swell
If Life and Song were one?
If every clanging discord softly died,
If loving heart and hand harmonious plied,
If all our days were shot, like silver shine,
With love and hope and yearning all divine,
What perfect peace might clasp our souls and twine,
If Life and Song were one ?
If every soul could see the light afar,
If every breast could hurst its prison bar,
If every goal to which our hearts aspire
Were nobly great and pure of mortal mire,
What seraph band could match our earthly choir,
If Life and Song were one ?
Q.
P.L.
