A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 17, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini.—
In the chromatic dusk of State Government
House drawing-room was held, last Friday after-
noon, a meeting to discuss ways for raising funds
to increase the accommodation of the two con-
sumptive sanatoria in this State. It was officially
a Mayoress’ meeting convened by Lady Rawson,
but bis albatross Excellency, Drs. Sydney
Jones and Purser, and Mr. T. A. Dibbs did the
work. The facts which these gentlemen set
before the thirty odd ladies were interesting and
saddening. Tuberculosisisapreventibledisease, yet
4000 persons die of it annually in Australia and
16,000 persons are now suffering from it. There
are only 62 beds available for patients at
Thirlmere and Wentworth Falls (inclusive). There
is no provision for women at Wentworth
Falls; it is found that four men apply as
against one woman, and as the man is usually the
breadwinner, he is given the preference. The
sanatoria cannot keep up steam unless they get
more gold to put into their bunkers, and the
Excellent Sea-Bird, and the two doctors, and the
banker, and Mr. James Fairfax told the women
that they would have to do something in the way
of stoking. From £75 to £BO a year would
support a bed. If each municipality would raise
£lOOO the interest on it would provide a perpetual
endowment, and the bed could be called after the
various municipalities. There are now chances
for the suburban mayoress to walk into the fore-
ground of the societyscape, to say nothing of
increased chances of walking in the fields of
amaranth in the Great Hereafter.
Mr. T. A. Dibbs, with his early Roman front
and curly top-knot, arose and spoke fearfully of
the possibility of inroads being made upon the
sanatoria capital-fund of £17,000, which is at
present bringing in an income of £5OO or so.
Then he added, “I’ll tell you what I’ll do, I’ll
give £lOOO to endow a bed—it can be called after
me.” And he sat down amidst considerable
applause.
His Ex. made a valuable suggestion that the
accommodation at Wentworth Falls might be
increased right off by means of tents. Mr. Fair-
fax moved for a ball at Sydney Town Hall early
in May, 1904, to raise money to augment the
Queen Victoria Home for Consumptives Fund.
And a list as long as Cranbrook drawing-room of
proposed committee women was read out.
As well as the ball and the suburban subscrip-
tions the trustees might well decide upon a flower
feast for next year. It could be held in Septem-
ber or October (our floweriest months) at the
Cricket Ground. I mentioned the kind of enter-
tainment a few weeks ago. Roses, roses, and
music all the way, good-looking girls selling
button-holes, and tea, and leaflets of verse about
waratahs and wattles and Spring in Australia, and
jugs of wine, and loaves of bread, and things
written by local poets, such as Victor Daley,
Roderick Quinn —oh yes, and Major Neild.
Sydney girls, even those outside the Went-
worth electorate, are taking considerable interest
in the campaigns of the two candidates for that
borough—Messrs. Dailey and Kelly. Both are
unmarried young men. Mr. Kelly has the advan-
tage of possessing a motor car, and he is doing a
lot of his wooing by taking out the feminine
electors (at the rate of five loads a day) for rides
in his 40-mule power car. Poor “Jack” Dailey
has no motor-car, and will have to get in oq, his
merits.
It would not be a feeble idea for the ladies who
are helping our flannel-flower candidates, to get
themselves dressed after the fashion of the six
handsome damsels who come on in “ A Country
Girl” to help their candidate jump through the
political hoop. These six are real pansies, and a
view of their gowns—dull rose (one with a wide
green belt that makes a most huggable curve all
round her equator) heliotrope, baby-leaf green,
and brown, the shade of a Gordon setter’s paws—
is remembered longer than most women remember
a beautiful sunset. Miss Claire Berry, who has
the speaking part of the six, gets to the inner
circle of style with her first gown of black net
embroidered with black chenille poppies, and cut
steel, over a robe of white crspe-de-chine. A big
white hat with black ostrich feathers (a whole
bird’s wing is on that hat) is worn at an angle
that would fascinate into a Protectionist the most
bigoted of Foreign-traders.
Did you see that the Prince of Wales has been
pleased to accept the appointment of Colonsl-in-
Chief to the Australian Light Horse ? The little
man is about 18 colonels, and he should—to be
'air to every one of the 18 regiments—wear his
18 uniforms. It would be a nice compliment,
which would be, to quote the words of a local
gush telegram concerning this appointment to the
King, “received with enthusiasm by the military
forces and people of Australia ” —and other
peoples too. He would be a beautifully cosmo-
politan jackdaw in all his uniforms.
A curious bundle of typewritten matter —letters,
circulars and excerpts from papers—comes to me
from “ Hickman and Co.” Mr. Hickman is the
father of the woman doctor whose disappearance
from her duties at a London hospital caused such
excitement. The subsequent finding of her dead
body, the inquest and verdict of suicide are now
things of the past. But these letters and circulars
were sent out whilst the lady’s fate was still a
mystery. The bereaved father harps upon his
daughter’s physical and mental strength, her
balanced intellect, freedom from sentimentality,
and dwells upon her “passionate” love for her
family and friends, and goes on to say that it is
quite possible that, overwhelmed with the re-
sponsibilities of a resident-surgeon at a large
hospital—she disappeared the very first day that
she entered on her duties there —her balance of
mind may have been upset and a loss of memory
may have caused her to wander.
Then a curious sectarian element creeps into
che heap of literature. Mr. Hickman points out
that owing to the great increase in the number of
convents in England the chance of his daughter
being ‘‘detained” —why any convent should seek
to detain her isn’t explained—is much greater
than if there were less convents, and he goes
largely into statistics. In a purely arithmetical
way, his view seems logical enough ; the increase
of buildings of any kind doubtless increases the
chance of anybody being in a building of some
kind. Still, the implied theory that the lady was
in 685 convents seems to suggest that she was
strangely numerous. In the end her body,
which showed traces of poison, was found
in a suburban park. Her death, apparently
the indirect result of the “overwhelming responsi-
bility ”, of the hospital, will be a boulder in the
path of women doctors who aspire to such
responsibilities in future. By the way, an Aus-
tralian lady doctor, who was once put in charge
of a hospital, signalised her promotion by getting
a bad attack of hysterics when her first serious
case had to be treated.
Fresh-Air Leaguers want more money. Last
year was a very lean year, because of the drought,
so they are holding a floral fete and concert, with
side-shows, in Sydney Town Hall, on Wednesday,
25th inst.
Students’ Operatic, Oratorio, and Dramatic
Society (Modesty, thy name is a synonym for
amateur), gave its first performance in Grand
Opera, at the Royal (Sydney), last week. As the
students hadn’t had sufficient rehearsals, they were
not so bad as they might have been. One of them
missed only eight lines out of one song. There
are some beautiful voices among them. Miss
Elsie Dwyer, a 16-year-old Bendigo girl, took the
part of Marguerite (“ Faust ”), and most agree-
ably sustained the role in voice and action. Miss
Carrie Lancelley as Senta (“ Flying Dutchman ”)
was also a very good girl. Want of accord
between singers and orchestra almost made comic
opera of the grand excerpts. Yet the orchestra
contained some of the best men in the Musicians’
Society. Perhaps it was . But no, such a
thing is impossible. On the other hand, it may
have been .
Students’ next appearance will be in a Shak-
spearian play, to be rehearsed and produced under
the direction of Mr. Laurence Campbell.
A sum of £l2OO is .dozing somewhere, waiting
to be spent in decorating the pallid interior of
our Town Hall. The artists might paint a frieze
of, say, laughing jackasses and waratahs round
the walls for that sum ; it wouldn’t run to much
more.
The wonders of up-to-the-minute surgery ! We
have all heard, but have lent sceptical ears to,
the stories of how faces may almost be born
.again by, the remodelling of certain features.
Those of us who believed the stories thought that
if we wished to have a feature chiselled down or
levelled up we would have to go hence—to Paris
for choice—to secure the operation. Well, the
face-values of the defective can be improved right
here in Sydney. The instances are very few,
because so few persons know that the men and
the means for the improvement are in their
midst. No advts. come from inside the sacred
aura that surrounds the British Medical Associa-
tion. Met a girl last week whom I had not seen
for two years. Then she carried one of the most
defective of noses—it was a deformity—con-
cave across the middle, with the tip jutting out
and upwards. It is now a nice normal straight
nose. Something which she described as paraffin
wax in a warm, fluid state was injected under the
skin of the faulty part and modelled into shape as
it hardened. A local doctor performed the ap-
parent wonder. He had also given a girl’s
brother, so she said, more chin. “Also, ”
(naming a well-known lady whose friends are in-
nocently imagining her increased weight to be
due to lapping cream) “has been to him to have
the ‘ salt-cellars ’ in her neck filled out.”
As Mr. Haddon Chambers is an Australian, his
doings as a playwright are some slight interest to
us down under. His play, “The Golden Silence,”
lately produced in London, is said to be a play
written round one player—Miss Irene Vanbrugh.
There is little of nature in it, but mountains of
unrealities. A certain sculptor is the honorable
lover of a wealthy, married countess. His ardor
cools when this lady announces her intention to
get a divorce, and he remembers that a sweet girl
is pining for him. But he won’t propose to the
S.G. because she is so rich. The worn-thread-
bare device of making the heiress pretend to be
poor is adopted, and she wins him. There is a
cake-walk and a gramophone in one scene) and a
really funny interlude, in which a foolish baronet
announces his engagement to a music-hall artiste,
over the telephone, to his mother. It reads
curiously to learn that “the author had a hostile
reception.” One wonders why—for the critics
seem to agree that the play is decent in tone, and
never dull.
Mr. J. 0. Williamson left Sydney last Sunday
en route for London. He took with him as secre-
tary Mr. Monty Grover, formerly of the Argus
staff.
If there are any women agricultural students at
the Australian agricultural colleges, they may be
interested to know that Lady Warwick formally
announces the inauguration in England of the
“College of the Daughters of Ceres.” That’s
poetical enough for the most romantic. During
the last five years 225 women students have quali-
fied in dairy-work, poultry-farming, bee-keeping,
horticulture, and the marketing of produce. The
chief college degree will probably be D.D.—-
Doctor of Digging. There is a great demand,
especially from Canada, for these Lady War-
wickers. The j ust establi shed college is at Studley
Castle, with its park of 340 acres. A number of
women, old, middle-aged and quite young, went
to Mildura, the irrigation settlement on the
Murray, in its early days, to grow vines, apricots,
oranges and lemons, and rear the bee all on their
own without male aid. On paper —the Chaffey
paper—the scheme looked idyllic. But in practice
the heat, or the lack of male aid, or the prose of
the life coupled with the way the lemon and
orange declined to be bustled into bearing, spoilt
the idyll for them. Very few, if any, women were
there on their own at the end of seven years.
Hibernian Society of N.S.W. is to send a bunch
of our native flowers to Lord Hopetoun, frozen in
a block of ice. Frozen blooms are a great failure.
While in their iceberg they look a mass of colored
rags seen through a dirty window-pane, and when
thawed out they are pappy and half-decomposed.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“These meetings to instruct women re their
political duties are not bad fun. All the men
I have happened to hear in the role of
instructors, are desperately conservative. They
say that, of course, they do not presume to dictate
to male or female how they should vote, but there
are ‘ a few points’ they will just refer to. Then
they proceed to throw out dark hints about
‘interfering with the individual.’ Scribe
hears of a woman, who,- puzzled by said
vague phrase, whispered to her ‘crony’
‘ Who does he mean by the individual ?' Crony
whispered baok ‘ I don’t know, but I fancy
he must mean fast women—you know what I
mean—it seems those sort of characters are trying
to monopolise the vote.’ ‘Oh yes, I suppose the
police are going to interfere,’ rejoined the first
speaker, placidly. Then the orator goes on to
warn woman against ‘Labor/ saying that ‘every
candidate should represent all classes, not Labor in
particular.’ Well-dressed woman approves of this
sentiment, not being much attached to Labor. A
Labor member in the audience shouts, ‘ Why, it
was Labor gave woman her vote.’ He is severely
sat upon by the ‘instructor,’ and told that
‘Woman has come here to be instructed, not
insulted.’
“There’s a ring of sincerity in the regrets
expressed regarding the going of the Sydenham
Clarkes. Stonington has been generous and
industrious in entertaining; almost every Sun-
day there was a rally in the big room for
pleasant little concerts. Chinny, too, has been
the busiest, readiest, most conscientious little Ex.
the State has known for a long time. He was
quite unequal to any kind of brilliancy, but he
was a serviceable little man, sincere—and prac-
tical, despite his art and music dilletantism.
“ One of the most chronicled frockers of the Cup
carnival was the youthful Countess de Rougemont,
a resplendent visitor to these shores. The showiest
of her turn-outs was a crimson and white foulard
hung in places with lace, with a crimson hat
depending for its smartness on the cunning
arrangement of its fiery-hued flowers.
“Collins-street, these days, knows a queer
sight in the fashion adopted by a Variety artiste
right from the land of the Burnt Nigger—skirt
short and so tight as to hint at the action of the
knee-joints in her perambulations, a close-fitting
coat over a plump bust and a sombrero skewered
to a compact brown thatch.
“ Those society girls who essayed to obtain a bit
of verse or a characteristic quote from Count Vay
de Vaya were not successful. A few favored
albums contain the mere signature of the Mon-
signor, nothing more. He could not be induced
to leave any sentimental tag to his most precise
signature. He was altogether a curious cleric—
no collection plate and no trite maxims to scatter
around.
“Mr. Carlyle Smythe was married at St.
Barnabas’ Church, Balwyn, on Wednesday last,
to Mrs. Bode—a very youthful widow—who was
born in Maoriland, educated in Germany, dwelt
awhile in Switzerland, and was hard at work
polishing her musical top notes for the concert
platform when Mr. Smythe and matrimony inter-
fered. The bride is said to be really able to enjoy
the Argus articles in which her husband spreads
his lore and his views of Art. St. Barnabas’, by
the way, has in the north wall a stained glass win-
dow in memory of Andrew Murray, who edited
the Argus what time the much-travelled Smythe
was writing, in the fashion of his son to-day,
dramatic and Art notices for the Melbourne
Herald, then an emphatic morning journal.
“Of the townspeople who profited by the Cup
visitors few reaped such a bountiful harvest as
one fashionable thatch-designer in Collins-street.
With five assistants she was kept going with
crimp and curl and coil the whole Cup week from
dawn till an advanced hour of gaslight. Not
having 30 hours for the hundred and one who
wanted to be thatched in style on Cup morning,
she had to resort to the device of dressing the
heads the day before—so that half of fashionable
Melbourne slept the night before the battle in its
beautifully brilliantined coils.
“ Lady Tennyson’s voice rang valiantly through
the Town Hall at the opening of the well-
engineered District Nursing bazaar, the other
day. It wasn’t the usual vacuous opening speech
with a smile and a so-glad-to-be-here assurance.
The practical Excellency fired off a column of
comparative figures in evidence of the extent and
work of the District Society in England and here-
abouts.
“The Countess of Darnley (formerly Mis*
Florence Morphy, of Echuca) opened the Fair,
second day, with much self-possession and quite a
champion little speech. The returned Victorian
has altered very little from the dashing girl with
the fine eyes who captured the then Hon. Ivo
Bligh, when he brought out his cricket team.
Her bazaar-attire of ivory-silk and handsome
lace was topped by drooping burnt-straw hat,
with wreath of pinky-purple fuschias, same
color appearing in waist ribbon, leading to crude
clashing of hues when a presentation posy of
crimson carnations was added to the picture.
The Cliveden widow, in cream oolor and. many
artificial violets, tripped round the stalls with her
kinswoman, the Countess, who (like many other
local belles who have married ‘ well ’) was
staying at the Clarkes when she captured her
cricket celebrity.
“There was a sprinkling of Society people on
the railway platform the other day when Bigjohn
Forrest and his dame set forth by the Adelaide
express. All the nice young men who had taken
part in the Forrest minuet class with the smart
girls nf Toorak brought along their offerings of
Beaucaire roses and carnations. It was quite
affecting. It’s a pity the painstaking dame hasn’t
a baker’s dozen of daughters to settle. Perhaps,
though, the absence of such family cares accounts
for the attention the lady receives from the
youths. Generally speaking, the Society mamma
is a horror to young Adolphus, who detects
ulterior motives in her ingratiating smiles.
“Miss Muriel Rich, theßrisbane visitor, whowon
admiration during recent Melbourne festivities,
was bound to be a beauty if heredity counts for
anything. Her mother, the wife of a former
Queensland P.M., was and is a gracious and
charming lady. Mrs. Rich’s father was an army
captain, who sailed for Queensland in the long
ago with his wife and their family of two hand-
some daughters and two sons (Stanley and
Willie Hill). The debonair brethren entered the
Bananaland Government service, one ultimately
becoming a P.M., while his brother (C.P.S.
at Rockhampton) made money in Mount Morgan
and went to England to buy back and settle
down on an estate, formerly owned by his for-
bears. On the voyage to Australia the Hill
sisters both became engaged to young sprigs
of good family (Messrs. Rich and Eden), whom
they subsequently married. But the outward-
bound journey, which began brightly, ended
sadly for the Hill family. Nearing Bananaland,
there was a death on board, and a boat party was
told off to bury the body on a neighboring island.
Captain Hill went with the party to read the
burial service. Somehow, the boat lost sight of
the ship, and was adrift on tropic seas for days,
her inmates suffering agonies from heat and thirst.
When rescued Captain Hill was stone blind, and
never recovered his sight.
“The Deakins have chosen a home far from
the jingle of tram-bells, the clamor of street
criers, the creak and the roar of traffic. Down at
the river-side end of one of the narrowest thorough-
fares in South Yarra, and elbowed by two close,
reserved-looking houses, the tall, secretive, iiigh-
walled residence of the Deakins is wedged. The
outer wall is formidable, and the massive gate,
which doesn’t allow a chink for a peeper, is locked
from within. A tote-shop could not be better
protected, nor a private distillery, nor a racing
favorite. A ring at the wall-bell brings
a maid. Then all the reserve vanishes. A small
garden—-where one of the tall Deakin girls dallies
with a book on music—is crossed, and then the
solid, compact, tailor-built style of a house, is
opened unto you. I happened on a charming
domestic picture. In the large, cool, uncrowded
drawing-room the wife of the Prime Minister was
seated at the piano, playing and marking time
aloud, while a very pretty silken-haired girleen,
the youngest daughter of the house, bent,
thoroughly absorbed, over a ’cello. No need for
Mrs. Deakin, in subsequent chat, to tell you
she is no society whirler. All her interest lies in
her family and her home. ‘ I don’t wear
dainty petticoats,’ laughed the matron, ‘and I
wear plain clothes, but in variably the papers put
me in something different —very often something
nicer. You see, I’m not known. I only go where
I absolutely must! ’ And she laughs the matter
off. ‘ Did you see how magnificently my husband
is being treated in Brisbane ? . Isn’t it great ? He
tells me of it all in a letter this morning. He only
wishes I could have a peep at some of the demon-
strations.’ And when at last you leave this tall,
shy, devoted woman it is with good wholesome
impressions of a cultured home.
“A dainty little Melbourne recitress has fallen
on her feet. A wealthy and childless Society
matron has taken her into the fold and threatens
to adopt her. That’s the sort of gift a recitress
should have. To draw rich ladies in this fashion
is better than to rouse an audience (personally
unconcerned about the fair one’s elocuting) to
delight. As a rule, the she-reciter has a hard
time in Melbourne. ‘At-home’ hostesses don’t
go out into the highways and by-ways searching
for her, and only at a rare suburban concert is her
art in demand. Private lessons seem to be the
only way to gather in grist.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Worker suggests that “Knight of
St. Grogery ” would be a better title.
Died in London, John Greenlaw Foxton,
aged 93, father of Queensland’s ex-Minister.
A girl aged 13 gave birth to a child,
t’other day, at the Adelaide (S.A.) maternity
home.
Zeb. Lane, father of the better-known
Zeb. of Westralia, is still, at 74, managing the
Lord Nelson, the principal mine of St. Arnaud
(Vic.). He passed the 74th year post on Nov. 5.
Three Federal members have died since
the opening of the Commonwealth Parliament —
Sargood (’Vic.), W. H. Groom (Q.), and Piesse
(Tas.). Dickson, a member of the Cabinet, died
before the elections.
The yam is revived in Adelaide that
Premier Jenkins Iscariot will shortly succeed
Grainger as Agent-General. In Fogtown Jenkins
will be an American foreigner; his naturalisation
doesn’t reach that far.
Said that “ Elijah ” Dowie is having a
residence built at Mt. Lofty (S.A.), the rich
man’s summer resort, about 20 miles from Ade-
laide. A yarn that he will fly up and down to
Adelaide every day is probably incorrect.
Bishop Welldon, of Calcutta, who visits
Australia, is head of a diocese that once included
the whole area of the Commonwealth. The first
C.E. clergymen in Botany Bay were subject to
the jurisdiction of the Bishop of Calcutta.
Cabled that Cardinal Moran, before leaving
Rome, asked to be allowed to settle in Italy, but
his Holiness hustled him back to where he came
from. It seems a pity. A less combustible
cardinal would have done well enough for Aus-
tralia.
King O’Malley, M.H.R. : “If any hard-
faced, ungaoled scoundrel should circulate slanders
about me on the eve of election, when too late to
reply, vote for me just the same, and if I fail to
1 ind him in gaol or the Bankruptcy Court, I shall
resign.”

This quaint advertisement appeared
among the public notices of Adelaide Advertiser
of 29/10/’O3:
IF A. Fisher attempts to assault or insult me while
my collarbone is unwell, I will not be responsible
for my actions.
John F. Condon.
N.S.W. Parliamentary candidates seem
determined to let no chance slip. One of them,
the other day, obtained three names to a requisi-
tion inviting him to stand, by assuring the owners,
separately, that each would carry off the first
prize in the current hen-laying competition at
Hawkesbury College.
The freshest-looking member in N.S.W.
Assembly during that recent all-night sitting was
Pa Brunker. At 4 a.m., when cart-horse young-
sters tossed about with furrowed brows and
bloodshot eyes, the venerable cow-auctioneer held
up a face of cherubic satisfaction. He was the
only living thing in the Chamber, barring “Larry,”
worth framing.
Last week saw the final departure of a
number of old identities. The widow of Edward
Knox, of the Colonial Sugar Refining Co., aged
82 ; Vincent Dowling, pastoralist; William Kil-
gour, of Dalgety's; John Lackey, aged 73; Pres-
byterian parson M’Kinnon ; Count de Rossi,
aged 89; and old Charles Nicholson, within five
years of his century.
Some birthdays of this month : President
Stirling, M.L.C. (S.A.), 60 on the sth ; Governor-
General Northcote, 57, and Representative
M’Millan, 53, on the 14th; Sailor Guthrie,
M.L.C. (S.A.), 45 on the 17th ; Archbishop
O’Reily (S. A.), 57 on the 19th ; Senator Dobson,
62 on the 24th ; Senator Tom Playford, 66 on the
25th; Mark Twain, 68 on the 30th.
“ F.” : At 84 years of age explorer A. C.
Gregory has been made a K.C.M.G. After a wait
of 30 years the old man could have done without
it. Apropos. The Sydney Lands Office niches
were reserved for statues of explorers by land and
sea, but some are empty and some contain stone
politicians—Parkes, Martin, Farnell, Deniehy,
&c. Surely there is a niche for Gregory ?
Joseph Winter, of Melb. Advocate, and
brother Sam, of the Herald, have returned from
their trip to the land of the Mikado. As a result
of the visit the Advocate is appearing with
photographs of Joseph being carried round the
country in rickshaws and other glorified peram-
bulators. He has the modesty not to disclose his
identity to the public. Not so diffident was Vic.
Judge Casey, who did some globe-trotting in his
time. On one occasion, when delivering a lecture
before a Melbourne literary society, he exhibited
a limelight view of the Pyramids. It was not so
much a picture of the Pyramids, however, as of
J udge Casey perched on a camel with the Pyra-
luids as a background. A badly-suppressed snig-
showed that the audience saw the point.
John B. Dailey— the National candidate for
Wentworth (N.S.W.) constituency in the Austra
lian Parliament —is the one son of the great W. B.
Dailey who recalls his father. Though young
yet, and new to public life, he promises to develop
on the same lines. His success, which is pro-
bable, will restore to Australian politics an historic
name, and give the national cause an earnest
advocate.
John Aitken Manton, who died the other
day at Woollahra (N.S.W.), aged 70, was the son
of Fredk. Manton, a Vic. pastoralist as far back
as ’39, and grandson of Joseph Manton of
“rifling” improvement fame. J. A. Manton,
with his brothers, erected the first Chilian quartz
mill at Maldon (Vic.) in ’53, and in ’6O discovered
the Peak Downs copper mine (N.Q.) which paid
£250,000 in divs.
E. T. Smith, South Australia’s brewer-
knight, who lately celebrated the 50th anniversary
of his landing at Port Adelaide, has been a city
mayor five times and a suburban mayor five
times, and served 30 years in Parliament. Also,
he was twice a Minister under Jaunty John Bray
—once for a month and once for two months.
But almost every old man in S.A. has been a
Minister at some time or other.
Said that Daniel Cooper, senior, deposited
£40,000 to guarantee the up-keep of the baronetcy,
Saul Samuel £30,000, and William Clarke
£lOO,OOO. England won’t give a new hereditary
title to any man who cannot make it quite sure
that his son, at least, will have some cash. The
grandson or great-grandson may be a Gibeonite
shoving a wheelbarrow, but no Australian
baronetcy is old enough for that yet.
“ M.”: The most intelligent democrat
out for the Tasmanian elections for the Senate
is Mac Master, the journalist who is nominated
in the Labor interest by the southern leagues.
Mac Master talked to a shocked crowd of
Hobart’s most fashionable women the other
evening, and his text was: “The Labor party
is going to win, but only after an arduous
struggle; and those who enlist now have the
stern delight of taking part in the battle of men
against money.” As a Laborite, Mae’s ideals are
broad. He admits two sections of society : the
Laborer and the Loafer, and he has no good to
say of the Loafer.
One representative citizen of Mel-
bourne got a considerable shock through
the Argus’ patent idea of finding out
whether its readers’ names are on the rolls
or not. It was very little trouble to go to
the post-office and look at the list himself,
but it was less trouble to let the Argus do it
for him. The Argus clerk replied that his
remarkable and unmistakable name of Beriah
Plug was on the roll for Toorak and also for
one of the outer suburbs, giving both
addresses. He shuddered when he saw them.
Of course, it wasn’t Flossie’s fault; the
suburban cottage was rented in his name,
and his little friend had naturally given
him as the occupier. But the awful thing
is that his wife is a shining light of a Woman’s
League, which is always pawing the rolls,
and now he daily prays to all his gods to
direct her eyes away from that column ; for
the name of Beriah Plug isn’t one that can
be explained away, and he is by nature calm
and stolid and hates rows. Besides, Flossie
wouldn’t approve of his wife.
Adelaide has its best-known identity
in paralysed Patsy Weldon, and wonderful
are the tales woven round him. One is that
he owns a lot of valuable city property ;
another, that he can afford to send two of his
sons to an expensive college. Folk have even
made a romance out of his marriage, and
yet there are those that say Patsy is a
bachelor. There is another story that he is
just as stone-broke as he appears to be, which
is probably the correct one.
Hackett, of Westralia, is the only Australian
politician who habitually gets styled Dr. on the
strength of an honorary degree conferred by a
foreign university. Kingston, Bigjohn Forrest,
Turner, Fysh, Seddon, Reid and numerous others
had the same “honor” imposed upon them, but
they have let the public forget it. Still, they
don’t edit, and partly own, a daily paper, and
Hackett does.
Australian members of Parliament are
put to queer uses. Some years ago writer saw
on Dubbo railway station a huge cage of white
cockatoos consigned by a Coonamble constituent
to a then M.L.A., Mr. Cass, for sale in Sydney.
Now John Hurley tells an admiring crowd in the
Opposition room that a settler at Etnmaville lias
forwarded him the skin of a black snake which he
desires shall be sold to the Museum.
The political career of ex-Senator Ferguson
is worthy of notice. Ferguson won his position
as Senator (while away in England) by virtue of
the efforts of a lawyer-agent who sent an election-
card to every Queensland voter. Elected as an
absentee, Ferguson consistently remained an
absentee Senator most of his time, and finally lost
his senatorship by too much absenteeism. And
yet he displaced really capable politicians at the
election.
A REASONABLE IDEA.
MRS. BEEF-EATER: “ There goes Mr. Le-Kab-
bage; doesn’t he look thin and delicate, and
he’s a vegetarian.”
MR. BEEF-EATER: “Tut, I never yet knew a
vegetarian that had any meat on him."
Deakin is not the only Premier in this
country who thought Hansard men should, as
part of their official duties, follow him about with
notebook and pencil. Patrick Jennings, when
N.S.W. Premier, ordered the staff out for his
country tour, but the sitting of a Select Com-
mittee was made an excuse for declining. Free-
trade leaders —Jennings at that time was ad
valorem —have no difficulty in getting themselves
reported, but Procectionist speeches delivered in
the country are simply travestied in the Sydney
Freetrade dailies.
A farmer in the Dubbo (N.S.W.) district
killed himself last week after coming to the con-
clusion that his crops had been ruined by heavy
rains.
Maoriland’s champion whited sepulchre
died lately. William Waring Taylor was, 40
years ago, a man of spotless reputation in Wel-
lington. He was the Speaker
of its Provincial Council and a
pillar of the kirk, and he was so
highly respected of the people
that he was made the trustee of
all manner of persons’ savings.
If a parent wished to point out
to his son a model of integrity
and probity Waring Taylor
would be the model. He was
the G. Washington of the
Maoriland city, and a street
was named after him. And all
the time he was industriously
conveying to himself large sums
out of the trust estates he was
managing. When the truth
came out Wellington was thun-
derstruck. Prendergast, C.J.,
sentenced him to a considerable
term of imprisonment and wept
over the old hypocrite. Judges
generally do shed tears over
these scoundrels. Taylor came
out of gaol a few years ago,
and spent the last of his days
in an out-of-the-way part of the
State. His name has long since
disappeared from what used to be Waring Taylor-
street.
An Adelaide pressman :
Bundey definitely steps down from the Adelaide
Supreme Court Bench at the end of the present month,
but probably his successor (Attorney-General Gordon)
will not be appointed until the end of the long vacation
in March next. The Bulletin erred in stating that Judge
Bundey was once Mayor of Adelaide. That Bundey, also
named William and also resident in North Adelaide, was
a pious contractor who long ago reaped the reward of
militant Methodism in the other world. His judicial
namesake’s only connection with local government
affairs was as clerk of a district council when he was very
young.
“ Flyspeck ” : Re B. par. (5/10/’O3).
John Dennistoun Wood is not an “alderman of
Dennistoun, Tasmania.” Dennistoun is the name
of his property, which is a few miles outside of
Bothwell, Tasmania. Wood was born there, and
means to die there. He is Warden (or Mayor) of
Bothwell. People thought J.D.W. was too old
even for the Tasmanian Assembly, but he’s the
liveliest and most brainy in the small crowd of
members. The Opposition wants him to become
its leader instead of R. C. Patterson, who’s more
engineer than politician. Wood has renewed his
youth ; the other day he published in London his
first volume of poems. Nominally, he’s half-way
through the seventies.
One lady, who takes a prominent part m
Sydney women politics, is said to have a grievance
founded on a wrong of the deepest kind, against a
very well-advertised politician.
Baronet Rupert Clarke takes himself far
more seriously as a sportsman than as a legislator.
Even the struggle over the Factories Bill, the
fate of which hung on a hair, failed to drag him
into the House of Rich Landlords for a single
division, Rupert being engaged in far more in-
teresting consultations with trainer Scobie and
the men who lay the odds. When the fight for
seats takes place, in consequence of the altered
constitution of the House of Rich Landlords, the
sporting baronet will probably retire gracefully
from the arena that he has so long ornamented
without doing or saying anything.
“T.” : In Melbourne political and legal
circles it is regarded as certain that Premier
Irvine’s resignation will be the preliminary to a
judicial appointment. Old Justice Holroyd, now
75, was generally understood to be only hanging
on to his billet until King Ned took notice of him
in some way, and this little matter having been
fixed up through Irvine’s agency, will leave the
way clear for Holroyd to get off the bench, and
for Irvine to get on. It was noticeable that
directly the Iceberg person mentioned his possible
retirement at the Melbourne Mayoral spree, C. J.
Madden got off a few opportune remarks on the
increase of legal work—a sign that even though
Justice “Holly ” shouldn’t retire, Irvine may fill
up the place vacated some time ago by Justice
Hartley Williams.
It seems that at least one Australian bishop
runs a business as well as a See. His lordship of
Riverina, who has the spiritual charge of many
thousands of square miles of sand and very nearly
a dozen clergymen, has a contract for pumping
water for the supply of South Hay, where he
lives. But he’s giving it up ; probably he is going
in for market-gardening now, and wants the water
himself. Or probably the strain ot finding enough
water to pump is too much for him.
Baronet George Clifford, owner of the
Flaxbourne estate, taken over by the Maoriland
Government for closer settlement, claims £410,000
as compensation. The property came to George
from his father, Charles Clifford, one of the first
settlers in Wellington (M.L.), under the N.Z.
Company in 1843. While in England in 1850 he
took part in the agitation for Constitutional
Government in M.L. On the passing of the
Constitution Act he was elected to the first
Parliament, and became Speaker. In 1887 he
was created a baronet, and after that he was a
rich absentee in England until his death, about
10 years ago.

How far Charles Nicholson, who died
only the other day, goes back in Australian
history may be reckoned up from the fact that
he was a considerable buyer at the earliest of the
Melbourne land sales. He missed the first
two, held on the spot in 1837, but bought
one lot at the first sale of suburban
(Collingwood and East Melbourne) land ; this
was auctioned in Sydney in February, 1838. For
a while he had the idea that Geelong would
knock Melbourne out, and he bought two or
three sections there, paying up to £155 for a
block. Thomas Walker, who was one of his
colleagues representing Victoria in the N.S.W.
House, was, however, a bigger buyer of Mel-
bourne lands ; he picked up half-a-dozen allot-
ments at the first sale—the sale at which Johnny
Fawkner started the bidding and paid £32 for
Flinders and King-st. corner.
The farmer, when the land is dry,
And oats and things decline to sprout,
Implores the Lord with bitter cry
To terminate the cursed drought.
But w,hen the Lord doth turn the tap,
And soak the earth with fruitful rains
The farmer takes his gun, poor chap !
And shoots a bullet in his brains.
Then spake the Lord—“ Since time began,
I’ve rarely pleased the farmer man."

COBB AND Co.’s DRIVRE (somewhere between Hay, Hell, and Booligal): “Did you say, just now,
that your station was worth £50,000?”
“I did, Tom.”
“Well, lookee here, sir, if I had that there property of yourn I’d sell out and buy a hob in
Sheol and get cool for once.”
“Jim Pan”: Rain falls so frequently
now in Broken Hill that some of the residents are
really afraid of growing web-footed. It even
rains between the showers with Big Scrub-like
frequency. The municipal baths have just re-
opened. There were swimming races the other
day. There is talk of forming a rowing club.
A stockman, who recently reached Kal-
goorlie (W.A.) overland from Fowler’s Bay, with
sheep, says that Groperland can’t form the least
idea of the extent of the rabbit invasion which is
in store for it. According to him, rabbits are
within 200 miles of Kalgoorlie, sweeping across the
country like a tidal wave, eating everything before
them, except bluebush, and leaving the land
behind as bare as a floor. The rabbit-proof fence
that was to have kept out bunny and co. has failed
utterly ; it was so badly built that bunny went
through it with a bright smile in dozens of places.
And Groperland, instead of inspecting its fence in
good time, now takes a lingering and unsatis-
factory vengeance by discharging the officials who
ought to have looked after things, but apparently
didn't.
* * * *
A church choir of Ararat (Vic.) was pound-
ing out alleged music one recent Wednesday
night. During a lull in the storm unsteady foot-
steps were heard moving down the aisle towards
the organ. The owner of the footsteps stumbled
into a seat with a noise that made the conductor
think the trap-door of Hades was situated under
the building and the inhabitants were breaking
out. In a few minutes the intruder rose and
approached the conductor. He deposited 2s. 6d.
in small coins on the organ, muttered, “Thash
squarsh me all righsh, boss,” and retired. He
went down the way he came, got lost in the maze
of doors at the church entrance, and returned in-
side again by way of the opposite aisle. He
arrived near the choir again, stood, and asked in
a piteous voice, “Shay, boss, thish isn’t Sandy
Mac’s pub, ish it ? ” He had got lost, wandered
into the church, and paid his beer score to the
organist. That official led him round to Sandy
Mac’s pub. in a Christian spirit that was beautiful
to behold. History does not relate what became
of the 2s. 6d.
* * * *
“ D.” : Kiama is the biggest fraud town in
the States. It owns a blowhole, and its residents
are mostly blowholers. After about 37 residents
who were up for a trip had told me about this
wonder, I took the wife down to inspect it. At
the station there was a sign about the blowhole.
Three photographers’ shops were filled with views
of the blowhole. When I went in to get a drink
and buy a banana or two they said, “Going to
have a look at our blowhole ? Well worth coming
down to see.” We walked about half-a-mile,
expecting to see a spurt of foam three hundred
yards high every minute, but when we got to the
top of the hill could see nothing. Finally, with
the aid of a small local boy, we discovered ten
dejected individuals sitting around a hole in the
rooks. We inquired if it had made a start to
blow, but, taking the mournful head-shakes to
mean “ No,” we sat down and waited. We waited
two hours, then got up and gave a look of disgust
at the hole, and went back to town. The barman
said, “ It’s a pity the hole isn’t blowing to-day ;
isn’t it?” I didn’t answer. He knew in the
morning that it wasn’t blowing. The hole is worn
so wide now that it takes a Dunbar gale to make
it blow. I believe it only blows about every three
months.
* * • •
Bourke was visited by one of the Seven
Plagues rather violently, t’other day. Flies and
mosquitoes began to arrive in clouds on 28th
October, and, by nightfall, had become such solid
masses that half the lights of the town were
smothered out by them.
“Periwinkle”: A school of American
silver-grey squirrels has taken up its abode in Mr.
Septimus Miller’s pretty grounds at Malvern, near
Melbourne, and the animals afford much amuse-
ment by their capers. They are apparently a family
sprung from some squirrels imported by the late
Frederick Sargood, and set free in the grounds of
Rippon Lea, some years ago. The springing
antics of these cheery little creatures are marvel-
lous, and put the native ’possum to shame. They
live on stray acorns and Finns insignis cone-nuts
that abound in the residence grounds around East
St. Kilda. The squirrels rarely touch the ground
in their peregrinations, but hustle for their food
in the tree-tops. The tribe is increasing, for a
nest of young ones has been observed. Probably,
in the end, they will develop into another
national pest, and learn to like other things
besides Pinus insignis.

* * * *
“G.H.” : Hotel life in Westralia, if costly
and complainable in the menu department, has
compensating features. One of my most plea-
surable Westralian memories is the spectacle
of a very large, pink mine-manager, clothed in a
ferocious expression and a large axe, chasing
another and smaller mining individual all over a
Kalgoorlie hotel. The large person had returned
on a very hot Sunday morning from a journey
way out in the mulga; and had hastened to the
bathroom—which adjoined his and his wife’s
bedroom —before making his presence known
to the said helpmate. He was in the bath
when he heard sounds of someone being cordially
kissed. Then he seized the axe, which happened
to be in the bath room (all sorts of strange
lumber can be found in W.A. bath-rooms) and
battered in the bedroom door unanimously. As
he sprang in a diminutive mine boss sprang out,
dodged, and ricochetted down corridors and stairs
like a wireless telegram gone mad. The lady, in
a scarlet a,nd green dress, clung for a moment to
her proprietor, not so much out of love as that the
other man might get a good start; but the gentle-
man with the axe cast her off like an exploded
theory and thundered down stairs in pursuit.
After chasing his enemy down to the cellar and
from there back to the balcony ; thence downstairs
again and through the dining-room into the
Erivate bar—the big man’s wind gave out; so he
urled the axe at his quarry, missed him, and
wrecked a £2O mirror, and collapsed on a blue
ottoman. Now that he had thrown away his only
clothing he looked shockingly undressed, but the
barman hastily forced him into a long dust coat
and some bar towels and tied a mat round him,
and so he who had come down dressed in a large
hatchet went up again clothed in things that
made him look like a dancing dervish of the worst
kind.
* * * *
“Nemo”: What is a carpet-snake, any-
how ? Burnt down a box-tree, the other day, and
out of hollow limbs got four snakes ranging from
18in. to 6ft. Was told they were all carpets.
Have got a skin in my possession 10ft. long, the
owner of which was shot while coiled round a
rabbit. This, too, lam told is a carpet. Two
men here, while timber-cutting two or three years
ago, killed a snake 16ft. long (one of them still
keeps the skin). This, too, is called a carpet. I
expect some day to come across a quadruped with
horns, and to be told that it is a carpet snake. A
carpet snake seems to be almost anything.

At a recent meeting of the
Jerilderie (N.S.W.) Pastures Protection Board
Rupert Carrington drew attention to the practices
of lessees of public watering places. He said that
some of the reserves were practically useless for
the purpose for which they were intended, viz., as
camping places for the travelling public, and
instanced one where, he said, out of 500 acres set
apart, only 11 acres were allowed by the high and
mighty lessee for public use. A motion expressing
the Board’s protest against the system was lost.
A good many other things are lost in Australia;
especially things that are likely to do any good.
* * * *
A weird contrivance which was going to
revolutionise pearl-shelling was carted north some
months ago, and put to work in Torres Straits. It
was a sort of diving bell, fitted with windows,
wheels, and a rake. The idea was to drop it into
the sea at depths which only the “flash ’’diver will
attempt, and then not for long; and the man
inside was to work the rake and scoop the shells
into a fixed receptacle. The first trouble was that
the wheels were found useless owing to the un-
evenness of the sea-bottom. After that, they put
jointed legs on to the monster; and that was a
very pretty piece of mechanism, only, unfortu-
nately, the legs used to get fast in the rotten
coral. After dropping many thousands in their
experiments the people behind the machine
reckoned that pearl-shelling would have to go on
somehow without it.
WASTED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poorest bit of ground
For miles and miles around
Was J. B. Riley’s 40-acre lot.
But all the best was gone
When he came ; ’twas that or none,
So he took it and began to clear a plot.
He possessed a crossbred ewe,
And a nannygoat or two,
A piebald mare, an ancient poley cow,
A dilapidated cart,
That had surely dropped apart
But for rope ; and he’d an old style wooden plough.
Now all round the neighborhood
The land was pretty good,
And each settler had a tidy flock or herd ;
But though great the handicap
Riley didn’t care a rap,
His soul was but to emulation stirred.
You would scarce believe the yield
From his scantily-tilled field—
Wilson’s richest land went thirty and no more ;
But when Riley threshed, ’twas found
Every acre of his ground
Had produced a trifle over ninety-four.
And that ancient crossbred ewe
Did not have a lamb or two
As is usual, but a litter like a sow.
They were pure merinos too,
And the fleeces that they grew !
I regret the weights I don’t remember now.
And Poley had a calf
About every week, and half
Were Devon and the others pure shorthorn.
01 the nannygoats then one
Had pigs; that took the bun
Till the other hatched out turkeys one fine morn.
Then the good old piebald mare
Took a hand in the affair,
With a bright bay foal, and thoroughbred to boot.
’Twas a strange coincidence,
Just the other side the fence,
Thompson’s blood mare foaled a ewe-necked piebald brute.
So J. B. Riley grew
Rich and richer as time flew;
And when neighbors hadn’t cash at their command
He had lots of it to lend,
And it turned out in the end
He invariably had to take their land.
Now he reckons by the mile
His country, and in style
Goes each week to take his seat upon the bench.
He’s especially severe
On petty theft, and here
His virtuous zeal can nothing ever quench.
Yet withal, I’m bound to say,
He’s completely thrown away.
Had he been by fortune differently placed
World-wide fame had sure been his
For the claiming ; as it is,
He’s just an empire-builder gone to waste 1
TOWRAI.
SILVER CHEQUES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The “cobbler’s” cut, and the shearing’s ended,
Away we go with our silver cheques ;
All castle building is now suspended,
Bright expectations are naught but wrecks.
The men on the bikes are sadlv wheeling,
The pace of the horsemen is deadly slow,
Whilst foot and horse are alike all feeling
As most folk feel when the funds are low.
Once Tarwong mustered a hundred thousand
(The old hands say there were sometimes more',
With plenty of bush on which to browse, and
Oarrot and crowfoot in galore.
The whims weren’t creaking late and early,
Or jumbucks waiting their thirst to slake,
And the canvas buckets were used but rarely
When whalers fished in the Tarwong lake.
Flow the staunch old-timer, so gaunt and hoary,
Takes his empty pipe from his drooping mouth,
As again he repeats the same old story
And points with a bony hand due south.
And we gaze on a few dead swamp gums standing
Midst skeletons drear and heaps of silt,
And he tells of the boat and the lower landing,
And islands green, where the wild swans built.
N.S.W.
W. TULLY.

Charles Nicholson, who died in England
the other day (his portrait appears in this issue),
vvas one of the three members for Victoria in the
first semi-reformed Parliament of N.S.W. in 1843.
IRVINE’S POLICEMAN.
As a measure of economy the Victorian Government has raised the retiring age of the
police to 65 years. This is Irvine’s policeman as he soon will be, bearing down on the push.
The retiring age of the push hasn’t been correspondingly extended.
Dr. Lang and Thomas Walker were his colleagues,
Nicholson became Speaker—he was the first under
whom Parkes sat. When the latter wrote his
autobiography in 1890 he could only think of four
other living men who sat in the Parliament of
1854, which ivas Parkes first—Nicholson, Daniel
Cooper, William Manning, and Augustus IHorris.
If Jrarkes was right, there is thus now not one
member of the ’54 Parliament left. Nicholson
filled many other positions at different times,
among them President of the Queensland Legis-
lative Council (for three months), and he did
valuable work for Sydney University. He died at
os
tne age ot 30.
JUST THE USUAL THING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The usual motion of censure was moved in the N.S.W.
Assembly in the usual way by Mr. Carruthers, and the
usual arguments were used by the usual speakers. The
defence of the Government was that it had done nothing
unusual. It had, in fact, acted in the same manner as
had been quite usual with previous Governments. As
usual, the Labor party supported the Government. The
tone of the debate was just as usual, and the matter
ended as usual. The whole thing was tremendously usual.
It was just a fair transaction,
Just a business deal and square,
Though the Opposition faction
Started rumors in the air.
There was nothing wrong about it,
Nothing “ cronk ” and nothing “ crook ”;
It was fair, no need to doubt it,
You could swear it on the book !
(But why should any man croak so
Of Milburn Greek long years ago ?
Now, supposing Thomas Waddle
Wants to buy a piece of land,
And he gets it in his noddle
That one piece will suit him grand,
And suppose that of the d’rectors
One should bear the name of See,
Why should that annoy electors—
What is that to you and me ?
(There’s one thing beats me here, and that’s
Why think of Coonanbara hats?)
It’s a very simple matter,
Though Carruthers makes a fuss,
But we needn’t mind his chatter—
Why should Joseph trouble us ?
If he moves a vote of censure
Half-a-dozen times a year;
Let us lift a modest quencher,
Wish him luck in lager beer !
(And yet somehow this last debate
Recalls the Hornsby land estate !)
“Though the Opposition hoot us,
We are honorable men 1”
Seems to me a chap named Brutus
Must have heard the same thing when
Mark Antonius was cursin’
In a tone of bitter gall.
“He’s an honorable person !”
Said Antonius. “So are all!”
(And so they are 1 Yet I’m afraid
I can’t forget the Mountain Maid.)
It is mean to be suspecting,
And it’s very foolish, too,
When the statesmen we ’re electing
Are so loyal, brave and true !
They are trusted with the dollars,
And their manners, as a rule,
Would do credit to the scholars
Of a Baptist Sunday school.
(In fact, they’re angels minus wings—
But somehow one remembers things.)
It’s a stupid thing to blame ’em,
Very stupid thing indeed ;
If you find a thing to shame ’em,
They will say ’twas done by Reid,
Parkes, or Dibbs. If that suffices
Then no further they may look ;
But if not, they’ll find some vices
In Caractacus and Cook !
(They seem to think there is no blame
If other cusses did the same !)
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
Adelaide Art Show.
THE LATE CHARLES NICHOLSON.
A notable Australian politician of 40 years ago.
The Bulletin’s Adelaide correspondent writes
Sixth “Federal” picture-exhibition in Adelaide con-
tains nothing startling, but, as the governors of the local
Art Gallery have promised to go on spending £250 a year
on their Australian Room, four States have sent in 95
picked works. N.S.VV. is disappointingly represented—
Hansen and Lister-Lister, who were both bought last
year, having sent nothing. Sid Long has his well-known
little girl in crimson velvet, and Fred Leist a clever portrait
of a girl (with no shoulders) in a white boa. Queensland
is found in this exhibition for the first time, and Godfrey
Rivers’ ambitious torchlight-effect in “Prawn-catching”is
about the biggest canvas on the walls. Martin Roberts
has a hill-and-valley impressionist landscape, which has
exactly caught Sid Long’s style (Long’s “ Valley,” bought
four or five years ago, is a bone of contention in the Ade-
laide Gallery to this day). Bernard Hall (bought last
year) has a classical nude which shows some abnormal
muscles, and fails to attract. M'Cubbiri deserts landscape
for a big “Australian Girl” in white. Probably it is a
portrait; anyway, there is nothing typical of nationality
about it. Withers, like M'Cubbin, has sold a big picture
on a previous occasion. Pie now shows “ Still Autumn,”
the Wynne Prize picture of 1901 ; it has a faint and wan
effect, like so much of the Melbourne work. Victorian
women are very strong. Helen Peters puts in a powerful
and painful lamplight effect of sewing women at work,
but the strongest impression is made by Edith Evans,
with heads of a man and an old woman. The latter
resembles one of the alleged Old Masters offered for sale
in London or Paris, and the Board promptly bought it
for £80 (catalogue price). This is the only purchase this
year from the visiting artists, who on the previous occa-
sions have had all the big cheques. James Ashton, among
the local men, has surpassed all his former work with a study
of the reed-beds between the city and the coast, and gets
£75. His son Will, studying in Cornwall, sends out two
small landscapes which show a transition stage with a
good deal of promise beyond. Two little flower studies
by women have been purchased, and a£100 commission goes
to young Power, whose big pictures of draught horses and
cattle teams are a leading feature of every Adelaide
picture show. The experiment seems rather risky, since
nothing he has yet done has been considered quite to
justify purchase, and it would be awkward to refuse
whatever he may offer as the result of the commission.
Tasmania and Maoriland, formerly pretty well repre-
sented, have sent nothing to Adelaide this year.
For the first time for years a couple of
mobs of cattle are coming South alongside the
overland telegraph-line. There is a fairly well-
defined track all the way from Port Darwin to
the head of the South Australian great northern
railway ; but for many years it has only been used
by the teams which carry supplies twice each year
to the repeating stations.

Bob Upstil: The Bulletin’s view is just this: The
man who holds £lOO,OOO worth of land in the country
may be monopolising 50,000 acres, and thereby depriving
5000 families of a chance to make a living. A man who

Our Debt to Major Mitchell.
Ernest Faveno writes:
Australia has always been a cruelly martyred country,
subjected to many and various curses which are not of its
own deserving, but which tend to hamper its progress
and damn its reputation. These curses are mostly well
known, such as convictism, imperialism, and the
curse of having been born too late in the world s his*
tory. These are all bad enough, especially the last, but
one curse we suffer under that has not yet been suffi-
ciently protested against, is the curse of our nomencla-
ture. We are branded with the official dictionary. Most
of the names are good enough as names, and there is no
glaring fault to find with them beyond the fact that the
original owners of them had little or nothing to do with
Australia, beyond a shadowy official connection.
MRS. ALFRED DEAKIN.
The Dutch conferred on many points the names of their
captains or those of the vessels they commanded—a
sensible enough custom, to which the west coast still
testifies. The French plastered the names of the first
empire on the land when they got a chance, and those
that survive have no meaning for us.
Cook commenced the game as far as the British were
concerned and New South Wales became our designation,
innocent of all guile as we were in those days. As far as
their limitations allowed, the first settlers showered their
names around without care or thought. Then came the
opening up of the interior highlands, and Oxley, Hume,
and Sturt followed the custom of branding official titles
everywhere they could. Our two largest rivers, the
Murray and the Darling, seem feebly named beside the
Mississippi or Missouri. True, the Murrumbidgee escaped,
but that was because the settlers heard of the river by
name from the natives before it was seen. It was nearly
being called the Brisbane for all that.
Amongst the explorers, Sturt and Mitchell stand
head and shoulders above the crowd, and Sturt is the
first of the two. Mitchell was more cautious. He would
never have led the forlorn hope down the Murray as
Sturt did, an exploit as daring and reckless as any
in the history of geographical discovery. But, strange to
say, while Sturt’s character appears to have a more
romantic side to it than that of the disciplinarian
Mitchell, the names Sturt gave the features of the
country he discovered are commonplace in the extreme,
whilst Mitchell broke new ground in selecting native
names as he could. His names, too, bear evidence of
taste and judgment. No fault can be found with the
Warrego, Maranoa, Belyando and Barvvan—not Barwon
as it is now wrongly called. True he missed the title
Barcoo, and in a mistaken fit of pride called that river
the Victoria ; and when exploring Victoria he did not
display his usual judgment in the Pyrenees or
Grampians, but we are indebted to him for making
an effort to stem the tide of commonplace and set
a good example, which could have been followed with
advantage, late in the day as it was. Unfortunately, it
was not; but what a different kind of nomenclature our
geography would have if the other explorers had taken
trouble with their names. In after years Mackinlay tried
designating places after the native names, but he had an
unmusical ear, and took the names at random. Leich-
hardt, when he blundered through his one successful
trip, had splendid opportunities, but not one creek or
river discovered by him bears a native name. Grey
neglected his chances, and the many rivers in Western
Australia that he named have all official titles. Mitchell
is the only one who showed any kindness to posterity ;
and to him we owe most of the few appropriate names of
Australian rivers, and also a suggestion of what the geo-
graphical nomenclature of Australia might have been.

The Country Workers’ Funeral.
All attempts to make Victorian legislators realise
that the workers in the country districts are as
fully entitled to Factories Act protection as their
brethren in the towns, seem to be utterly result-
less. A weird tradition has grown up to the effect
that, while the boot manufacturer and the cigar-
maker, for instance, can and should pay their
employees a decent wage, the farmer, who is sup-
posed to be engaged in a “natural” industry,
can’t get along without under-paid and sweated
labor. This ridiculous notion was pandered to in
the old Factories Act, which expressly excluded
all rural industries from its operation; and
Parliament stands firmly by the ancient de-
lusion. Yet those well competent to judge
contend that there are no industries more in
need of legislative regulation than those con-
nected with the land. More especially is this
the case with the alleged “great” dairying in-
dustry. Whenever this industry is mentioned there
is a roar of indignation—from the land owners, and
it is scornfully asked whether the Act is to provide
for cows that will only need milking six days a
week, and whether it is proposed to limit the
labor of the farm-hand to eight hours per day.
These insane queries are only evasions of the real
point at issue. Cows must be milked seven days
a week, and early and late each day ; and the
farm-laborer is quite prepared to do all this
cheerfully— provided he is properly paid for
his work. That is the crux of the whole position.
If the farm-laborer were given a decent wage, say
(is. per day, he would be able to marry and settle
down and become a useful citizen. At present he
potters along on anything from 7s. 6d. a
week and found, and is rarely more than a float-
ing, irresponsible nuisance to his district, in a
social way. He can’t marry, but he is the father
of the unmarried girl’s baby. As to the ability of
the industry to pay decent wages, there should be
no two opinions. When dairymen can give up to
£3 5s. per acre per annum rent, for grass land, it
is no use contending that they can’t pay honest
wages. Probably, after paying fair wages, they
couldn’t pay quite such atrocious rents, but that
is a vastly different matter, and of no interest to
the farm-laborer, though it is the secret of nearly
all the opposition to the extension of the Fac-
tories Act countrywards. And, apart from the
farm-hand, there is another class of country labor
that is underpaid all over the State. There are,
in Victoria, some 300 butter factories and
creameries, as well as several cheese and bacon
factories. All of these establishments are, for the
mysterious reason already mentioned, allowed to
have Freetrade in labor, with the usual disastrous
results to the laborer. Even the managers of
such concerns are usually underpaid, but the worst
cases are to be found in connection with the minor
billets of butter-maker, engine-driver, and ordinary
hands. These men all work seven days a week,
mostly at a wage, for the ordinary hands, of 30s.
or less, while the engine-driver and butter-maker
may get 35s., or in exceptional cases, £2. Then
there is a lower grade still—the unfortunate con-
tract managers of the creameries. Most factories
let the management of their creameries either by
tender, or at a fixed lump sum. A typical adver-
tisement lately asked for a creamery manager at
£168 per annum. For this sum the unfortunate
man was expected to run a creamery for 365 days,
employ a man to help him, find his own wood (for
the engine), horse and waggon, and cart the cream
seven miles every day to the factory. This case—
which is, in fact, a favorable one—occurred in a
district whose factories publish balance-sheets like
these—the latest
Butter Sales for
12 Months. Profit.
Colac £104,000 £2517
Camperdown .. .. 81,370 1805
Glenormiston .. .. 56,222 1756
Cobden 54,194 1518
The rottenness of the situation is accentu-
ated by the fact that, apart from the Western
District, the majority of the butter fac-
tories and creameries belong to Melbourne
firms, such as the Fresh Food Co., the Melbourne
Chilled Putter Co., Wood and Co., and half-a-
dozen others, while most of the cheese factories
lire proprietary concerns, generally owned by big
Undowners. These are, to a great extent, the
‘country producers” whom the Government and
i arliament are so fearful of hurting.
The Art Position in N.S.W.
In the discussion on the N.S.W. Education Esti-
mates this year there was some pertinent criticism
of the State’s system, or want of system, of subsi-
dising Art, and Minister Perry promised to make
a radical change and have “a satisfactory
scheme ”, ready next year. The N.S.W. Art
Students’ League is responsible for this promise,
which, if carried out, will crown long labors with
a^‘ 11 ® venieil t. N.S.W. has made the instruction
°r o public in almost all things a matter
of State interest and State subsidy, and
is, therefore, as well justified in subsi-
dising the teaching of picture-painting as
of chemistry or letters or any of the
numerous subjects grouped as technical education.
Put the money spent, so far, has been mostly
wasted—divided between the Sydney Technical
1 • i?t two wrail gling art societies, each of
which had too much to do scratching at the other
eat to perform any useful work. With a view to
reform, Education Minister Perry recently insisted
on the two wrangling societies amalgamating or
else losing their subsidies. The only practical
result was to bring the row under one roof, and,
t° r all Es unctuous satisfaction in now being
dubbed a “Royal” institution, the N.S.W. Art
Society is as inept and useless a body as ever. It
inspires no confidence in students, and the nature
<>f its teaching can be judged from the fact that its
instructor was, last year, paid only £92 6s. lOd.!
1 hat represented the actual sum spent on tuition,
though the State subsidy—voted for tuition and
tuition alone—was £800. From the pupils the
society obtained as fees £180, and the total
expenses of its classes —excluding rent —were
£154 11s. Id. Evidently the money voted by the
State for education was mostly put to some other
i?qo * lhe nn sweated instructor, with his
£92 6s. lOd. (in Sydney a tailoress has a minimum
uage of £1109 a year !), cannot be expected to give
good service to 291 students. The N.S.W. Art
Students’ Union claims, with justice, that the
Sydney student is deprived by the Art Society of
any chance of genuine instruction, and shows the
lesult as compared with the system of another
(apital, Melbourne:
With the exception of Air. Frank AIahony, whose
natural genius has enabled him by great labor to over-
come obstacles which no other Sydney man has been able
to surmount, Sydney-trained artists are almost wholly
unknown outside New South Wales. The South Aus-
tralian Art Gallery has purohased from artists in Victoria
and N.S.W. works to the value of £1357 ss. Of this
amount £862 ss. was expended on nine works by artists
trained at Melbourne, and £SO 10s. on two works by
Sydney-trained artists. No Victorian art gallery contains
an example of a Sydney-trained artist’s work.
Sydney National Art Gallery, for pictures in the Aus-
tralian section, has paid to Sydney-trained artists, £1913
12s. ; to Melbourne-trained artists, £1946 Bs. ; and to
artists trained abroad, £4661 9s. If art students were
asked which paintings in the Australian section of the
N.S.W. Art Gallery afforded most assistance to them in
their studies they would probably say those of Streeton
and Longstaff. But for the effects traceable to the
influence of Melbourne-trained artists, the paintings of
Sydney-trained artists in the National Art Gallery would
be devoid of merit. Intrinsically, the Melbourne painters’
work is as valuable as the work of British and foreign
trained artists resident in Sydney. Of all work in the
Australian section of the Gallery it shows the most
serious attempts at solving difficult problems in painting.
It has those qualities without which no work can be of
permanent value—namely, sound draughtmanship and
knowledge.
Further, as showing the way in which the public
money has been spent by the rival cliques of
artists, acting through their societies or the
trustees of the National Gallery, these facts,
cited by the Students’ Union, are valuable :
John Longstaff was paid £55 for his admirable “ Por
trait Study of a Lady in Black,” which, when exhibited
at the Royal Academy, was highly commended by
the late Leighton, and £65 for a portrait of Henry
Lawson. Julian R. Ashton was paid £250 for a portrait
of a late statesman, smaller than the first mentioned of
Longstaff’s pictures, and £l5O for a figure study.
The plain fact is that, in the matter of art
instruction and of art purchases, the N.S.W.
Public Treasury has been made the victim
of various cliques of artists, and it is time
that the system was stopped. A thorough reform
is called for both in regard to the Government
subsidy to instruction and the Government
purchases of pictures. The scandal of running
into the National Gallery portraits of living per-
sonages—more or less celebrated—for personal
rather than for art reasons is one of the symp-
toms of the mutual back-scratching which has
infected the N.S.W. art system. One artist
friend paints Premier Parkes, and, of course,
Premier Paukes goes in at a big price. Then
Dibbs is Premier, and he has an artist friend, and
his portrait goes into the gallery. Premier Reid
comes next, and Heaven alone knows why N.S.W.
has not had, in its National Gallery, Premier Bill
Lyne and Premier John See, done more or less after
the traditions of Chinese portraiture. A portrait
of Education Minister Perry did appear to hold
a threatening hand over the community a few
weeks, but, praise to the Fates, the danger passed
away. N.S.W. Parliament should insist on cen-
tralising Sydney art-education at the National
Gallery, and at the same time seeing that the
trustees of that Gallery are independent and
knowledgeable enough to give the public good
value for the money it votes for art-purchases
and art education.

A quaint person named Craig, who is one of the
pillars of the Empire Navy League, writes to the
S.M. Herald about cotton-planting in Australia :
Knowing that cotton was being “ringed” and
“ trusted ” in the Southern States of America, that the
mills of Lancashire and Lanarkshire were almost stopped
for the want of the raw fibre, and that £1,000,000 had
been subscribed by Bjitish mill-owners for the purpose
of growing cotton in unknown and untried rivers in
British Crown colonies and protectorates, I wrote the
directors of several companies at home, but whilst they
all recognised the suitability of Queensland for cotton
culture, they would not risk the investment of money
owing to the socialistic legislation of the Labor party.
This is either dull ignorance or invention. The
Labor party never had a majority in Queensland
Parliament, and hasn’t got one now, so it has
never been and isn’t now in a position to carry
socialistic legislation. If the quaint Craig and
his alleged directors didn’t know this, it was
dull ignorance. And, anyhow, if any such
directors did write so stupid a thing, and
Craig didn’t reply that they were laboring under
a misapprehension, and that the Morehead, and
MTlwraith, and Griffith, and Nelson, and
Byrnes, and Dickson, and Philp and other
Ministries which have bossed Queensland abso-
lutely for the past 15 years have been all anti-
Labor Governments, then he has, by his silence,
deliberately deceived large British capitalists.
Knaves and Fools.
When one knave falls a victim to another knave
honest men can afford to stand aside and watch
the game. In Philadelphia recently a man was
accosted by a well-dressed youth in the street who
handed him a hundred-dollar bank-note, saying :
“Sir, you have dropt this!” Now the man had
drept nothing; but seeing the bank-note, and
believing that a mistake 1 ad been made, he took
it from the youth, thanked him for his honesty,
and him with five dollars. After
gloating a few days over the accident by which he
had profited ninety-five dollars, the man took the
note to a bank to change it, where he was
promptly arrested for uttering a forgery. He
was detained for over a week, to his great loss in
time, money, and dignity, and finally, to clear
himself, was obliged to disclose the real facts of
the ease.
Serve him right, you say. Why certainly.
Not only was he a knave, but he lacked skill to
distinguish the true from the false, and was
swindled in consequence.
If I enter a shop and ask for a bottle of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and the shopkeeper offers me some-
thing else instead, that shopkeeper is a knave and
takes me for a fool. Very few shopkeepers are
guilty of such practices ; they are, as a class, as
honorable as any other: but, unfortunately, no
trade or profession is without its black sheep.
A false medicine is more harmful than a false
bank-note. For thirty-five years Seigel’s Syrup has
been the favorite family medicine in sixteen of the
largest countries in the world, and is an unrivalled
cure for indigestion and all the numerous com-
plaints which accompany or arise from that
disease. “It was proved by doctors,” says Mrs.
Cheetham, of Winchester Road, Rand wick, near
Sydney, N.S.W., “that I was suffering from
general debility, nerve exhaustion, bronchitis,
asthma, and malnutrition —a formidable list of
complaints. My condition was terrible. I spent
nearly five years in trying various patent medi-
cines, under private doctors, and attending hospi-
tal, but obtained no relief until I took Seigel’s
Syrup. A short course of that medicine effectually
remedied all my complaints, and I have since
remained well and strong.”
It lias been said that this would be a dull world
without knaves and fools —that both are as neces-
sary to the balance of society as sharps and flats'
are to harmony. But nobody can afford to allow
knavish tricks to be played with their health.

Unionism, it is claimed, has nearly
doubled the wages of tailoresses in Sydney.
The last vessel which will ever leave
Queensland to import niggers from the South
Seas sails this month. After that, the traffic
will consist solely in the return of Tommy Tanna
to the place where Providence deposited him.
Speaking of the Immigration Restriction
Act at his recent meeting at Adelaide, Kingston
said that he would gladly make the undesirables’
test even more severe. He suggested that the
reading of a page of his own (Kingston’s) hand-
writing would about fill the bill.
The threat to petition the British Govern-
ment against allowing the Commonwealth to take
1000 square miles for a capital is quite in accord
with N.S.W. historical character. It petitioned
against Victorian separation, and besought the
Queen not to assent to Queensland's attempt to
dismember “its natural territory.”
“ Thank goodness,” said Premier Seddon,
in one of his recent bursts of frenzy, “I have
never yet heard a Maorilander express the desire
to have a navy of our own.” It is fortunate for
Britain that it isn’t like Seddon. If it had never
had a desire for a navy of its own somebody
would have burned London before now.
Bruce Smith, in a recent speech, wasted a
lot of time in clearing his character from a charge
of sending his children to R.C. schools. The
time might have been much better spent in
explaining how he happened to be a violently
Freetrade Minister who gave large Protection to
nearly 50 industries, and then pointed to the
results as a proof of the blessings of Freetrade.

—MR. DEAKIN, at Toowoomba.
There is still nothing so very bad to weep
aboutin the Commonwealth’s population statistics.
The increase of population in the last two years
was 111,370. N.S.W.’s population increased
during the one year 1902 by 28,180—the largest
increase since 1892. Federation, or the Six
Hatters, or something, seems to have given the
N.S. W. population increase quite a vigorous lift.
The inquiry into the irregularities in the
N.S.W. Chief Secretary’s department has already
resulted in four persons being criminally proceeded
against, and other charges are said to be pending.
The late Principal Under-Secretary must be some-
how responsible for the prevailing chaos. Yet
his mana was so great that nobody would have
dreamed of overhauling his department during his
lifetime.
Theo. Fink is again endeavoring to get
through Victorian Parliament his Bill to allow
legal practitioners of other States to practise in
Victoria, provided the other States reciprocate.
The idea is a good one all round. When legal
sharks wander all over Australia to appear before
the various courts it will help the railway revenue,
and when there is a railway accident there will be
a fair probability of at least one shark being killed.
H. H. Fyfe in the Nineteenth Century :
The presence of aliens has transferred certain areas of
the East End (London) into entirely foreign quarters.
Out of these areas British workmen are driven, either by
landlords, lost to all sense of decency or patriotism, who
find they can screw more out of the alien than out of the
native-born tenant; or else by their natural repugnance
to neighbors whose standard of manners and living can
only be compared to that of a not very particular pig.
This has the double effect of excluding a large number
of natives from the districts where they have been
accustomed to work, and of introducing into these dis-
tricts a state of overcrowding far more dangerous to
health, and far more prejudicial to public decency than
would exist if we had the native population alone to deal
with. •

These few United States immigration
statistics for August, 1903,,are presented to those
cheerful lunatics who how] at the alleged severity
of Australian legislation. During that month 810
persons were debarred from entering the country,
of whom 55 were paupers, 156 diseased, 6 convicts,
7 polygamists, 1 idiotic, and 95 contract laborers.
Yet, despite this ruthless discrimination, the
high-tariffed country gained 64,977 of population
by immigration in that one month.

received so coldly that the meeting had to stamp
its feet to restore the circulation.
London Times on the Australian attitude:
At any rate, we believe, nothing can alter such patriot-
ism as the Commonwealth displayed during the late war.
But the history of that outburst has yet to be written.
It was a spasm of intense feeling, preceded by—and likely
to be succeeded by—a period of growing indifference to
things outside the Southern Pacific ; and all through it,
like the voice of a sort of devil’s conscience, the bushman
heard his Bulletin murmuring persistently, “ Yes, yes,
you’re a little excited now, but you’ll get over it, and
then you’ll see what a fool you have made of yourself.’’
No Briton likes to think he has played the fool rampantly,
and the Australian is already uneasy about his recent
behavior.
And, now that it is plain that he fought the Boer
farmer in order to flood his country with Chow
labor, has not the Australian good cause to be
“ uneasy ” in his conscience ?
