The “Upper Forty-Six,” who overlook
the work of the M.L. House of Reps., came in for
some hot criticism lately when Tommy Taylor fired
off a speech against the “ forlorn creatures ” in lan-
guage dipped in brimstone. Seddon, as usual,
defended his blessed appointments, and declared
that if the Upper House was aged and incom-
petent no one could blame him for it. Then

that Seddon was responsible for 26 of them.
The Sydney papers have been full of
complaints of the shocking way the Federal
Government had compiled the electoral rolls, and
ascribed the alleged chaos to the inherent
depravity of the Deakin Ministry. Yet the West
Sydney electorate revision court, held to rectify
any omissions, resulted in exactly TWO persons
coming forward to claim insertion on the roll and
the West Sydney electorate comprises nearly five
State electorates, and represents some 25,000
names !
Miss Yida Goldstein, who is standing for
the Senate, told a Prahran (Vic.) audience last
week that she. was a Protectionist but wants to
sink the fiscal issue so that more time can be given
to domestic and social legislation. If Miss
Goldstein doesn’t realise that high Protection—
the finding of work and wages for the people —is
domestic and social legislation, then The Bulletin
is disappointed in her. “Social and domestic
legislation ” is the art of bringing about a fairly
equal distribution of the comforts of life. Pro-
tection is the art of having some comforts to dis-
tribute. You may make a country rich by Pro-
tection and forget to provide for the fair distri-
bution of the wealth; and, again, you may pro-
vide for the distribution of wealth and forget to
provide the wealth. -Also, you may enact that
nobody shall be overworked in factories, and
forget to provide the factories. Protection can’t
be dissociated from social and domestic legislation.

N.S.W. Premier See :
As far as State and Federal rights are concerned, I am
not prepared to give one scrap more power to the
Commonwealth Government and Parliament than is con-
ferred by the. Constitution Act.
Mr. See run's the N.S.W. Parliament on cat-and-
dog wrangle-and-scratch lines. Under his leader-
ship it has shown an absolute incapacity for useful
work. But he won’t consent to any other body
taking over the work which it won’t do. A
typical dog in the manger.
Opposition-leader John Darling, of S.A.,
heaved a boomerang when he launched his motion
for the reduction of the land-tax by a farthing.
Faced with a threatened deficit of about £20,000,
independent of the chronic Northern Territory
shortage, the Government has set out to get the
money mainly by increasing the railway rates on
coal, coke and ore—in other words, chiefly from
the Broken Hill Proprietary Co., of which John
Darling and his legislative friend and protege,
H. C. E. Muecke, are the S.A. directors.
A member of Sydney Labor Council is
responsible for the suggestion that the difference
between the tender of the State Clothing Factory
and the successful private tender, for the railway
clothing contract, is to be made good out of the
funds of the Employers’ Federation. Certainly
the employers have a very solid interest in
enabling a private firm to cut under the Govern-
ment factory, so as to show the superiority of
“private enterprise,” and bring the State-owned
shop into discredit. The suggestion isn’t wildly
improbable.
John Forrest raised a roar of laughter dur-
ing his Adelaide meeting. He told the audience
that during May, 1897, Acting-Premier Holder
offered to join W.A. in building an overland rail-
way, although it would have cost S.A. £2,000,000.
“That’s just like Holder,” commented old John
Darling. Forrest continued that the proposal was
declined by his Government, adding : “ We were
both hard up ;we had no money.” “ Oh, Holder
had plenty,” sneered Darling. “No,” said For-
rest, “Holder was as tight as I was.” Now
Holder was never tight in his life.

A few honest folks in Tasmania want to
have their votes counted in the election of Sena-
tors, and claimed it would hurt their consciences
to vote for any sort of a piebald candidate. The
Labor party found it had to nominate three can-
didates for the Senate, otherwise no Tasmanian
could have recorded a straight vote for White
Australia. There are a full dozen candidates,
more coming, and they advocate all shades of pie-
bald and various degrees of wobbles, but the only
unmistakably clean white candidates are the
Labor men. Labor’s three Tasmanian candidates
for the Senate are a carpenter, a manufacturer,
and a journalist. The last-named (Milner Mac-
Master) was for years at the head of Devon
College, and is said to be the clearest and best
public speaker in the State.
Two States are at last thinking hard
about getting rid of the Legislative Council
incubus. In M.L., Seddon, asked if he would
help to make the Unnecessary House elective,
told the Reps, that he hadn’t any idea of
strengthening it, but he had very serious ideas
with regard to getting rid of it altogether.
And in South Australia its early abolition is
pretty certain —it drove another nail into its
almost-finished coffin when, the other even-
ing, it tossed out the Bill for reassessing land
for the purposes of the land tax, and thus
forced the Government to put up railway
rates, starve the road vote, and make another
cut at the civil service—already about the-
ntost miserably paid in Australia.
Tims Mr. Spruce Myth : “ The Aus-
tralian people are not displaying the courage
of their ancestors in meeting all-comers in
the national race for life.” Well, our Pro-
tectionist British ancestors —the people of the
Peninsula, Trafalgar, Waterloo, Blenheim,
Oudenarde, Malplaquet, and very nearly
every other big fight that the Smith party
brags about—were a pretty good lot. Since-
Britain took to Freetrade its biggest event
has been the Boer war, in which it took
three years to man down a nation that wasn’t
equal to the population of Sydney. Free-
trade seems to have wrecked the Briton as' a.
fighting man. Let us imitate our ancestors —
Smith's Protectionist ancestors.
How business is done in N.S.W. Assembly.
Last session the Free Education Bill of Griffith,
M.L.A., was left unfinished Owing to muddle and
“ jaw.” The other night:
(1) Griffith moved that the House should resurrect the-
Bill where it left off. General excitement. Talk. Divison.
Carried by 63 to 8.
(2) Griffith moved that sufficient of the standing orders-
be suspended to allow of the Bill being put through its-
remainirig stages at one sitting. Ruled out of order.
(3) Griffith moved that the standing orders be so far
suspended as to allow of a beginning being made with.
the Bill. Excitement. Debate. Moved that there
should be no more debate. Division. Carried by 51 to 37.
(4) Dick moved that Griffith be no further heard.
Division. Defeated by 73 to 13.
(5) Griffith moved that there now be a division on his
motion (referred to in paragraph 3). Division. Defeated-
by 50 to 39.
That left things practically where they were.
More crocodile tears by George Reid :
Suppose that England shut out six Australians from
her shores, and passed an Act to stop Australian ships-
trading in British waters, wouldn’t it be icie for England
under such circumstances to talk of undying friendship
for Australia? And yet Mr. Deakin, who was doing all
this sort of thing to Great Britain, claimed support on.
the plea of his loyalty to the Empire.
The first part of the statement is, of course, an
implication that Australia shut out six English-
men (the famous Six Hatters), and as they weren’t.,
shut out the statement is, by implication, a
shabby slander. Also it is an implication
that Australia shuts British ships out of
Australian waters, which is a he by in-
ference. As for the possibility of Eng-
land shutting out Australian ships from its
shores, England does much worse things than
that already. It declares that an Australian is
unfit to be Governor of his own State. It shuts,
out Australia from all share in the government of
the empire. And Australian laws are subjected
to the veto of a Parliament in which Australia,
has no vote or voice, which is flat despotism and
an outrage on every principle of Constitutional
government.
THE FINANCIAL
TROUBLE.
Signor Rosano, Minister-
for Finance in the Italian
Government which came
into office about a week
ago, has committed suicide with a revolver at Naples.—
Cablegram
The poor man, on his appointment, opened the
public safe, took one wild look at the deficit, and
then decided that death was better than finance.

VITADATIO
Another Victory for the Great
Herbal Remedy.
READ MR. RUDDOCK’S TESTIMONY.
192 Elizabeth-street, North Richmond,
Melbourne, April 22nd, 1901.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Dear Sir, —It affords me very great pleasure to let .you
know what Webber’s VITADATIO has done for me. Two
years ago last January I was brought home to my wife
very’ ill; a very peculiar feeling came over me, which
caused me to become quite helpless. My wife sent for a
doctor ; he ordered me to be painted with iodine. This
was done for seven weeks, and he (the doctor) then
ordered me to the Alfred Hospital. After being there for
two months, the doctor sent me home, stating that I was
too weak to go under an operation, and said that I must
go for a trip in the country. I did so, and returned
home slightly better; but after a few days I became much
worse vyitb pneumonia, and after suffering six months
between life and death it was decided that I should go to
the Sanatorium for Consumptives at Echuca. My wife
interviewed the lady secretary of the Sanatorium,
stationed at Kew, and, after examination by the doctors
they pronounced me a fit patient for that institution, as
I was full of consumption, but that I could not get in for
a fortnight, as there would not be a vacant bed till then.
At this time, Mr. Campbell, grocer and wood and coal
merchant, of Vere and Nicholson-streets, Abbotsford, and
now of Fairfield, urged me very strongly’ to try Vitadatio,
which I did. The first upset me very’ much, but
by r the time I had finished the second bottle I was able to
get out of bed, and on taking the third bottle I felt so
improved that I decided not to go to the Echuca
Sanatorium. My 7 wife took that message to the secretary
to that effect. I continued with Vitadatio, and after
taking nine bottles I was able to go to work, so I called
on Mr. Wallbridge, carrier, Lincoln-street, North
Richmond (my last employer) and started at once, and
have been there ever since. I do very heavy work
delivering coal. I feel strong and healthy, and can truth-
fully say that Vitadatio has saved mv life, and the least I
can do is to hand you this testimonial. It is one year
and eight months since I took the last bottle of Vitadatio.
I have lived in this locality for many 7 years and am well
known. I will give further information to any’one calling
at m.v above address as regards my illness and cure by
Vitadatio, and you can refer to any of the undermentioned
names, who are quite prepared to substantiate my state-
ment.
Yours faithfully,
THOMAS O. RUDDOCK,
E. Ruddock,
28 St. Phillip-st., Abbotsford.

Varian and Hunter-sts., Abbotsford.

“ B.” from Melbourne :
Say, Sydney, we don’t want your Dill-Macky. Take
him back and drown him in the harbor. On a recent
Saturday evening I was strolling around Bourke-street
way, and every couple of yards I was falling over a police-
man. Some of them looked at me with a suspicious eye,
and then when I scanned them suspiciously I could see a
baton up each man’s sleeve. Also there was a crowd
gathering, and a lot of respectable-looking middle-class
people were wearing red, white, and blue rosettes.
“What’s happening?” I asked a policeman. “Dill-
Macky.” Now, when I was in Sydney, I got
to know that one of the disadvantages of Har-
bortown was that there Dill-Macky was continually
happening, but I never expected him to happen in
Melbourne. Then I wandered round into Exhibition-
street, and along to the Protestant Hall, on which the
Grand Old Flag was flying. There was a great crowd
there, and a small regiment of mounted troopers. I went
up to a man who looked like a Luther or Knox or Calvin
dressed up in present-day clothes, and wearing a big
rosette. “ Say, what’s all the row about?” “ It’s them
accursed Romish dogs,” quoth Calvin. “ What are they
doing?” I asked. “They’re doing nothing,” he says;
“ but, if they could, they’d assassinate Dr. Dill-Macky,
who has kindly ” “But,” I interrupted, “wouldn’t
it be a good idea to get rid of a pest like him?” Then
Luther began to fume, and he called me a Romish dog,
and I told him I was a Buddhist—and left.

Touching the comedy of “ Monsieur Beaucaire," does
anyone remember that when Napoleon Bonaparte, aged
19, thought of becoming a novelist, he published “ The
Supper of Beaucaire,” a crude performance (though not
without signs of talent), for which the printer was never
paid, owing to lack of public support? When, later in
life, Nap. took to writing military bulletins, he proved a
brilliant Hedonist.

At Poverty Point.
" Beth ” : Thus “ 0.K.” on two great singers (Bulletin
15/10/’03): “ Comparisons drawn between Melba and Ada
Crossley are absurdly unfair to the greater singer,” &o.
“O.K.V’par as a brief for Melba as a woman, boiled
down, is a suggestion that the contralto should not be
loved as much as she is, inasmuch as she is still unspoilt
and lovable, and that the soprano should be loved more,
inasmuch as her lovable qualities have been dissipated
and she was “ long ago hardened.” Also that Melba is
really a nicer woman than she appears to be, be-
cause she tries to be and fails, and that Crossley, being
naturally nice, is consequently less nice than Melba!
In another “Poverty Point” par. “0.K.” says: “One
gets so tired of hearing that Ada Crossley is a good
woman. The same advertisement was uttered on behalf
of Antoinette Sterling, but it carried no weight, because
.... she was fat.” Does “ 0.K.” believe that Ada
Crossley is not a good woman ? Or does he mean that
she is a good woman, and is compelled to remind the
public of this toisupply some deficiency in her singing?
And anyhow, why is Madame Sterling dragged in ? And
was she good because she was fat, or fat because she was
good, or bad because she was fat, or good and fat
accidentally, or fat because she was bad? Anyhow,
Melba did not make people out here love her, and
Crossley does.
“A Flab": Concerning “J.A.O.’s" remarks (B.
29/10/’O3) on Ada Crossley and sacred music, it is
surely a mistake to select music from the operas
in order to illustrate what contralto music really
is. As a rule, operatic music has been written
for phenomenal voices, and in some cases the part
almost disappeared with the singer. For example,
the extremely high music written for “ The Queen of
Night” in Mozart’s “ Zauberfloto ” was written for a
special singer, and when she retired the opera had to be
shelved until Carlotta Patti (Adelina’s sister) and lima de
Murska came along with their three-octave organs, and
gave the great work another show. In “Un Hallo in
Maschera” the music for the contralto, or rather second
soprano, runs up to B in alt, long-sustained notes, too;
and the same can be said of the second lady’s part in
“ The Huguenots,” and that of Maria in the “ Daughter of
the Regiment.” As for sacred music, that is a
sort of musical Mrs. ’Arris—there is really “no sich a
person (or thing) at all.” Set to suitable words, the
“ Cujus Animam ” in Rossini’s “Stabat Mater” would
make an excellent love song, and the duet, “ Quis est
homo?” could easily he fitted into “ Semiramide ” instead
of the “Giorni d’ Orrore,” while the ‘‘Salve Dimora”
from “ Faust ” would make an excellent offertorium for
the Mass service if suitable Latin words were arranged
for it.
A CONCERTO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hush ! Straying fingers on the ivory keys,
Wayward and fitful as a summer’s breeze,
Send down the shivering silences a web
Of pulsing notes and broken chords that ebb
And flow in tangled rhythm ; then a cry,
Monotonous —and, ere the echoes die,
There comes the music of a strange, wild song—
A ghost of melody—that from the throng
Of wandering sounds breaks forth, and thin and clear.
Like a bird’s call that o’er some moon-lit, mere
Wakens the night—wild as a cloud’s swift path
O'er wind-whipped skies, above a sea of wrath.
A song so shapeless, twisted, almost vile,
Yet all-alluring as a siren’s smile ;
Plaintive and pleading as a wind that shrills
At twilight in a land of mist-wrapped hills.
Then one long note that, like a sob of pain,
Thrills to the very centre of the brain.
The wailing echoes madden as they throng
Swift on its piercing path—and then the song
Flows on. But now, all other thoughts above,
The burden of its melody is Love.
But Love primeval ; Love uncivilised ;
Love almost monstrous, unidealised.
A theme some Cave-man of a long-dead age
Might to his mate have sung ere yet a page
Of the Great Book of Time was read and turned,
While in a strange-starred sky a live moon burned.
Heavy with sorrow and vague half-felt fears,
Yet speaking nothing that could move to tears,
With even rhythm now the clear notes fall,
Thin, yet persistent; as ’neath willows tall,
Through damp, dank mosses runs the trickle cool
Of water dripping in some fern-wreathed pool.
Now in a mist of sound they seem to fade,
Drowned in dim echoes that themselves have made ;
Then the whole wearied cadence sinks to rest,
Hush 1 One last echo stirs on Silence’ breast.
N B.W.
CHARLES E. HOWELL.

At the end of May, 1903, John Walker and

Operations will be very soon commenced at
Manly by the “Water Chute and Amusements, Ltd.”
The principal attraction will, of course, be the great
Canadian Chute itself, but in addition there will be a
“Camel-Back Toboggan” and a “Fiery Dragon”—a
miniature railway, contrived to represent a monstrous
dragon. Liberal musical entertainment will also be pro-
vided, and it is intended to have Skater v. Skater, Cyclist
v. Skater, and Toboggan races, for prizes—such as proved
so attractive at Adelaide.*
Woman’s most natural ambition is to look as
sweet as possible. And that thisambition maybegratified
toitsfullest possible extent, a wise individual invented the
P.D. Corset. The new P.D., as everyone should know,
creates a contour in keeping with the requirements of the
prevailing fashion, giving the wearer a perfectly graceful
and artistic figure—“the bust well forward and the
shoulders well hack.” This is the ideal figure in the eyes
of Modern Culture. And as every right-thinking woman
strives after the Ideal, it follows, as a matter of course,
that the perfected P.D. is displacing old styles of corset,
even in the most conservative circles.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Melbourne people are wanting to know what
was the “ game ” of the police who organised the so-called
raid on Wren’s Tote, at Collingwood. Nothing came of
the “ raid ” except public contempt for the absurdity of
it. Nobody was arrested. Wren’s business proceeded as
usual within eyeshot of the police, who occupied the old
premises, and Wren presumably continued his friendly
relations with ’tecs in general and some ’tecs in par-
ticular.
Meanwhile, the legalisation of the tote in Vic-
toria seems further off than ever. The illicit machine
which works in a mysterious way, yielding enormous
profits to the original Wren (and to sundry parasite specu-
lators who pay Wren’s prices) is practically under the
protection of the law. It continues to flourish more
and more, merely because the racing clubs are prohibited
from setting up a respectable tote which would
deal fairly with the public and the charities.
A large percentage of Wren’s income is drawn from
punters who don’t even get a run for their money. The
tote swallows up all the money “invested” on non-
starters, and the prices it returns for “ win and a place”
are absurdly unsatisfactory, under the conditions. If the
ordinary, uninformed punter were not an ass he would
soon get tired of betting with Wren on the “all in"
principle, by which he is tempted to lose his money on,
perhaps, two, or four, or six non-starters before he has
the luck to find a winner at 10 to 1. Congratulating him-
self on the “ splendid ” odds of fifty bob to five, the
average ass punter forgets that he has sacrificed many
previous shillings in vain attempts to spot a runner— let
alone a winner.
* * * •
That Sydney betting-shopkeeper who eloped
without paying up was originally a newsboy. From that
he worked up to being a sort of newsagent, and then
■worked agencies in flowers, icecream, lollies, and other
street lines. Next, he became a betting-shop tout and
then a full-blown “ bookie.” He is said to have got away
with about £IOOO.
* * * *
“J.A.C.”: The welcher is getting a little too
strong at suburban pony-meetings. The other day, on
the “ outer-outer ” at Kensington, a “ lucky ” punter, on
going for his money, was kicked on the jaw, and
told, “ There, you’re pyde ! ” Ticket-snatching is too
common an occurrence to call for special notice.
* * * *
History repeated itself when Billali won the
Williamstown Cup on Monday, after getting well beaten
at Flemington on the Saturday. Last year Vanity Fair
and Eleanor lost a lot of money for the punters on the
fourth day of the V.R.O. meeting, and then went to
Williamstown and dead-heated for the Cup.
** * *
Owner “Bob” Phillips and his friends are
quite persuaded that Abundance’s disappointing display
in the Melbourne Cup was due to bad luck. A reserve
price of 1750 guineas was put upon the four-year-old when
he was offered for sale last week, but the bidding stopped
a long way short of four figures. The public “form” of
Abundance this season is still a very disputable question.
When he beat Wakeful and Ibex at Caulfield there were
others—Postillion and Emir—which shared the same dis-
tinction, and nobody can deny that the subsequent
running of Postillion and Emir discounted the apparent
merit- of Abundance’s one win. It will be interesting to
see what the Sydney and Melbourne handicappers make
of the big horse if he is entered for the autumn cups.
** * *
“San Remo” : Please crack this nut for me.
Two horses, Optimist and Jo.vance, dead-heated for the
Hopeful Stakes, rim at Eagle Farm. Self, chum and
another pal attended thereat. I put £2 on Optimist at
evens, and chum laid £5 to £4 ; pal invested £1 on
Joyance, getting 4 to 1. My ticket was pencilled £4,
chum’s £9, and pal’s £5. Owners agreed to divide.
Bookies called “Divide! divide!” and proceeded to
halve face value of tickets with punters. I got back my
£2. Pal received £2 10s. and chum £4 10s. of his
fiver. Now, how can this be right ? We three backed
winners, or, rather, J winners. I won nothing, pal won
305., and chum lost 10s. Query: Is a bookie a thief,
swindler, or pickpocket,- or merely an honest man
afflicted with mental aberration? We argue that unless
a horse loses, a bookie has no right to put a thieving
finger upon a punter’s stake. For a dead-heat he must
pay out half the amount which he would lose if that
horse had won, together with the stake. According to
the laws of justice I. Mo swindled me out of £l, pal of
10s., and chum was robbed of £2 10s. Ohum in particular
is tied up in a knot. He backs a winner, and Ikey scoops
10s. over the transaction.
** * •
Dick Brad field’s system of training horses to
stay rather than sprint seems to have been very effective
in the case of old Combat. Until Bradfield took charge
of him a few months ago, the son of Carnage had never
gone further than 11 furlongs successfully, though he was
several times tested over a longer distance. At the
recent V.R.C. meeting Combat’s lean neck came to the
fore in a couple of 12 furlong races after he had finished a
respectable fifth in the Cup. One always thinks of him
as a gee-gee that needs a course of Sandow exercises to
put muscle on his neck.
* * • > ' «■
At Y uille’s sale of thoroughbreds in Melbourne
last week 25 out of 37 lots were knocked down to buyers
for markets beyond Victoria, anyhow. Several of them
will leave Australia. There isn’t much Vic. money for
racers except among the little group of capitalists who
support Scobie’s stable ; and they were not taking any
at this sale.
** * *
Sydney Grafton filly Graft, who won Victorian
Club Handicap, at Caulfield, Saturday, after a desperate
finish with Avalon, has shown remarkable form down
Smith. Within past several weeks she has secured
two races at Flemington, one at Sandown Park,
and one at Caulfield, and her party must have profited
materially in consequence.
*♦ * *
Scobie’s scoop through V.A.T.C. and V.R.C.
Spring meets didn’t reach amount anticipated through
his opening successes, still it was substantial, totalling
£7668 (£4950 at Caulfield and £2718 at Flemington).
Information concerning money expended by that stable
in entrance fees, forfeits, sweeps and jockeys’ remunera-
tion would be interesting.
Printed that Wakeful’s winnings rank next to
Carbine’s. But Wakeful never had a Melbourne Cup
haul to swell her accumulation ; also when Carbine
succeeded in securing that prize it was nearly double
present sum. Owner M'Donald’s good judgment in, some
time ago, refusing £6OOO for his remarkable mare has
been amply endorsed ; he has since made that in prize-
money, and stud possibilities are big.
* * • *
Poor display by and heavy loss on Crick’s
Ossian and Oblivion in Melb. Cup (former of whom was to
win “ by a street ”) has apparently stimulated rather than
checked their owner. His trainer has been instructed to
put a lot of youngsters by Positano (bought at recent
sales) into work straightway, which means that
W. P. C. will be up to his eyes in the turf game ere the
season closes. At the time of the purchases ’twas
thought and printed that buyer purposed sending his
newly-acquired neddies to the S’African market.
Something refreshing. Told- that a Granville
(N.S.W.) resident, who drew Wakeful in Melb. Cup sweep,
wired owner (L. M'Donald) an offer of £3OOO to nothing.
Mr. M‘Donald declined, with thanks, and added that the
mare was a sure starter and would nearly win. A £3OOO
offer usually brings a bluffing demand for another thou-
sand, otherwise no start.
* * * *
Running of M.L. Derby, the other day, reminds
that only thrice (1885, 1888, and 1900) before in 26 years
did Mr. Stead fail to start one of his horses. Altogether,
he has won 11 Derbies, and had horses in places ten
times—only twice did his reps, run unplaced. Owner
(Mr. George Clifford) of second (Treadmill) this year has
often tried to win the race, but failed always.
* * * «
Ikey Mo.’s gains must be huge in Westralia
when he can afford to pay 70gs. license fee at big meets
and 20gs. for odd gatherings. If it didn’t pay him he
wouldn’t stop, as Ikey never allows patriotism to interfere
with pizness.
* * * *
Winner (Roseal) of M.L. Derby brought 78gs.
as a yearling.
* • * *
Good winner (Australasia) at recent Otaki and
Wellington (M.L.) gatherings was once “hacked” by
General Babington and then sold to a blacksmith, who,
after failing to get a race out of him, parted with the son
of The Australian for a trifle.
* * * *
Apropos horse racing, a Queensland Parliamen-
tary committee recommends that the minimum racing
distance should be j-mile and the lightest weight 7stone.
* * • *
Yankee exchanges record that champion trotter
Lou Dillon’s mile, in 2min., wasn’t allowed to stand long.
Cresceus had a cut at it last month and brought figures
down to 1.59 J. Lou Dillon people aver that she can, and
will, ere long, cover the journeyin 1.58, which is a second
better than Dan Patch’s world’s pacing best.
* * • »
Late Count de Rossi was prominent as a turfite
25 years ago. His big nag (Lord Burghley) annexed
several events, and after running second (in Metrop.,
1879) to Secundus, and third (in Melb. Oup the following
year) to Grand Flaneur and Progress, was sold to his
trainer, Mr. Tom Ivory, who, a little while later, won
Wagga Oup with his new purchase.
* * * *
Scottish King’s finish in Spring Handicap at
recent V.R.O. meet topped anything done on same track
for many a day, and was a wonder in view of previous
failures, which left the impression that he wasn’t near
the nag estimated. Now cramped odds will be the rule
until S.K. misses badly once or twice—which is quite
probafile, seeing he is brother to that bad disappoint-
ment Kinglike, who at three years promised so much.
• * * *
Big horse-racing events offer excellent oppor-
tunities for passing forged notes, Mo being frequently
victimised to a considerable extent, and often—uninten-
tionally—passing notes on to backers. Last V.R.O. meet
saw several £1 notes accepted for tens, and great was the
howl of Israel in consequence. Forgeries were excellent.
Union Bank notes were operated upon, “One” being
obliterated and “Ten” substituted so cleverly that
only holding the note before a strong light gave the
fraud away. An exactly similar fraud, also with Union
Bank notes, was worked on almost every shop in Dunedin
one Saturday night about 23 years ago.
* * * *
“Petramos”: At Adelaide Zoo, t’other day, I
stood me before the baboon’s cage and thought to gather
in wisdom, but alas! the baboon had been ruined by a
craze. I held my paw to his and proffered him sweet
nuts in a brotherly fashion, but he heeded not. He
would look at the nut sadly, shake his head, and then
begin -to rub himself vigorously. He was training.
Striking an attitude he would pause for a moment and
then commence to walk, slowly at first, and then rapidly.
Round and round the cage he would go, and at last,
apparently exhausted, he would throw himself flat on the
floor. In a little time he would repeat the performance.
The Bulletin said the walking craze had died in Adelaide
—but it hasn’t. The monkeys have got it.
* * * *
M.L. long-distance runner H. C. Murray badly
fractured the Australasian record recently, getting home
in 56min. 16secs., or 40secs. quicker than the previous
time.
• * * *
Warner missed winning the S.A. match through
two causes. One was the fine batting of Clem Hill and
young Jennings, who made all his bowlers look cheap.
Local critics seem to think they have found another Hill
in the colt, who is only 19, and scored 26 and 77 (both not
out) with the utmost confidence and in perfect style.
But the main cause was the greed of the local Cricket
Association in insisting that the second day should fall
on the holiday (Monday). The English team had landed
on the previous Monday, and the match might, and
should, have started on Friday, as usual. There should
be no such thing as a drawn game in first-class cricket in
Australia; and the 3J days allowed for this match would
mean a draw in nine cases out of ten.
* * * *
Mayor M'Eacharn, lunching the English
cricketers in Melbourne, said he hoped there would he no
waste of time in the matches, but that the players would
show the same punctuality in getting to their posts “as
anyone should when sitting down to meals.” The
mayoral mind runneth ever on the matter of meals.
* * » *
Cricket matches are mostly won or lost after 10
o’clock at night. Warner talks a lot about the necessity
for “ playing the game ”—in the field. A captain’s first
care should be to see that his men play the game outside
of the field. This time, however, the visitors will start
their test matches with the comparative advantage of not
having to meet men who are battling for inclusion in a
team that sails for England at the end of the season.
Maclaren’s Eleven w r as opposed to the keenest of triers
in every instance—men who took every possible pre-
caution against failure. They were playing for places in
the next Australian Eleven. This time there is no such
incentive to steadiness on the part of the local champions,
and some of them, perhaps, will take whisky-and-soda
risks.
* * » •
Fielder, the young fast bowler of Warner’s
team, is rather a disappointment. He takes as long a
run to the crease as Tom Richardson took, but his action
is not so impressive, and he doesn’t rival Terrible Tom in
the pace or “devil” of his bowling. All the same he
threatens to be more dangerous on a dry wicket than
Relf, who gets a good pace with comparatively little
exertion. Nr ..
* * * *
Harry Trott, malting his re-appearance against
an English Eleven after long years, got a reception from
all round the M.C.C. ground. Same old Trott; same old
bingey on him. He was doing pretty well at the wickets
up to the time when Rhodes took the ball. With his
fourth delivery the slow left-hander basely deceived
Harry—the veteran smote the air, and the wicket-keeper
closed the incident.
Hayward gives one the impression that he is
likely to bat a little above his “ form” of 1901-2, but his
want of resolution at the outset of an innings is as
noticeable as ever. A sound, cautious batsman —with no
squareness in the
Rhodes “came off” brilliantly enough in his
first attempt against the Victorians. Until Monfries and
Horan got together the Yorkshireman was quite a
conquering hero, but the newcomers played him with
more confidence and he was taken off, remaining off for
some time with his record at 4 for 26. Finally he came
on again to break the last partnership, and off his second
ball Collins was caught out. For disposing of weak
batsmen Rhodes promises to he as effective as Peel, of
vore. on anv sort of Australian wicket.
* » * *
What a valuable cricketer is Braund —at his
best! In the second innings of the Soustralians, when
the English bowling was coming easy, Braund made three
catches, two of them brilliant, and got another man
caught off his own bowling. In the first innings he had
the best bowling average—2 for 15.
* * * .
Hill’s 115 against the Englishmen in Adelaide
recalls his 200 against Stoddart’s team in the first match
of the ’97 tour. That stands as his record against t’other-
side visitors, though he had previously made 150 not out
against Richardson, Lockwood, Peel, and Briggs, which
was perhaps a bigger performance. He was 19 then.
...»
Though the team to represent N.S.W. against
England, Friday this week, has been subjected to much
criticism, taken by and large it is at least a good batting
combination—possibly as good as could be chosen.
Exception is made to presence of S. E. Gregory, Howell,
Hickson and Fisher. Services of Gregory and Howell in
past teams rendered their claims second to none,
despite that neither has shown the requisite form so far
this season. Howell, particularly, has been a rank
failure in club cricket, but it was so with him once or
twice before, and yet he subsequently proved more than
fit for a place in every one of several important matches.
Hickson undoubtedly won for N.S.W. the match against
Victoria in Sydney last year; up to now, however,
present season’s club cricket has shown that he has been
anything but the same man. Though Fisher’s inclusion
reads like an experiment, it must be remembered he is a
first-class all-round man—a capable bat, a sure field, and
a steady, medium - paced bowler. Both Fisher and
Hickson came from the Church of England school,
where there are still one or two other possibles for big
cricket. Overlooking of Burwood player Mackay is re-
grettable—he richly merited better treatment.
* * * *
Match, England v. Victoria, Monday, ended in
a decisive defeat for home side. At 443 for 8 wickets,
Warner, in accordance with an arrangement made before
starting, declared the innings closed ; Victorians then had
281 to get to avert a one-innings rout. First five made
170, but the rest only managed 26 which, together with 14
sundries, produced 210, leaving visitors best by an innings
and 71 runs. Though Victorians were among the weakest
that ever represented their State the Britishers are
proving a tough lot. Up to date they have lost 15 wickets
for 926—an average verging on 62 per wicket. Lesson of
S.A. and V. matches is that cricket in those States has
deteriorated considerably, or that their form is very much
off, or that Warner has brought an exceptionally strong
combination evidence of its batting power comes
through fact that Rhodes (who got 1100 runs last season)
goes in 10th man.
* • • *
Concerning Sydney first-grade cricket, Satur-
day, Sydney and Redfern clubs provided something that
puzzled. On a batsman’s wicket, M'Beth and Marsh sent
Redfern pavilionwards for 77, and Sydney looked a sure
winner in consequence; but Johnson, anew bowler in
first-grade matches, settled Quist and Chapman in his
first over, and in the second bagged Richardson—the
three costing but 1 run. The whole side went down for
63 and, ere stump draw, two Redfern men were out for 5.
Twenty-one wickets fell for 145 runs. North Sydney,
opposed to Central Cumberland, notched 494 for 9 out.
Howell was among the bowlers. Hopkins put up a
brilliantly-accumulated 183, and Hickson (another mem-
ber of N.S.W. combination to meet the Englishmen, and
whose inclusion has been kicked against) compiled 93.
Burwood set up 330 for Paddington to beat, Garnsey con-
tributing 82, Hogue 68, and Barbour 64. ’Varsity, play-
ing Leichhardt-Balmain, made 326 for 7 wickets. Waddy
contributed 146, and he and new State rep., Fisher, added
145 for the sixth wicket. Fisher was 53 (not out) at the
close of play.
• • * •
“P.”: Wellington (M.L.) “two-up” school,
reported in Bulletin (8/10/’O3) as being impregnable, was
raided by the police on 24th October, and 35 kippists
were handcuffed and marched away with ignominy. It
appears that the force had had emissaries in the camp for
some weeks past “ two-upping ” with much earnestness,
and it had been arranged that a descent would be made
at the time stated. As one member of the school entered
from the street the doorkeeper was seized and gagged
before he could press the electric alarm button. Second
doorkeeper was collared by one of the inside emissaries,
and the door opened to admit the ’tecs. By the time
these reached the room above, the members had got the
hang of the situation, and no sign of “two-up” was
visible—hut a prayer-meeting was being held. The
premises were those of the Olympic Club, ostensibly an
athletic school, and to bear this out there were clubs,
dumbbells, rapiers and a punching-ball lying about
promiscuously. The keepers of the Olympic elected to he
tried by jury, and the crowd all pleaded “ not guilty ”
among them “ Snowy ” Sturgeon (boxing instructor),
who was recently downed in Sydney by “Soldier"
Thompson.
* * *
Signs are plentiful that there’ll shortly be a
mild boom in Australian ’rastling. Two Mahomme-
dans have held Australian supremacy between them
for years, and their manner of rubbing it in by challenging
to throw all comers thrice before being once thrown
themselves, has evoked responses from M.L. and W.A.
Backer of W.A. champion (Scott) offers to support him
to a big tune against any “ flaming nigger in the world ”
and has followed this up with a £SO deposit. Whilom
top-notcher Perryman (in W.A. now) who only suffered
defeat once, at the hands of “ Dan Stewart ”
(McLeod) when he swooped on Australia from ’Murka
some years ago, also accepts the gage and promises to find
big money for a match. Meanwhile both Hindus are
willing and will accommodate the lot in turn and take a
deal of putting down.
* * * *
It grows more apparent that visiting bikists
invariably beat Australians by tactics. Last season
Taylor was successful chiefly because he knew when
to make his final rush for the tape ; now Lawson and
McFarland are scoring by similar headiness. The
Australians have had plenty of opportunities to profit by
the tact of visiting cyclists, but, somehow, they don’t
seem to learn anything. A N.S.W. paper is advising
bikists that “ the only way they will beat Lawson is to
cultiyate a ‘jump,’ and, by sticking to his wheel like
grim death, moving when he moves, and sprinting when
he sprints.” That is what Australian riders have been
doing for a long time ; but the trouble is to find out
just when Lawson is going to do his marvellous “ jump.”
* * * *
The American cyclists already in Sydney are
intent on making a splash for prizes before Major Taylor
arrives. At present handicap form, and by the way they
scoop in scratch races, the best of the Australians are but
second-raters in comparison. Taylor may not be the
all-conquering flyer he is made out to be; still, his
presence will, perhaps, liven things up somewhat.
Yankee bikist Lawson headed the list of winners at the
recent Sydney meeting, raking in £llO 10s., and, con-
sidering that he raced at every meet day and night, the
meagre prize-money isn’t much for visitors to rave over.
T’other Yank, McFarland, won £3O 10s.
» * * *
N.S.W. Bike League troubles are still unsettled.
So-called “executive” people, supporting deposed sec.
Brannigan, insist that they are the rulers, and have some
support from country affiliations, while the “ council,”
whose mouthpiece is M'lntosh, treats opposing faction
with disdain. Both sides have been bidding for recog-
nition from other States. Victoria suggested a general
election, and promised support to the officers appointed.
Proposal was adopted, and result announced within the
last day or two. “ Council ” sec. M‘Intosh alleges that of
31 officials declared chosen only seven are league mem-
bers, and four were nominated without authority. Also
stated that no metropolitan members of league interested
themselves in the matter —voting came principally from
a few country clubs. Things are as far off being straigh-
tened as ever.
* * * *
Sydney National Sporting Club’s quest for new
blood caused management to match “Robbo” and ex-
middleweight champion Mick Dunn’s pet pupil, Harold
Smith, last week. Though principals had done well in
previous engagements, they fell off lamentably pitted
against each other, although splendidly trained and in
superb fix. “ Robbo ” showed more mobility and
generally outclassed bigger and stronger Smith, whose
operations lacked “devil” and whose blows were pushed
rather than hit. From beginning to end the thing was
deadly tame. In the 6th round a hard punch in the
stomach, as he came in, knocked what little ginger he
possessed out of “ Robbo ” and he kept away mostly till
shortly after the next opened when, staggering suddenly,
he nursed an apparently painful arm. Later twas
explained that “Robbo” had put his left elbow out,
though how was a mystery. Neither will make a
champion.
In the Victorian Assembly the other night
those two excited Tories, Swinburne and
McCutcheon, raged about the iniquity of the
Income Tax. One of them spluttered out that
“to his own knowledge hundreds of thousands of
pounds had been driven out of the State and had
gone to Maoriland ,” while the other backed him up
by giving a specific instance of a “firm that had
sent ,£4OOO to Maoriland to invest in place of
leaving it here.’.’ These capitalists, be it notecb
were so enamored of ‘ ‘ Socialistic Legislation
that they actually sent their spare cash to be in-
vested in the most socialistic country within sight !
Surely McKetchup and the other ’Pootras must
be Socialists in disguise.
Dill Macky’s mission to Melbourne, to
dare the Scarlet Woman to tread on the Yellow
Pup’s tail, didn’t amount to a row of pins.
Gaud Mayor Gillott hadn’t the sense to veto the
ridiculous “procession.” First he said “Yes,”
then he said they mustn’t march, but might stroll;
finally he said, they might scramble through any
way they liked if they wore no regalia. And
when the two or three thousand youths and weedy
adults, protected by 20 mounted constables and
60 of Irvine’s aged foot police, finally marched
through the city hardly anybody stopped to look
at them or took his pipe from his mouth to ask
what it all meant. Not a public man came near
them save the few who trade on the game—stodgy
Simon Fraser, Ramsay, and one or two others.
There is no doubt that David Syme is
still the best hater on this continent. Irvine’s
announcement that he was going to throw up the
Victorian Premiership was accompanied by a
stinging proclamation that he “ did not think it
necessary to take a certain gentleman connected
with a certain newspaper into his counsels,”
although he had undoubtedly done so during the
railway strike, and at other times when his
Government faced trouble. Therefore the Agk
bade him farewell with some nicely chosen
epithets, said he was suffering from “mental
failure,” and after speaking of his “exhibitions of
bad temper and lack of good judgment, which
plainly indicate that he will act. wisely by seeking
retirement and rest,” solaced him by stating that
“it is not given to everyone to possess the
temperament and capacity that will successfully
carry a man through a political campaign.” The
rapidity with which the Age discovered that
Irvine was a statesman was nothing to the speed
with which it subsequently discovered that he
was not.
OUP DAY IN THE BUSH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The strong north wind, a doleful blast,
Comes down upon me from the hill 5
The wild galahs go screaming past,
A wagtail’s on my window-sill.
And dreaming here in discontent,
I watch the lazy wreaths of smoke
That, upward from my lone couch sent,
Are vain as ’tis to back a moke.
Still I’d be there if only she—
The lady with the money-bags—
Had cast a handful unto me,
That I might put it on the nags.
From this weird, weeping solitude
One brief, warm, lively, reckless week
I’d have where exiles do not brood,
But where the lost their shekels seek.
(That hot north wind howls through the frame
Of many a mental skeleton,
That one time was a horse of fame— *
The “stiff ” I put my money on.) ,
Now in this God-forgotten spot,
Far from all human kith and kin,
An exile, I can’t put the pot
Upon the horse I know will win.
vio.
WILL JARRETT.


Franz Josef Lager

That Sydney Government House young
tnan has been acquitted of the charge of stealing
his suffering country’s typewriter and giving it to
his best girl. As the Judge expressed it: —
The case really narrowed itself down to the question—
Did the accused, in his madness of love for the girl Gates,
give her the typewriter ?
Love breaks out in curious ways : The Bulletin
\ aguoly recollects a person who, in the frenzy of
his affection, gave his girl two sacks of onions, a
sun bonnet and a bull-dog. In the present
instance, however, the loss of the machine was
satisfactorily explained.

“ Rimutaka ” : During the debate in the
M.L. House of Reps, on the new Licensing Act,
members so lost control of themselves that-
they quite overlooked the supposed subject of
debate and raised a howling discussion consisting
mostly of descriptions of other members’ allegedly
immoral ancestors. One alleged legislator in-
formed the House in a grieved, big voice that the
father of another “allegator” was a thief. This-
shocked the Speaker into a mild reproof. The-
end of a mutual argument on the mutual back-
slirlings of two of the House’s notorieties ended!
outside in the lobby, where the bigger of the two
severely plugged the other on the nose. Immedi-
ately the House was adjourned, and outside the
Speaker’s control, a glowering and angry member
thre itened with profanity to “wring the blanky
neck’’ of another, and with abuse, curses,; spilt
gore and Dick’s awful temper the scene was re-
markable.

A miserable proof of the demoralisation of
N.S.W. Assembly turned up last week, when
Premier See, plainly in revenge for the Opposi-
tion’s criticisms cm the dealings of his Government
with the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co., of which he
is chief director, wasted the time of the House
with silly and feeble accusations about a wharf
which Opposition leader Carruthers persuaded a
dead and forgotten Government to build some 18
years ago. The session is supposed to end in about
two weeks; but so far nothing of the least value
has been done; and the Premier is too busy throw-
ing ancient clods at his enemy to do anything.

The Bulletin’s Christmas number will be
issued on December 10th, and, as already the rush
for extra copies has set in, early application is
necessary to ensure supplies. It will contain the
largest and best selection of the wprk of the
picture, song and story artists of Australia ever
issued to the public of this continent, and without
it no holiday will be complete.
A confiding Victorian nurse latejy lent
to a turf adviser (who used to be a sailor and
probably got his knowledge through seeing the
white horses on the sea in rough weather), for the
purpose of backing horses by an infallible method.
She is now suing for the return of her money, the
infallible method having broken down. It was a
very old method, but the nurse evidently thought
it was quite new. The process looks infallible
enough when it isn’t too closely inspected. You
put, say, £1 on your selection for the first race.
If it loses you put, say, £2 on the next selection.
If it loses you put £4 on next time. The theory
is that you must win some time; when you do.
win you must be a good deal in pocket; and then
you start again at ill and win some more. There
are several flaws, however, which a nurse couldn’t
be expected to notice. If you lose, say, eight times,
in succession, going on the doubling principle,
you are £255 out, and want to stake £256 on the
next horse. You may not have £256 to stake. If
you have it, the bookies may refuse to. bet to any-
thing like that extent on the animal, and you may
fail to get the money on; the bookie is apt to
grow suspicious of an excited person who wants
to stake £256 on a horse in a small handicap. The
scheme is always liable to break down through
some bookie failing to pay when the infallible
person wins. And a person who goes to a race-
course with £511 in notes in his pocket often gets
his pocket picked, sometimes he gets drunk, and
occasionally he has his skull fractured. All these
things knock large holes in the infallible system,
and then distracted nurses fall in.
The dreadful habit that Australia has of
being heir to an immense fortune asserts itself
again; an ancient “hatter,” up Herberton (Q.)
way, it seems, is entitled to £3,000,000. Aus-
tralia, as a whole, has fallen heir, on paper, to
about £100,000,000 (all in England) during the
last two years, and it is doubtful if all these
golden visions have panned out in the aggregate
for one long beer or the value of a Swiss cigar.
CHAMBERLAIN’S COUGH REMEDY,
cuts out the phlegm and germ-infested mucous,
and lets the life-giving oxygen enrich and vitalise

and lungs.*
Coghlan’s annual volume of “ Statistics of
the Six States of Australia and Maoriland ”
for 1903 is now available. Begun a few years ago
as a small pamphlet, the “Statistics ” now reach
100 pages, packed tight with information of the
greatest value regarding the progress or otherwise
of this continent with respect to population, pro-
duction, revenue, debt, expenditure, commerce,
accumulation, and most of those other things as
to which it is necessary to have precise informa-
tion for the intelligent government of the country.
It is about the best-condensed statistical volume
on earth.
To a deputation, demanding that N.S.W.
Leg. Council should be made swallow the Eight
Hours Coal Mines Bill or be itself abolished,
Premier See replied :
When a measure had passed the Legislative Assembly
live times that should be sufficient for the Legislative
Council. When the people expressed their views time
and again upon certain matters, and their wishes were
thwarted, then some means would have to be taken to
rectify that state of things. He would give them the
assurance that he would bring in this measure, if he were
in power, next Parliament.
Next Parliament! There is time to do something
this Parliament, but a barren-minded Ministry is
always afraid to do.any thing, lest it have nothing
left to promise next election. When it has to
live for two Parliaments on one idea it naturally
economises the idea.

“ Wussleean ” writes :—
Had to “ take ” a meeting of the women electors of the
wealthy Melbourne suburb of Hawthorn the other night
It was organised by the alleged Reform League. I ex-
pected to hear a good deal of twaddle, and I did. The
first lady urged frequently that “ first of all God must be
put into our Parliaments”; “only God in our Parlia-
ments can save them from Socialism." Another “ would
rather be put in a wool-press and screwed down tight
than crushed by the juggernaut of Socialism.” It was
also stated that “private charity should be exercised
toward the aged and for the relief of neglected children ;
the Old Age Pensions Act had done more harm than
good.” “ The daughters of the pioneers had joined the
league because it gave them the most room for patriot-
ism.” An excited lady cried out: “A ‘ brickfieider ’ is
needed to blow our shores free from the curse of
Socialism ; the church should cleanse itself of Socialism.”
Although the meeting supported the proposed com-
pulsory re-purchase of land, it also held that the “ sturdy
pioneers ” who own the large estates should be allowed to
“ go'quietly,” and “ their beautiful homes not be broken
up.” Also a dyspeptic-looking gentleman pointed out
the “grave danger in the growth of an Australian
national feeling as opposed to the Imperialistic senti-
ment.” They were a various lot, but they were all solid
on opposing Socialism. And not one of the lot attempted
to explain what he or she meant by Socialism. The
meeting didn’t really know it from a cow.
USEFUL ARTICLE.
Chamberlain’s Pain Balm is a general family
liniment, especially valuable for rheumatism. It
also cures lame back, sprains, bruises, burns, and
scalds. Sold everywhere at Is. 6d.*
Not generally known that Pa Brunker,
who doesn’t look like a fighting man, took lessons
in the art fistic, when a young man, from the giant
pug. Black Perry. The latter, in the fifties,
travelled the country in regal style, giving exhi-
bitions and lessons. Perry was knocked out him-
self later on by a person named Grog who hit
him in the stomach with a bottle.
Poor old Victoria has some marvellous
curios in the way of shire councils. T’other day
the Ovens council set out seriously to assure the
State (per circular) that compulsory land resump-
tion was a sin and a shame, and could not be
tolerated. All other shire councils were invited
to assist in blocking such legislation. In most
cases the circular met with a very cool reception,
but when it reached Ararat it fell among friends,
and was promptly taken to the waistcoats of the
local councillors. The bumptious reactionaries
who bluster round that table spluttered out an
unqualified endorsement of the Ovens doctrine,
and instructed Toutcher, M.L.A., to oppose
Irvine’s proposed land settlement legislation.
Seeing that Ararat’s representative has been one
of Victoria’s most persistent land reformers, the
brassy council hasn’t much hope of having its im-
pudent instructions carried out.

CRY.
When a person suffers from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders
Anaemia
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
Nature is crying aloud for aid. Pain is really Nature’s voice, and is her only method of communicat-
ing with us, therefore pain is a blessing in disguise. By the infliction of the pains attending the
above group of disorders, Nature is calling our attention to the fact that there is something which is
impeding the action of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER,
and which she is unable, by her own efforts, to correct. She is appealing to us, as intelligent human
beings, to bring Science to assist her in the constant struggle which she is making against her enemy
Death. The kidneys and liver, being the most important organs of the human mechanism, have
many natural ways of indicating their disability to perform their functions, and by causing us to
suffer from the disorders mentioned, we are compelled to bring Science to their relief, or to die as the
consequence of neglecting Nature’s appeal.
The Kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly
an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily, all this solid matter leaves the body
dissolved in the urine, but if through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these ir inary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins,
contaminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as ,
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia,
Gravel, Stone, and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are
healthy is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural
color, or has particles floating about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must
immediately be taken to restore their vigor, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or: some of the many
manifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. ThO liver
takes sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again
supply it to the blood, as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which
is insoluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood, corpuscles
which have lived their life and are useful no longer. _ When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer
from some form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice,
Sick Headache, General Debility, and Blood Disorders.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys, and that done by the liver,
that where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys the liver becomes affected in sympathy and
vice versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of those vital
organs which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
Ce. tain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be found
which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive
search for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in compound-
ing -a medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warners Safe Cure ex-
hibits a marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and liver,
and restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all complaints due
to the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys and
liver naturally eliminates tl e poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease. Cures
effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent simply because they are natural. [2O
Apropos Alfred Deakin and “Spooks.’’
John Bowie Wilson, Minister for Mines in three
N.S. Wales Ministries, was a spiritualist pure and
simple, and had the services of the spook fraternity
performed at his burial.
Some curious items appear on the N.S.W.
electoral rolls. At Orange, J. M. Paul, who is a
saddler by occupation, and an Orangeman by
profession, is classed as a female ; while Sergeant
Coyne, of the police, is set down as “ domestic
duties.” And a lady at East Orange is described
as a shunter.
Austen Chamberlain, Chancellor of the
Exchequer, is the youngest man but one, over
a long course of years, who has been the head of
the Treasury. His age is 40. Randolph Churchill
was 37 when he took the post, with leadership of
the House of Commons, in 1886. The ages at
which other Chancellors have taken the office
are : Gladstone, 43; M. Hicks-Beach, 48; Chil-
ders, 55 ; Stafford Northcote, 56; Goschen, 56;
Harcourt, 59 ; Ritchie, 64.
A. O. Sachse (pronounced Sacksie), C.E.,
M.H.C., who was recently returned unopposed for
the N.-E. Victorian Province, when appointed
Minister for Education in the re-shuffling of the
Irvine Cabinet, is the young man who, when he
first arose to. greatness, seriously proposed driving
through his immense electorate, accompanied l.y
hisicharming wife, in a drag drawn by four horses,
to thank each voter personally for returning him.
Sachse knows the precipitous nature of his electo-
rate better now. There are some of his constituents
who could only be thanked per medium of a rope
ladder.
litniipp Green, the most numerous prelate
in Australia, owes his success to his untiring
energy—a rare quality in the tired Australian
church. His practice of playing duets with his
wife is said to have been abandoned because he
fled through the music at such a pace that he was
always at the end of the piece when the bishopess
was half through. His career through the sees is
as breathless as his playing, and if a new arch-
bishopric isn’t created for him to rush through, he
may yet fly off the handle and become a Cardinal.
Bishop Green is a man who simply Can’t Keep
Still. He would be an ideal driver for a coach
that was late and had to get downhill in a hurry.
From London Daily Chronicle re the
Good win case:
Few more cruel hoaxes have been perpetrated than the
bogus advertisement which brought an aged Queenslander
from the Antipodes to England to claim a legacy of
£90,000. No such legacy awaits him, and his case was
rendered harder by the response he obtained from the
Agent-General of his own colony. Armed with a kindly
letter from the Southwark magistrate, Mr. Goodwin
called at the offices of the Agent-General in the hope of
getting a passage back to Queensland —for he is now
penniless. The only reply he could obtain was the advice,
through the mouth of a messenger, to “ get a job as a
stoker.” As Mr. Goodwin is an old man in feeble health
the suggestion could have been nothing more than an ill-
timed joke. The Queensland Government will doubtless
note the treatment accorded to a distressed Queenslander
in London by its represen tavive.
Has Horace Tozer a Tozer to meet this accusation ?
“Iris” writes from Q. Premier Morgan’s
constituency :
Generally thought that Premier Arthur Morgan has a
sound political investment in his constituency of War-
wick. But this is hardly the case. Arthur is not a par-
ticularly strong man, as he is afflicted with a liver which
sometimes slips a cog. On such occasions he is less
affable than usual to the constituent who wants to borrow
from him, and his popularity suffers. However, he has
always had the luck to he opposed by some aspiring
ignoramus. During the campaign the ignoramus, of
course, speaks disrespectfully and generally betrays a
lack of Christian charity towards his opponent. But
Arthur preserves an ominous silence both on the platform
and in his newspaper. When the figures go up the
ignoramus is lost in amazement at his ridiculously small
score, and evinces a desire to dodge further publicity.
But Arthur seeks that ignoramus out, and, supported by
his little brother, Syd., and with the light of battle in
his eye, proceeds to vituperate. He reviews the whole
campaign with unpleasant directness, recalls disagreeable
incidents, and speculates on motives, and when he has
finished there is very little ignoramus left. This habit of
walloping the enemy after the row is over is one of Mor-
gan’s little peculiarities.
D. J. R. Watson, who succeeds the late
Kates as member for Cunningham (Q.), is brother
to John Reay Watson, author of various volumes
of fiction. Last general election D. J.R. tackled
Kates, and received scarcely sufficient votes to
light a pipe with. On that occasion Watson
chartered the only caravan of its kind at large in
the electorate—an affair after the pattern of a
hearse, with palatial glass panels in the sides of it
—and with four red roans hooked on to this vehicle
part of Watson’s committee rushed out some 12
miles to bring in a hairy hatter to vote. They
brought him in, but rounding the street corner
the caravan turned over, and the elector was
heaved out through the glass on to the street.
The red roans cleared on a trip of their own,
knocking daylight into things as they proceeded ;
and the man with the vote picked himself out of
the dust, shook his fist at the fleeing turn-out and
at the ruins of Watson’s committee—some of whom
were still on board the wreck and hanging on for
dear life—and voted for Kates.
Life is not always a circular pleasure track
for the beer-house man. A Sydney publican
suicided lately because a great crowd went daily
past his door and hardly anybody came in. For
months before his death he used to walk round to
opposition grog shanties and peep in at the door
to see what business they were doing. When he
found the bar-counter lined with furry-tongued
drinkers he would return to his own hopeless
show, call the barman a Jonah, kick himself for
being so foolish as to take the house, and go
quietly to bed. One night he took a revolver to
bed, and the beer lever knows him no more.
What a lot the oily hotel broker will have to
answer for when confronted with all the dead Jug-
ginses he put into houses which “were doing a
roaring trade.”
An old pressman’s reminiscence :
Asked once why he voted for the Payment of Members
Bill in N.S.W. Leg. Council, the late W. B. Dailey replied :
“ The truth is I had poor Deniehy in my mind. Well do
I recall how, often, when we were gathered around him
in the Chamber, or in one of the rooms, charmed by his
discourse, tea would be announced, and we would all beg
of him to come along. But he nearly always had the
excuse of a pressing appointment; or a letter to write ;
or an authority to consult in the library ; and it was not
till later on that I discovered that his refusal to join us
was frequently due to the absence of the necessary half-
crown. It stung me moie than words can tell, to think
that a representative of the genius, originality and
legislative aptitude of Deniehy was forced to forsake the
Parliamentary refreshment-room and go hungry, while
searching in the library for points and authorities
for a masterful address in the interests of his country.
Of course, there was only one Deniehy ; but there are
poor men of patriotism, character’ and capacity who
should he encouraged to participate in, rather than be
excluded from, the country’s Legislature. That is why I
supported payment of members.”
An Adelaide paper explains the difference
between Elijah the Tishbite and Elijah the Wing-
flapper. The first was fed by the ravens; the
second by the gulls.
The title “Monsignor,” becoming very common
in Australia, causes a little cash to go Romewards.
The Monsignor must pay £25 in fees for his
patent of honor.


The best Yuletide greeting to send to
friends in other lands will be the Christmas
number of The Bulletin, to be issued on
December 10th. In its pages will be found the
best presentation of Australian life and thought
ever issued, and no effort has been spared to make
it the best Christmas number up to date.
The death of John Lackey, ex-President
of the Upper House, leaves no visible gap in
N.S. \Y. polities. It is 20 years since the deceased
ancient was superannuated out of live politics—
when he gave up the portfolio of Lands in the
last Parkes Ministry but two. When a man
grows too antiquated to be re-elected to legislate
for three years lie is put into the Upper House to
legislate for life, so almost every man who
dropped out of N.S.W. Ministerial life since
1875, if he isn’t dead, can be found on that dusty
top-slielf that John Lackey occupied till he died.
It is a public place where the man who was once
mentally vigorous is deposited, so that his gradual
decay may be seen by all men, and where his
fading intellect is indecently exposed to the scoffs
of the ungodly.
The Commonwealth Patents Bill has now
become law. Beyond the fact that an inventor can now
get one patent covering the Commonwealth very little
improvement has been made to benefit the inventor. A
patent will still be simply a license to go to law with
every patent thief in Australasia. Take the case of the
Acetylene Gas people. It is acknowledged by every
authority that Wilson invented the commercial method
of manufacturing carbide. The Acetylene Gas Company
of Australasia Ltd. bought the patents, spent thousands
of pounds to develop the business, and as soon as the gas
was proved a success, they were surrounded by claimants
of all descriptions. The Company secured four injunc-
tions and is still up to the neck in law-suits against
infringers of its Carbide Patents and Generator Patents.
Why should a man who steals a patent when once the
steal is proved, he treated differently from a man who
steals a purse ? Yet the patent thief is considered a smart
man, whilst the purse lifter gets six months. A patent
should be treated in the same way as other property.*
