Still another attempt is to be made to put the
Pinnacles, the Barrier’s best-known mine outside Broken
Hill, on its legs. It is older than Broken Hill, and wa9
smelting before its rival, which soon eclipsed it, got to
work ; but, though it has big ore bodies, the stuff is so
refractory that it has broken three mainly-Sydney co.’s,
and is now in the hands of the Queensland Smelting
C 0.,• having passed into that control from a mortgagee.
F. J. Odling here tried the very first magnetic separation
plant erected on a mine in Australia ; he had some
success, but not enough to save his co., which was in a
bad way at the time. It is also handicapped by being six
or seven miles from rail, and it has never had the
funds to build a connecting tramway. It is not a
history which holds out much promise ; yet, presumably
on the strength of the improvements in metal-extraction
lately made, the present owners are again attacking the
mine vigorously, and intend sinking the main shaft
another 300 ft. Even, however, if the Broken Hill mines
can get out more of their metal, it does not follow that
the same appliances will succeed at the Pinnacles, which
has always had special metallurgical troubles of its own.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction’s surplus of liquid assets-
over liabilities on Sept. 30 was £5557. The mill remained
idle all the half-year ; in fact, this mine ceased to work
at a profit at the end of 1900, and has done little but
development and machinery-construction since then. In
the half-year ended March 31st., 1901, it made a loss
of £23,040, and silver was then 2s. 2Jd. and lead
£ls Is. lid. (Present prices are about 2s. 3d. and
£ll 55.) Still, it had made very big profits with metals
much lower than that. The fact is, the Junction is a
patchy mine, and it has not had the luck to strike
anything rich for a long while past. It has, however,
gone on opening large quantities of very fair grade ore—
a good deal richer ore, some of it, than some
Other mines are working at a profit ; but it has been so
badly opened up that cheap working is impossible This
defect has, however, been to some extent overcome during
the last year or two. The co.’s future depends upon the
improvement of the processes for extracting the metals
(so far less than one-third in value of the total metal
contents of the ore has reached the market) cr the
improvement in the price of lead. At present the mine
can’t be worked at a profit ; yet no very great improve-
ment would have to take place in either respect to carry
it over the line.
* * * *
Broken Hill is having one of its periodical
rows with S. Australia. Whenever S.A. gets more hard
up than usual, or when its Parliament won’t authorise
necessary taxation, the Government raises the rail freights
on the Barrier railway, which provides anything from a
third to over a half of the railway revenue of the State.
Then t he Barrier kicks, and talks vaguely about agitating
for other lines—sometimes a line via Wentworth and
Mildura to Melbourne, and sometimes a line via Wil-
cannia and Cobar to Sydney—but, as the Barrier hasn’t
any votes in the S.A. Parliament, and as S.A. has got
used to the threats, it goes on collecting the higher rates,
and summer comes, and the Barrier gets tired and leaves
off kicking. The present advance in rates, made in order to
compensate for the farthing which John Darling induced
the Assembly to knock off the land-tax, works out as
equal to a fall of 3s. Id. per ton in the price of lead. But
the most serious allegation is that the rise is a breach of
an understanding between the railways and the mines,
under which it was agreed that rates should not be put
up until certain metal prices were reached, and that it
was on this promise that certain treatment plants were
laid down. It is not a great deal to make a big row
about; yet several Broken Hill mines are having a hard
struggle, and the new move makes the situation a trifle
worse for them.
* * * *
“11.5. R.” writes from Emmaville (N.S.W.): —
“ On 28th Oct. there left the second batch of men from
here for Sierra-Leone, West Africa, commonly called
‘The White Man’s Grave.’ These were principally
young men, and they were rushed off before any news
came from the others who went about three months ago.”
This cheerful readiness to go anywhere, even to The
White Man’s Grave, in search of a job is a sorry commen-
tary on this country’s “magnificent natural resources”
that so much money is borrowed upon. The miner, almost
more than anybody else, depends on the “magnificent
natural resources.”
» * * *
Among the phenomenal crushings recently
announced in W.A. is that of the Lucky Call, Norseman,
which produced. 2830 z. from 4 tons. The tributors of the
Queen Margaret, Bulong, haven’t done badly as, although
the tribute has only been a month in existence, three
parcels totting up 50 tons have already been treated for
511 oz.
* * * *
Island Eureka, Lake Austin (W.A.), manage-
ment is, evidently, not very keen about deep mining, as
most of the development and stoping is at No. 1 level of
the Queen lease. Or is it that the gold doesn’t go down,
as has been proved in the adjoining Golconda? The latest
returns from the mine read 3920 z. from 410 tons. The
co. will in future have to do its own pumping, which will
entail an extra expenditure of £BO a month, as the
cross actions between it and the Golconda have both been
dismissed. The co. sued the Golconda for ceasing
pumping and flooding its mine, and the Golconda counter
claimed for half cost of pumping since 1897. The flooding
of the mine since the closing down of the Golconda is un-
deniable, so that the Golconda must have previously been
draining the workings. Hence the decision doesn’t seem
altogether logical.
?,* * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.) is being rapidly de-
veloped since Manager Ralph Nichols took charge. The
main shaft has been deepened and crosscutting at 1000 ft.
is now in operation. So far, only the Lake View lode has
been worked in the sulphide zone, but the present
crosscut will be carried right across the lease to test the
Perseverance lode at this depth. If properly wcrked,
there is no reason to doubt that the mine will eventually
come on the div. list. The treatment of the old residue's
by cyanide is'.now in full swing, and the first results will
shortly be published. As there are nearly 70,000 tons
awaiting treatment, there is fully a year’s work ahead of
the cyanide plant, and, before their treatment is com-
pleted, fresh reserves of ore should be opened up.
« * * *
Associated, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), evidently in-
tends to go in for a proper system of development,
as tenders have been let for carrying down Judd’s shaft
another 60ft., which will make it 1673 ft. So far levels
have been driven at every 100 ft., but in future, to
expedite matters, intervals of 200 ft. will be left. The
second half-dozen Merton furnaces will start next
month, and, early next year, the 20-head mill will start
on the lower: grade ore. Prospects are exceptionally
good at present.
* * *
Peak Hill (W.A.), has shown vast improve-
ment during the past 18 months. Prior to that time the
available ore was rooted out and the mine and co. got
into a bad way. The present manager (Leslie Hall), in
addition to wiping off some debt and paying one div. of
£14,000 in April, has now got ore reserves a long way
ahead of the mill. The mill is treating about 6000 tons of
lOdwt. ore monthly, and showing a fair margin of profit.
The last crushing shows 28210 z. of standard gold from
5770 tons. Asa large percentage of the ore comes from
open cuts the total mining costs are low, and the co. is
earning a net profit of over £4OOO a month. The accumu-
lated profits are more than enough to pay another Is. a
share, and a div. may be announced at any time.
» * * *
To give the Hainault (W.A.) a proper chance of
earning divs., the present mill should be extended, and
this will probably be done if the developments at 1000 ft.
justify the work. Manager Frank Moss has gone to
Europe to consult his directors as to working the mine on
a larger scale.
* » * *
New Victoria Consols, Coolgardie (W.A.), the
mine on which Morgans, M.L.A., placed a 20-head
mill, some years ago, on condition that he got a con-
trolling interest, turned out a frost under that control.
Recently tributors got to work, and their first clean-up
shows 2030 z. from 170 tons. This stone was broken from
the 140 ft. level, and the tributors estimate that they have
a fair supply of similar stone in sight. The number of
mines in W.A. which never produce any gold till the
working miner gets them on his own is marvellous. A
large number of the so-called wild cats round about Kal-
goorlie will yet turn up trumps, when the Bull co’s are
tired of wasting money and the Minister of Mines of
granting unlimited exemptions.
* • * *
Bay ley’s, Coolgardie (W.A.), after undergoing
its third reconstruction, has again got to work, but the
prospects are decidedly gloomy. Operations are being
condu"ted at the 6CO, 800, 900 and 1000 ft. levels, but
nothing payable has been encountered, the reefs consist-
ing entirely of white buck-quartz. The mine has already
been so thoroughly tested, without anything of value
being encountered in the lower levels, that shareholders
would be well advised to try and secure another and more
promising venture.
* * * *
Mount Yagahong (W.A.), which owns the Great
Fingall Main Reef, 60 miles north of the Consols, has
been i with an assessment of 3s. a share.
The directors have already splashed up £BO,OOO cash, not
to mention £130,000, proceeds of gold won, without any
return. The concern owns several regular producing
mines in various districts, hut, apparently, the manage-
ment is so poor that they can’t be made to pay. The
present assessment, if fully met, means another £60,000
to play with.
* * • *
There have been dark hints in Westralia lately
of a sensational find of rare minerals away out towards
the sunrise ; but the locality isn’t very accurately defined
yet. The Government Analyst has tested some concen-
trates sent down by the prospectors—tin mostly, but very
rich in platinum, and with a little gold. The sands that
were assayed were worth £IOOO a ton ; but, though a good
deal has been made of that fact, it doesn’t give the least
indication of what the crude stuff is worth. Still, the
proof of the presence of platinum is interesting, for pla-
tinum is one of the rare metals for which there is a
steady demand, and for which the market is quite open.
* * * *
South Australia will shortly see the end of a
rash experiment. With the idea cf giving the northern
copper shows a lift, smelting works were built at Port
Augusta during a copper boom ; but though there are
copper and silver-lead shows enough to keep half-a-
dozen furnaces going, they are scattered over about a
quarter of the dry continent, and most of them haven’t
any concentrating plants (and few of them have the
water, even if they had the mills). Also the copper
market slumped, and the Government found that the ore
which had been bought and paid for while the smelter
was being got ready, wasn’t worth anything like what it
had cost, and in despair, after sinking about £16,000, the
experiment was left to fizzle' out. A Melbourne firm has
picked up the whole fixings for a trifle for shipment
West.
* * * »
So far this year Queensland mining fields have
had a remarkably good time. The activity has by no
means been confined to the gold districts. Apart from
gold and coal, the first nine months’ output of last year
was worth £292,982 ; of this year £485,770, or £192,788'
more.. Tin shows up at the top ; it looks as though the
year’s yield will be worth a quarter of a million, which
will beat anything since 1882, and be about seven times
more than the figure for 1898. The copper output, too,
will be an easy record in the State’s history.
* * * *
There has been a steady increase lately in the
number of Westralian mining leases owned by private
individuals—a healthy sign, because it means in most
oases that the whole of the product stays in the country,
instead of much of it being sent to fatten foreign share-
holders. Of all W.A. leases, 538 have not passed into
companies’ hands : 526 have. Still, mere numbers don’t
count for much. There are 250 leases at Kalgoorlie, and
175 of them belong to co’s. and account for five times
more gold than all the others put together. It is in the
small-mine districts that the private leaseholder is com-
monest ; on the Murchison, for instance, outside the Day
Dawn district, only 75 leases are company-held, against
280 of the other sort. But absolutely the biggest propor-
tion is at way-out Phillips River, where, of 89 leases,
companies own but two.
* * * *
Fremantle (W.A.) is now being regularly
visited by British India steamers from Calcutta, which
arrive with Japanese coal, rice, sugar and other things,
picked up en route, and then get filled up with sawn
timber of the jarrah variety. The jarrah of Wes-
tralia is one of the finest woods in the world,
but at the present rate of denudation the magni-
ficent forests will soon be wiped out. Beyond the
wages earned by a mere handful of woodcutters and a
small addition to railway earnings, the State derives
absolutely no benefit from the timber trade. The Millar
Bros, and other boss timber concerns are hewing and
trucking in every direction, and a log of jarrah promises
to be as hard to find in another few years as a herd of
buffalo.
* * * *
S. A. wheat crop is now calculated at 14,000,000
bushels at least, being an average of from nine to ten
bushels per acre under cultivation. The better districts
are now fairly sure of their crop, but red rust in the
upper north and the far west may, or may not, bring the
total below 14,000,000, or (by holding off) allow, a
16,000,000 record to be established. S.A'. raised 14,649,230
bushels in 1884 ; 14,621,755 in 1885 ; 14,577,358 in 1890 ;
13,618,062 in 1894; and 11,253,148 in 1901. As far as is
known these are the only times 10,000,000 bushels have
been reaped during the last 20 years, but there were four
years when S.A. was tired and didn’t collect the figures.
* » * »
It is reckoned that the Victorian wheat harvest,
this year, may reach 26,500,000 bushels—last year it was
2,560,000. Bent’s railways collapsed miserably under the
strain of carrying last year’s crop. This year, with
probably ten times the amount to carry, heaven only
knows what they will do. The new manager has made,
preparations by re-christening the maintenance men the
“ track force,” or something like that.
* * « »
First barrel of gooseberries purchased from the
poor farmer by Queensland Minister for Agriculture was
found to be loaded. In the handling the barrel sprang a
leak, and about a bucket of creek water trickled on to
the officials. The poor farmer is always doing things
like that when the State tries to help him.
* * *
Suffolk Tribute Cos., which has come to the front
lately at B ndigo, has, under present arrangements, only
between eight and nine years to live ; the tribute runs
out in July, 1912, and reverts to a small private syndicate,
which took it from the owners, the Suffolk Cos. Even to
hold it that long the Tribute Cos. must, before the middle
of 1910, sink the main shaft 400 ft. (to 1610 ft.) The
tribute also involves payment of 10 per cent, of the gross
yield between 4dwt. and loz. per ton, and from 15 to 20
per cent, for anything over loz.
William M‘Millan, ex-member of the
House of Representatives, farewelled his old con-
stituency of Wentworth last Monday, and raised
his blushless importing countenance to heaven
while he talked of the blessings of that Freetrade
which he used to preach, while in practice he
sneaked in Protection. When M'Millan was
Freetrade Treasurer in Parkes’ violently Freetrade
Ministry he protected almost every N.S.W.
industry—except the ones that would compete
with his own importing business. The inferences
which a lot of people have drawn ever since have
been: (1) That M‘Millan knew that Protection
was good for the community though it was
bad for the importer; (2) that he practised Pro-
tection for that reason where it didn’t interfere
with his own importing business; (3) that where
his own importing business was concerned he
sacrificed the people’s interests to those of his
own importing business ; (4) that he was a
theoretical Freetrader because he was an im-
porter, and a practical Protectionist where it
didn’t interfere with his importing business,
becadse he was a human being. Anyhow,
M'Millan, the man who protected almost every
important N.S.W. industry except the ones which
would have interfered with his importing business,
has gone and left behind him no coherent explana-
tion of his remarkable conduct.
At Poverty Point.
Tyrone Power has been the subject of a law-suit in
New York. The Fiske and Frohmann managements
both wanted him, and the latter won. However, in
Stephen Phillips’ “Ulysses” he made something like a
failure, indifferent mounting being much to blame. The
irreverent American will not take things for granted as
the Londoner does, and a well-founded complaint that
the hinges of Hell squeaked went far towards killing the
play.
“J.A.0.”: “A Woman’s Letter” (5/11/”03) has the
following re Nance O’Neil’s appearance as Rosalind
“ For the first time in her stage career she has exhibited
her extensive flgurs in tights.” In this connection a
correspondent writes me : “In ’97 she was playing in
a stock company at the Murray Hill theatre, New York
city. I saw her play Billy Piper in the ‘ Danites,’ in
which she wore tight knee breeches, boots, and blue
flannel shirt. She was then a very slender girl, and
made an ideal frail youth. Imagine my surprise on
seeing her again in Sydney, barely three years later,
playing ‘Lady Macbeth,’ and fully three times the size
of poor Billy Piper.”
The “Golden Silence” of Haddon Chambers, at the
Garrick Theatre, London, has afforded the paragraphists
an opportunity of relating how he got his first play,
“ Captain Swift,” accepted. He saw Beerbohm Tree
going into a Turkish bath and followed him. There was
no escape for the actor-manager, and while “ cooling off ”
the playwright read his production, and the manager
accepted it. A naked manager is at a serious dis-
advantage.
“Fakir”: Ever noticed that the so-called tiger-tamer
(a tiger may be kept in a chronic state of dread, but he is
never really tamed) always carries two whips while doing
his turn in the cage ? In his right hand he holds a strong
cutting whip, and this, at times, he lays on to “Mr.
Stripes ” rather freely. The other is a short, straight
stick, and is never used except in a case of dire necessity.
Even then it is merely held in front of the tamer so that
the tiger can see it is there. In itself this whip affords
no more protection than a boiled carrot would. It is the
mystery surrounding it that makes it as formidable as a
red-hot crowbar. The tiger knows from much experience
the exact worth of the cutting whip, but, as the other
whip has never been used, he imagines it to be an extra
dreadful instrument.
The most ardent non-admirers of Wilson Barrett must
admit that the unconscious humorist has been a wonder-
fully virile battler. He produced a three-act play—
“,Twilight”—of his own manufacture 31 years ago, when
he was leading man in Miss Heath’s travelling co. And
he is still doing the same things with undiminished aggres-
siveness. For fully 40 years W.B. has insisted on taking
himself seriously as a “ star ’’-actor, manager and drama-
tist, without once condescending to attempt to be funny.
Marie Lloyd has been telling her story of how she came
to take to the stage. She was one of a crowd of amateur
girl performers who called themselves the Fairy Bell
Minstrels and gave a Band of Hope show (there isn’t much
Band of Hope about Marie nowadays) for the benefit of
some London mission, or other. From that time she
became a restless and discontented work-girl, and “got
the chuck ’’ from a succession of factory jobs. At last she
achieved an engagement to appear as a serio-chronic at
the Grecian Theatre, and her ballads were so beautifully
blue, she says, that she nearly got presented at court for
singing them. Hence her success in life.
“ H.P.” receives a letter from Alice Crawford, (the
clever Bendigonian, at present with Wilson Barrett) on
English weather mostly : —“This is an awful country to
live in. The climate is too dreadful for words. The
summer was just as trying as the winter, and now—rain,
rain, rain, and more rain, blowing rain, drizzling rain,
teeming rain, in all sorts of plain and fancy ways, and
then by way of a novelty, real wet weather. The sun,
formerly' a curse, is now a positive luxury. If by
chance I wake to see the sun out I fly into my clothes,
seize my hat and hurry out, fearful to miss it, for if you
loiter it goes in and stays in. I must not forget
to mention that to-day (Sept. 20th) has been fine. I dare-
say we shall look back to it for months and date things
from it, and say, ‘Well, you remember that Sunday the
sun was shining?’ I’ve already marked it down in my
diary in red ink. ... I weigh exactly 81b. less than
when I landed in England—am now a modest ten stone,
and by copious draughts of milk am seeking to cover my
bones with a fitting raiment of flesh. I am still on tour
with Wilson Barrett, playing principal comedy parts.
The salaries they pay in England would frighten you—
scarcely enough to live on. I see Joseph Came is
appearing this week in the Drury Lane drama, ‘The
Flood Tide.’ Thom. Kingston is on tour with Lewis
Waller in ‘ Monsieur Beaucaire.’ ”


Thirty Years 0 f Torture
MR. JAS. LANE, OF ROSE HILL, ORANGE, CURED OF CHRONIC SCIATICA.
AN OLD MAN’S STOUY.
No farmer is better known in the Canoblas
District than Mr. J ames B. Lane, who lives at
Rose Hill, three miles from Orange. As his
wife says, “ they are as well known as the Town
Clock in Sydney.’’ Mr. Lane has been a martyr
for years to Sciatica, which no doctor has been
able to cure. Off and on, for thirty years, he
has suffered, experiencing that fearful grind-
ing, agonising pain, which all Sciatic and
Rheumatic patients know only too well. Mrs.
Lane, in a recent visit to the Specialists of
The Freeman and Wallace Institute, told
them of his marvellous recovery, through
wearing their ONE POUND ELECTRIC
“INVIGORATOR” BELT, and adopting
their Remedies. Here is her story:—
“You say your husband has suffered for
thirty years ? ” asked the Specialist.
“Yes. Off and on,” replied Mrs. Lane,
“he has suffered fearfully. Last winter, in
1902, he was confined to his bed. His right
foot and leg were swollen greatly. He was
never free from the pain, and never expected
to get well.”
“ How long has he been under treatment ?”
“He wrote for one of your One Pound
Electric ‘ Invigorator ’ Belts about twelve
months ago, and took your medicines. He
improved from the start.”
“And what is his condition now!”
“He is so well that he can go without the
stick he used for years. He can now ride
round the paddocks for his cows; a thing he
could not do before. Anyone at Rose Hill
can prove this, and I think it all the more re-
markable because of his great age. Had he been
yohnger it would not have been so wonderful.”
“How old is he?”
“Seventy-three years old,” replied Mrs.
Lane, with pride, “but thanks to you and
Jour wonderful Belt, he is the picture of
ealth to-day. It is really a wonderful cure.
I am glad to be able to speak of it to you.”
“We are glad we have done him good.”
“Good is not the name for it. When I
think of how he suffered, and look at him now,
I can scarcely understand so great a change
for the better. I have brought the Belt to see
if it wants re-charging. Will there be any
price to pay ? We do not understand it ex-
actly, but we have followed your instructions
as to its wear correctly.”
“There will be no charge whatever. We
will hand it to our professional electrician,
who supervises these matters. It will be
ready in a day or two. Call again and see us.
We shall be glad to see you at any time and
hear you tell such a pleasing story.”
“I think everybody should know of this
wonderful recovery,” said Mrs. Lane, as she
left the Consulting- Room. “Persons suffering
from Sciatica might be cured as well as my
husband. I am sure he is a living advertise-
ment to your wonderful skill, and to the great
powers contained in your Electric ‘lnvigo-
rator’ Belt. Is he to go on wearing it round
the waist as usual ? ”
“Yes ! if necessary. But if he is free from
all pain he need not wear it again.”
“Very well, I’ll call for the Belt to-morrow,”
and with a smiling face, Mrs. Lane took her
departure.
This Is Mrs. Lane’s story. Any sufferer should
write to the Specialists for full particulars. This Is
only one case. The Specialists have thousands of
testimonies, and will mail a special pamphlet of
testimonies of cure free to any applicant.

Abortive threats of pulling noses have
Become so common in N.S.W. Assembly that it
is proposed to move that any member making the
threat and not executing it shall be suspended for
the rest of the session.
“ Yarro ” : In a recent speech the Bl’owe
Sullivan informed his hearers that he yearned to
go to the other side to assist Joseph of the
Monocle in the preferential trade crusade. Now,
why not assist the Bl’owe to realise his yearn?
Why not float a loan and ship him off ? A million
pounds worth of “ Bl’owe Sullivan Yearn Deben-
tures ” would probably be snapped up readily, and
the expenditure would be more productive than
any incurred for many years past.
To put an old councillor out of his seat is
recognised as an impossibility in most Vie. muni-
cipalities. The old dog knows all the ropes, and
pulls them at all hazards against his opponents.
An insidious form of bribery plays an important
part in his moves. He knows all the ratepayers
who are likely to overlook their rates by the 10th
of June, so, after winking at them, he pays the
amounts due, and they repay him at their leisure,
if at all, after harvest. Anyhow the amount is
debited in their accounts.

Governments’ advertisements of cheap land now
being circulated vigorously m Victorian country
districts are likely to affect the success of Irvine’s
Compulsory Resumption scheme more than the
local House of Squatters. Even Western District

George Prendergasfc discovered a nice little
steel trap for the working miners stowed away
in. a clause of the Vic. Mines Act .Amendment
Bill. The Bill provides that if an.ining company
has spent £lO an acre on its lease, it can claim a
suspension of the labor covenants for as long as
three years. Prendergast pointed out that this
provision could and would be used as a weapon
against the miners. If a company had a dispute
with its miners at any time after spending the
p resor ib e d sum it could obtain a suspension and
j 0 the men out with perfect safety. Many years
w j ien the Bendigo miners were fighting
a g a j ns t a decrease in wages and demanding an
hours day, the mine owners came to Francis
Longmore, then Minister of Mines, and asked to
a ]j owe d to suspend the labor covenants of their
j rase>s anc j look the men out. Longmore told them
tliafc if they j e f t Q ff work he would forfeit their
i easeg# The Irvine Government is not built that
way. Moreover, it has a wildly thick-headed idea
that the way to shove the mining industry along
is to allow mines to shut up for three years.
Victoria maintains in its asylums some 50
Chinese lunatics, one of whom has been an insane
Chow under Government supervision for 46 years.
Chief-Secretary Murray has now decided that
there is room for a sizeable economy here, and
proposes to ship the whole orowd back to China,
which can be done for £6OO or £7OO. The economy
is visible enough, though the heartlessness qf
turning loose an aged lunatic of 46 years derange-
men in a land that he has forgotten is pretty
glaring also. Under what law is it proposed to
do this sort of thing, and where or how has any
mere State Government power to start deporting
citizens in this off-hand fashion ? Isn’t it a Com-
monwealth matter ?
Melbourne Age, which has suddenly dis-
covered that, after all, its “still, strong, firm
man” is just the usual kind of political dodger,
draws attention to the disgraceful way in which
Irvine is allowing Parliamentary business to drag
along this session, and says that the Man of Ice
“ now finds it politically expedient to loosen his
grip on the reins,” in order to hold back most of
his programme for election purposes. Irvine has
this session practically abandoned the Assembly
to its own resources, letting whole weeks go to
waste without putting in a word for greater
despatch, and he hasn’t yet tabled the preliminary
motion for the introduction of his Compulsory
Land Purchase Bill and other important measures.
Even the weak Peacock got his work through
more rapidly than the frozen apparition that now
dreams on the front bench.
That Maori myth, the Kumi, alleged to
have been the slowest thing ever created (the worm
gave him 75 yards in 100 and he was left at the post)
is only a circumstance compared with the somno-
lent Privy Council. In the Wariongaromia native
land case, plaintiffs secured judgment against the
M L. Assets Board, but latter obtained a stay of
proceedings for nine months pending appeal to the
P,C. English lawyers, however, were aghast at
the idea of getting the case heard in that time —it
was quite out of the question. So the M.L. court
allowed the matter to stand over indefinitely, and
feels that it is looked down upon in England as an
undignified body that is in a lather of perspiration
through excessive haste.
Chamberlain pushed home some un-
answerable arguments for Protection in his
address to the Liverpool workers: —
Cobden declared that trades unions were based on
brutal tyranny and monopoly. From his standpoint of
right it was impossible to reconcile Freetrade and
unionism.
The Factory, Mines, and Truck Acts, compensation for
workmen, fair wages, and the clause relating to the pro-
hibition of prison-made goods, are all opposed to the
doctrine of Freetrade, which is against Interference with
individual freedom.
What is the use of prohibiting sweating in England, if
you allow sweated goods to enter the country?
Unless manufacturers are given a compensating ad-
vantage, they will not be able to compete and you are
workless.
In fact, it seems as if Joseph had been carefully
studying The Bulletin.

CASINO, N.S.W.
Thursday, 26th November.
The Celebrated Kyogle Estate,
Richmond River, N.S.W.
One of the Finest Estates in Australia.
RAINFALL, averages over ro'n. per annum.
Magnificent Lucerne Country.
The Finest Maize-Growing Flats.
Splendid River and Creek Frontages.
RAILWAY now open to Casino.
Metalled Road from thence to the Property, 13 Miles.
TERHS OF PAYMENT UP TO TEN YEARS.
HAMILTON G-ROOM & CO., of 116 Queen Street, Melbourne, and J. B. McDOUGALL, of
Casino, have received instructions from the owners to sell by public auction at the
SCHOOL OF ARTS, CASINO, on THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 26th, at one o’clock,
The First Sub-Division of the Kyogle Estate containing 20,000 Acres,
sub-divided into Farms from 50 to 400 acres.
The Kyogle Estate contains 41,000 acres, of which about 12,000 acres are the finest river flats in Australia—deep
black and dark chocolate loam. The balance is rich dark chocolate ridges, free from grit—the true chocolate soil so
highly prized by farmers, soft, mellow, and greasy to the touch, retentive of moisture, and overlying a basaltic
formation.
The first sub-division is now being offered to the public, containing
20,000 ACRES,
of which 7000 to 8000 are river flats—the balance rich dark chocolate ridges, all lightly timbered, and open grass
country—no heavy timber to clear —no scrub to be cut down and burned off.
Dairying can be commenced on this land at once with no further outlay than for fencing and necessary buildings,
and the carrying capacity can be gradually trebled by working in paspalum and other grasses.
For many years the Richmond River districts were practically unknown, and the dairying industry there is
comparatively of only recent growth, but has taken such gigantic strides that the output of butter, bacon, etc., now
exceeds that of any similar district in New South Wales, and there is a population now settled there of nearly 50,000
persons, which is rapidly increasing. In no part of Australia is there a district better suited for carrying an
enormous population on small holdings—the excellence of the rainfall, mild climate, and fertility of the soil all com-
bine to make this the Garden of Australia. The Government of New South Wales has recently surveyed a line of
Railway from Casino through the Kyogle Estate, and with the sub-division of the property there is every probability
that the line will be constructed at no great distant date. A Township has recently been formed at Kyogle, 18 miles
from Casino, to which there is a metal road ; and there are already several stores, an Hotel, a blacksmith’s shop, two
churches, Creamery, a saw-mill in full swing, a State School close by, a Post and Telegraph Office, and more buildings
in course of erection, while the Bank of New South Wales has recently opened a branch there.
Never before has such an opportunity occurred to secure some of the finest land in Australia at such low figures.
Owing to the demand that has existed for dairying and farming lands in favored districts properties have been
forced up to extreme values, and the purchasing power of the would-be settler much restricted. He has now the
opportunity of securing land at least equal, if not superior, to that in any other part of Australia at one-fourth the
price paid in some districts ; and he will have the advantage of good roads, excellent market facilities, and a climate
that will enable him to dairy successfully all the year round, and where he will have the satisfaction of seeing his pro-
perty steadily improve in value for many years to come.
Most liberal terms of payment will be given—viz., one-fourth cash, one-fourth in two years, balance up to ten
years at 5 per cent., or all cash at any time if desired.
TITLES FREEHOLD UNDER TORRENS ACT.
SPECIAL NOTICE.
Exceptionally Low Reserves will be fixed for this First Subdivisional Sale, as the owners
intend to offer every inducement for settlement on the Estate.

“ Castle ” writes from Maoriland :
Let me gave vegetarianism a reference. I tried it six
months ago, “since when I have used no other.” Results,
morally simply splendid. Don’t wish to look at a pretty
girl now ; don’t even revel in a glimpse at a natty ankle,
blow the wind never so strongly, As to kissing a girl, I
believe I would faint at the idea. Smoking appetite is
rapidly diminishing—one cannot enjoy a pipe after a dish
of onions or a plate of tapioca. Can’t drink beer with
my meals, and whisky goes to my head. Balance at bank
getting larger. Am not a bit charitable ; when I see a
poor man I wonder he doesn’t live on turnips (six a day
will support life) and save his money. Just as strong and
well as ever; awfully mean ; possessor of a sickly
morality ; don’t laugh much ; and cut by all my chums.
Shall give it up and have a chop.
An old Maori, suffering from financial
depression, quietly started a new industry a
little while back. He travelled through the King
country and issued liquor licenses to natives,
allegedly on behalf of the Maori Council or some
such body. The “ licenses ” were nicely printed
in Maori, and only cost 50s. There was a great
rush to secure the bits of blue paper. When the
law took a hand later on, and charged many
brown citizens with sly grog-selling, the brown
accused presented their bits of paper with great
confidence, and the astonished court adjourned to
wipe its spectacles and see where it were.

PLAIN FACTS.
The Liver is the mainspring of the system.
When active it is the great purifying organ.
When sluggish it easily develops a poisonous ten-
dency and contaminates the other organs.
The Liver both secretes and excretes.
It secretes about three pounds of bile daily.
It excretes the impurities and waste materials.
The Liver is the sewage farm of the body, and since
it is the sieve it must perforce be kept clean,
otherwise it becomes sluggish and cannot expel
its refuse, which, consequently, is absorbed back
into the blood, and is tne cause of biliousness
and other distressing maladies.

The Song of the Joy of Life.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Raking the rack of a purblind past,
Burdened with world-old woes,
Joyance atrip and long of lip,
Droning the Weeper goes;
Ranging the gamut of age-worn griefs—
Heart-wrenchings, silence, tears—
And the sorrow wrung from the slow, sad tongue
Of the listless years.
Sorrow of years ! Nay, but joy of days
Clear-hearted, swift and chaste ;
Revel of feet through the plain-grass sweet—
Lip-free and open-faced—
Challenge of eyes in the opal dawn,
Wildness of wind blown hair,
Rushing divine of the hot heart-wine
At the kiss of the air.
When dawn strikes white down the rolling slope,
Where the weaving current calls—
Wavers and creeps thro’ the fern-screened deeps
And the woof of waterfalls—
Ye have seen soft eyes, and the hare afoot
Where shrouded night had been,
And the flash of wings where the torrent springs
To the wet ravine.
Ye have lifted your heads to cast your jest
In the throat of the shouting sea ;
When the years have failed, ye have fought and
sailed
Through the glamor of days that be ;
Ye have watched the runes of the rocking mast
Vague-writ on the cobalt dark,
And the petulant drift of the waves alift
’Neath the pearling-barque.
The wide-armed joy of a white-ridged world
That runs to the blatant breeze,
The shriek behind in the whooping wind,
The shock of the stamping seas,
The flicker and fall of the flying drift
That leaps at the low-hung star,
Round-eyed, ashine, where the breakers whine
On the harbor bar.
Sorrow of years, or joy of days—
Here have ye chosen true.
Honey of life or husk of life —
These were the choice ye knew :
Haloed ye lie at the world’s warm breast,
For ye have felt astir
The slow breath sweet, and the soft, dull beat
Of the heart of her.
* * * *
Out from the west, where the dear, grey road
Reddens a league away,'
Over the range lies near and strange
The finger of drowsy dq,y ;
Glory of sun and of star-shot night
Kiss on the crowning hill;
Rushing divine of the hot heart-wine,
You are sweet and still.
VIC.
ORESTES.
A Stroll to the Strand.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To get a good picture of London, in some of its
aspects, walk down Liverpool Road, from High-
bury Orescent to the Strand, as I did, some
months ago.
In Liverpool Road notice first an old church
with a blind clock tower, and a big yard where
the children play. Nearly all the old church-
yards in London, in main roads, and in odd,
almost forgotten corners, have been, or are being
turned into playgrounds for children, with grass
plots, and with seats for mothers and nurses and
old folk. The head-stones have been placed
round, side by side, flat against the boundary
walls, as close as they can stand, with the foot-
stones flat against them ; and the children play
and romp over the mould of the dead.
Speaking of churches and churchmen, athletic,
well-developed, young men in boating, rowing,
cricketing, football, boxing or biking dress, are
taking the places of pale young curates, and
weeds with side whiskers, shawls and ginghams,
in England ; few English provincial audiences
would appreciate or understand the “Private
Secretary ” nowadays.
Lower down Liverpool Road I notice a little,
one-horse eating-house with a sign which says
that it is the best stopping place for waggons.
There’s a horse-trough, and a farm-waggon, and a
van and a cart in front; the horses with their
heads in feed-bags. Probably there’3 a trusted
farm-hand inside, in corduroys and wonderfully
made coat —of rough dark stuff, square cut in
front and double-breasted, wide over the hips,
and seamed and slit behind. Earm-waggons and
carriers’ vans still come into the heart of
“Lunnon” —to Covent Garden mostly—in their
hundreds, from all round, from the grain farms in
Herts and Bedfordshire, from vegetable and fruit
gardens up the Thames valley towards Windsor,
and from hop-fields down in Surrey and Kent.
Shaky, home-repaired and painted, one-horse
vans carry household goods, and chattels, and
parcels from the heart of roaring London away
out into quiet, sleepy, little, old fashioned villages,
hidden in corners of hedge-rows, 30, 40, 50 or 00
miles out. The farm-waggons start for London
at three, four, or five o’clock in the morning,
according to the distance from town ; halt at bne
of the “stoppin’” places on the main roads, on
the outskirts of London, to bait their horses and
have’ breakfast, and again for tea, reaching home
in the evening. And, along towards midnight,
the wife in the farm village makes up the fire,
arranges the bread, butter and. cheese on the
table, takes a sip, perhaps, from the can or jug of
four-ale, throws something over her head, and
goes out to the little gate to peer up the long,
white road—ghostly between the dark blackberry
hedges on summer nights—or else strains her ears
for the “clock-clock-clock” and rattle, and her
eyes for the glow in the opaque fog that advertises
the coming of her “chap.”
A babel of voices, a glare of oil torches by
night, costers’ barrows hurrying across, a litter
of rubbish, and much mud, slush and smell,
advertise Chapel-street. Chapel-street is one of
those many little by-streets about London which
might have been cut off old Petticoat-lane, and
toned down according to the degrees of respecta-
bility of the localities they eater for—one of those
little streets you can buy anything in, from a
double bedstead to a pound of cats’-meat for
breakfast. Imagine Paddy’s Market, Sydney, in
its best, or worst days —in the bygone days of its
glory. Imagine the whole of Paddy’s Market
crowded into one little street, with costers’ barrows
in the place of vans and stalls, crowded little
shops of all sorts on each side, and a pub. at each
•corner. That is Chapel-street.
At the first barrow, a stout, healthy Jewess and
her assistant —a thin, draggle-tailed, unhealthy-
looking young woman, with sore eyes and a
swollen face in a dirty handkerchief—sell little
bunches of watercress, parsley and sage. Next, a
young half-Jew, with a hatchet face—and that
spitefully inquisitive pinching-in at the base of
the nostrils that reminds one of a mongrel about
to snarl—before a barrow of oranges, is rubbing his
hands with great unction, and fairly writhing at the
remarks of an elderly Jew, with a very shiny tall
hat and a frockcoat. This person bears all the
marks of a retired pawnbroker, and has evidently
condescended to take a stroll through Chapel-street
(where he may have started life with a barrow, in
other days) and air his affluence and exercise his
wit on degenerate sons of his tribe. He may have
capital invested in those barrows, or he may be
saving some from starvation.
A coster, near by, has scraps of pork on the
tray of his barrow, and little heaps of alleged
chops—some of them manufactured from odds and
ends of meat, and marked 3d., 4d., (id. and 7d. A
seedy, shabby-genteel little elderly weed, with a
lost and apo'ogetio air, who might be a messenger
or under-clerk out of employment, whose wife
keeps a lodging-house, and who has been swept
out that morning in the spring cleaning, drifts up
in an eddy in front of the pork barrow, and blinks
abstractedly and helplessly at the meat, as if it
reminds him of something he forgot long ago.
The coster suddenly opens fire on him.
Well, sir ! If yer not satisfied, go somewhere
else—go somewhere else. If yer can get better
quality for less money, go by all means. Buy or
leave it! —buy or leave it. No ! We don’t sell
no Horsetralian meat here. Only good English
meat here. If yer wants frozen meat, yer must
go up the street —yer must go up the street, or to
Botany Bay,” &c., &c.
The poor landlady’s husband slowly collects
some of his scattered wits, and shuffles off as fast
as he can, with a confused idea that he must have
offended the coster in some way, or that he had
no right to be standing there looking at the meat
when he hadn’t been sent out to buy any, and
hadn’t the slightest notion of doing so.
Further along, an old woman sells fragments of
linoleum, odds and ends of oilcloth, and scraps of
threadbare carpet. She has a small iron bedstead,
a rusty bed-chair, and some stained mattrasses
and bolsters ; also some chenille and other cur-
tains, all well smoked. She probably owns a rag
shop somewhere. Next to that a woman sells old
clothes, and has a line in female underwear which
is exposed for sale, and advertised verbally and
bargained for with no nonsensical delicacy.
There is seemingly a total absence of a sense of
humor, or of the ridiculous, in low-class London.
Down between Holborn Town Hall and High
Holborn are two mirrors, on each side of a shop
door. One morning I saw a stout, full-faced
woman straightening her hat and fixing the pins
at one of those mirrors, while a thin youth had
laid his gums bare and was critically examining
his big yellow teeth at another. They were gro-
tesque mirrors; the one at which the woman
stood was convex, and the other concave. And
not the ghost of a smile on either of their faces !
At the corner pub. _ I noticed the difference
between the beer-drinking and the wine-drinking
workman. Take two scenes :
(1) Wine-cellar in Italy : Italian posing—not
the arrogant, insolent, you-be-knifed swagger of
the Spaniard, but the we-are-happy, we-are-
brothers, I-love-the-world pose of a son of Italy
with wine in him, head well back, foot forward,
wineglass held. Curve of arm and wrist artisti-
cally perfect (in a country where they can’t hang
a rag from a balcony without doing it artistically).
Eyes shining and dancing: “ ’Elio, Pietro there !
How is it with Pietro ! Good old Pietro ! Here’s
to Pietro ! ” _ Brings glass round down to lips with
a sweep, drinks, and sets glass on top of cask.
“ Here, my dear ! More wine ! Fill it up ! Now
Pietro ! ” etc.
(2) English beer-house: awkward, heavy, un-
lovely Bill lurching over Tom, spilling his muddy
ale, and speaking like a bull in the next paddock :
“ Here ! Wake up yer ! Wake up ! ” (stir-
ring Tom with his foot). “ Here lave another
beer. Wot? Wot the yer talking about?
Who’s going home ? ’ome! the missus !
’Ave a beer ! Wot, yer won’t ? Then yer! ”
Lurching over to bar, “ ’Ere, gimme a pint o’
four-ale. ”
I make a fresh start for Chancery-lane. I turn
into Upper-street, and a little further on is “The
Angel.” The name of that tavern has been heard
in all quarters of the globe. I have written it
down in the Bush for a woman going to Engl md :
“Take a ’bus to the Angel.” In itself it is
nothing ; just a shabby, dingy little corner pub.,
no better and no worse than hundreds of its kind
in London, with nothing special in the way of
liquor, and nobody in its public bar this morning
save a hopeless old boozer of the type that haunts
every public*house, and a very tired and sleepy-
looking barman. But the Angel is a land-mark, a
finger-post, a sign-post, and the end of many
penny sections: and it saves years of time in
giving directions.
I take a ’bus, and as it swings out it is blocked
by a crush of traffic—but mostly by a coster’s
donkey-cart.
“ Now then ! ” says our driver, sharply, to the
coster. “Wake up! You ain’t in bed. Give the
other hass ’is ’ead—it’s better’n yours.”
The coster looks black and gasps. He opens his
mouth, but the cartridge jams. The reputation
of costerdom is stake. But just as the other
ass takes his head the coster reorganises.
“ Hi! Hi !” he shouts, suddenly, to our driver,
who turns his head sharply. “Hi!” shouts the
coster, “’Ow’syour wife an’my children?” and
he gives “the other ass” more head and hurries
off in surly triumph.
“ He might’a’given me a chance to get back
on ’im,” says the driver, ruefully, seeing me
glance at him, and taking a hand from his reins
to rub his ear. “ They’re too smart, them costers;
that’s what they is. You see,” he adds, reflec-
tively, “it’s a long while since I was a coster
meself. ”
But here’s Chancery-lane—you might walk past
and miss it several times if you didn’t look for the
name. For Chancery, like other lanes in London,
is short and narrow.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

Burning-Off,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kurnow’s Swamp called him Old Gridley from
the day it first saw him, for his hair was even
then iron-grey, and he had the slow reticence of
speech, the heavy movement of the English farm-
laborer. His wife, sitting by his side in the dray
with one baby in her arms, and another lying at
her feet, looked a middle-aged, subdued woman;
for theirs had been one of those wonderful, almost
interminable English courtships, of which Aus-
tralia has no knowledge nor conception.
Gridley put up a bark humpy on his selection,
and forthwith “ burnt-off.” He has been burning-
off, at intervals, ever since—on wild winter nights,
in calm spring evenings, in scorching summer
afternoons when the stretching acres of brown
dust leave no danger to be feared.
Now, from the slab-house to the road, stretches
a miniature park ; crop paddocks, cow pasture,
and orchard, are neatly fenced off, only, down by
the big dam, remains some half-acre of ring-barked
timber. Every evening after supper Gridley
leaves his wife reading the paper dropped by
the mailman in the roadside box, and plods out
towards the uncleared spot. Yet the burning-off
progresses but slowly ; one would almost fancy he
is in no hurry to complete the task.
To-night he piles up the brushwood round a
tall, slender sapling. The blaze runs round the
circle at the base, glides up the trunk in clear
orange flame, and moves out among the spreading
branches in fiery forks. From the place where
Gridley stands in shadow, out of the current of
the smoke-drifts, it looks like the huge candela-
brum of some foreign cathedral. The glare throws
up the spectral greenish pallor of the dead trees
against the sky of peacock-blue, and nearer, from
a blazing stump, burst forth showers of gleaming
amethysts, emeralds, sapphires and topaz, sub-
siding swiftly in a transfigured mass of vivid rose.
To old Gridley the roar of the flame, the keen-
ness of the night air, the loneliness, the absence
of human suggestion, his nearness by reason of
his age to the Unknown, act as a draught of a
magic philtre. On the dark log to the left of the
fire sit dream-children—five of them—their legs
dangling from its edge—Charlie the sober, Jack
the rogue, Mary the motherly, with little Jim the
cripple resting on her arm, and tiny Jessica,
father’s beauty, with hair like yellow floss-silk
and eyes like black diamonds. The dream lips
utter no sound, yet the children clap their hands
noiselessly when the fire spouts more fiercely from
the candelabrum, and their eyes grow soft and
sleepy as one by one the flames die down.
These are the dream-children. The real ones—
what of them ?
Charlie, the shrewd, is an auctioneer in a distant
town. On one of his yearly visits he talked
wisely to his father as to the advisability of
making a will. Old Gridley turned the subject,
yet the suggestion rankled, and in his heart the
old man loves the dream child best.
Mary, the motherly, became a school teacher,
and married a railway man (he is traffic inspector
now). She sends a letter and a present every
Christmas, and her children (as their grand-
mother explains with much unction) are quite
“little ladies and gentlemen.” Old Gridley
went to visit her once in a stone house on the
northern line. He wore his best clothes un-
comfortably, and was afraid to smoke, even in
the kitchen.
As for Jessica, he never goes to Sydney now,
lest her painted face should peer at him in the
lighted street.
And Jack, the careless—there was no better
bushman than he, when he chose ; as long as he
stayed at home, the light-hearted whistle, the
homely jest, the ready pound, and the smile on
the frail old mother’s face, were never wanting
But Jack took the war fever, and died from
enteric soon after landing, and was buried on the
open veldt. He was not even a hero, though
God knows if that would have comforted his
mother much !
The fires are low now. Old Gridley banks
them up carefully. He no longer sees the faces
of his dream children, for heavy shadows fall on
the old log. There is a light moving to and fro
in the distant house. He remembers now, and
memory is more bitter than fancy, how they
trooped homeward, behind and in front of him,
with sleepy chatter, and how softly, as he carried
him, lame Jim’s face nestled against his own.
Ah me ! little Jim’s leg left off aching long ago.
And he is the only child left them, after all.
JOHN CAREW.
That maniacal solicitor in England, Edalji,
who ripped up scores of mares with a razor, and
was threatening to “ treat 20 wenches in the same
way ” when arrested, was a Eurasian—father
Parsee, mother English. He is described as a man
with practically no chin, and who walked on his
heels. Nature exacts some gruesome revenges
when man disobeys her ordinances, and mixes
the white blood witli that of Bruce Smith’s friend,
the nigger.
It would appear, from an English paper of
some little time back, that “ ragging” prevails in
English universities and other scholastic institu-
tions as well as in the British army. A promising
young fellow had to be escorted by a lunatic-
asylum attendant to his home in the country,
from one of the universities, having become insane
in consequence of a series of outrages by other
students. He is at present an inmate of a mad-
house, and the most pathetic part of it is that he
is the only son of a widowed mother. Another
youth, at a training college for teachers, for the
heinous crime of “cheeking a monitor,” was
seized by 35 cowardly ruffians, kicked black-and-
blue, half-drowned, and otherwise abused. An
appeal to the Principal met with callous indiffer-
ence, the college doctor refused to examine him,
and a magistrate applied to said that “as he
could not tell which one did it, the issue of 35
summonses would be a ‘tall order’ not to be
thought of!” Beautiful nurseries for “gentle-
men ”!
“ G.H.” : I am watching a man in a long,
swallow-tailed coat, laboriously chopping wood.
(There is only one more pathetic sight—it is that
of a man in a belltopper hanging out the wash-
ing.) Under ordinary circumstances the slavey
chops the swallow-tailed man’s firewood; but I
am informed by our Mary Ann that their Mary
Ann has left, and that Mrs. Swallow-tail “can’t
get hanother girl hanyw’ere hat hall.” I notice
the lady, in the distance, gathering up the bits of
wood as her husband mournfully cuts them. She
wears black silk and a broken expression. There!
I knew it would happen. A piece of springy tim-
ber has struck Mr. Swallow-tail in the commis-
sariat department; he is a church pillar, and
arranges his swears with an utter disregard of
syntax. Mrs. Swallow-tail still wears black silk,
but has changed her expression for a.confection of
mixed solicitude and horror. Mr. Swallow-tail
throws the axe away and holds his stomach. I
think I’ll cut some wood now.

ing wherein he had offended, while the crowd roared
at the fun of the thing. “GenTmen, there’s been a
humor round town that this ’ere fight isn’t fair dinkum.
Well someone is always puttin’ them there humors about
when a big fight is cornin’ off at my place. I say it’s a
, and lie, etc., etc. GenTmen, this ’ere chap is the
champion of the Eastern Subwebs,” etc., etc.
* * * *
Tipped by forefront English player and
authority, O. B. Fry, that Worcestershire man Arnold
will do best work with the ball for British eleven now in
Australia. There’s a deal of change and trick in Arnold’s
operations, ’tis said. Last year, against visiting Aus-
tralians, he bagged 18 wickets at an average of 16.49,
doing best in South of England’s interests by scattering
8 for 57 and 4 for 30.
• • • •
“Marat”: Be “Scrip” (8., 22/10/’O3) on in-
ability of sporting writers to pick winners. I know one
sporting tipster who backed the winner of the big handi-
cap three Saturdays in succession, but he didn’t tip it.
He was afraid the market might be rushed before he got
his own dollar on, and his own dollar was of more account
to him than the public’s dollar.
• » ®
“Old Timer”: Anent demonstration against
Rupert Clarke after F.J.A.’s Derby, t’other day. Success
of longest priced of two or three from same stable and
all legitimately backed is not rare. It has occurred with
owners in a small way as well as with wealthy ones. It
happened to the late James White (among the straightest
the Australian turf has known) more than once. Uralla
and Nordenfeldt (A.J.C. Derby, 1885) and Singapore and
Rudolf (1889) are cases in point.
* • * «
Question of method of selecting next Bisley
team is worrying Australian rifledom just now. Com-
monwealth Rifle Association agreed that scores made in
Commonwealth matches should form the basis, but
restricting the field for selection to 10 men from each
State brought a forcible kick because of its absurdity ;
that particular event is held in a different State every
year and only men with time and money at their disposal
can go from one place to the other, consequently, it is
doubtful whether the visitors would represent the
Strength of their respective sections. Again, it isn’t the
least bit likely that all who do best in Commonwealth
matches could go ’Ome—three out of four N.S.W. men
who qualified at recent Randwick meeting for places in
the team state the trip is impossible to them.
* * * *
“ J. A.C.” : The welsher appears to flourish here
at pony meetings, and he has just been giving the trust-
ing public a turn over the Cup. They are more drastic
in their dealings with him in Scotland. Some five or six
weeks ago, on Ayr racecourse, the aggrieved punters
broke one of the fraternity’s legs, and left him to be
carted off to the infirmary.
London Local Government Journal on the
cult of sport: “ The education of the masses to-day has
not reached a pitch beyond a knowledge of the odds on
a race, the ‘form’ of a footballer, or the centuries of a
cricketer. Sport of all kinds is being abused. We seem
incapable as a people of steering a middle course in any-
thing. A fashion becomes a craze ; a craze becomes a
mania. We have elevated athletics as the Golden Calf
was elevated by the Israelites in the wilderness ; we
assemble in our thousands to see athletics—not to become
athletic ourselves. The crying need of to-day is not
public libraries, but public gymnasia.”
